Philotimus.

THE WAR betwixt Nature and Fortune.

Compiled by BRIAN MELBANCKE Student in Graies Inn.

Palladi.

Fortuna virtutis famula.

Imprinted at London by Roger Ward, dwelling near unto Holborne Conduit at
the Sign of the Talbot. 1583.

To the right honorable, Phillip, Earl of Arundell, Brian Melbancke wishes
increase of honor, with the full fruition of perfect felicity.
THE baseness of this present (my very good Lord) compared with the
worthiness of your honor, may argue me impudent in exhibiting such a gift,
and confirm your vvoonted clemency if you deigne to accept it.

The firstlinges of our fruits are due to the Gods, and men are discharged if
they offer them devoutly: the prime of my labors I give to your honor, and I
think my self acquitted though they be not very exquisite. A Caesar's mind
will vouchsafe a Codrus might, a courteous Laelius will give attention to a
stammering Lentulus, and Jupiter in that did show himself Jupiter, that he
refused not the supplication of poor Liberia.

Meet it were, feign I would, and I hope I shall gratify your Lordship,
edify the Reader, and damnifie none by this simple Treatise. If your Lordships
Triple be not to high for my Base, I doubt not but my music will be
indifferent, and if your honor disdain not my seely Pamphlet, I hope it
will be reasonably vvelcome to others.

Had PHILOTIMVS been served in at the first course, when your stomach was not
quatted with other daintier fare, his relishe perhaps had been something
toothsome: But since you are cloied with more delicate cates, thus fair
notvvithstanding I dare presume, to bring him in for a shovv among other
banqueting dishes. And as Olautia rejoiced, that Thalestris the Queen of the
Amazons received a hosegay at her hands, though she never smelled to it: or as
Cherilus the rural Poet triumphed, that the great Macedo had seen his verses,
though he never liked them: So if now and than you do but look on
PHILOTIMVS, when other vvaightier affairs do not otherwise employ you, I shall
think my self no small deal happy, that your honorable vievve has
ouershadovved my book.

The morning is meet for grauer studies, the postmeridian for pleasant
solace, so that, PHILOTIMVS which comes in limping at the latter end of the
day, though be be no author for knowledge of importance, yet may some
refection by reading his toies, lend recreation to your vvearied Muses.

Iuball first made the portatiue tents, ordered his flocks, and appointed
when rams should go to the eyves: Tuball devised the science of music,
Tubalcan searched out the commodities of metals, Parmenides invented the art
of Logic, Saturn first taught the conceit of shooting, and Noema found out
the mystery of vveauing: Yet neither the kind of Deiotorus his grasing, not
the commodity of Lagus his tent making, nor the commendation of Empedocles his
melody, nor the fame of Vulcans smith works, nor the renoune of Arist.
Logic, nor the praise of Lancus for weauing of his silks, nor the steady
hand of Comodus that never missed mark, are any whit impaired by their
predecessors praise: no more I trust shall my poor Pamphlet be defaced by
the eloquence of them that have forerun me, (though the comparison be far
unequal.)

Galen was a cunning physician, Saluius Iulianus an expert lawyer, Fon^o a
disert orator, Oppian a famous poet, and Maximinus of so strong constitution,
that he was able to support any pains: were the properties of all these
coniested in me, which am invested in none of them all, then vvoulde I try,
whether my service were able to stand your Lordship in some stead, to whom
my serviceable duty shall be ever obeisant. But alas (my Lord) a barley
grain can but yield an ear of barley, and two pence is large usury of a
poor penny: a mightles marling can afoord but a willing mind, and a
halfepeni-worth of skill, a two-peny Pamphlet.

If plain utterance be no blemish to a very truth, and truth do not breed
suspicion of flattery, I protest to your honor, that as you have won the
hearts of all them, who have heard of your demeanor: So I amongst the rest
(though one of the most that can do you least service) have in faith and
fancy, love and loyalty, done unto you such hearty homage, as is more possible
for you to conceive, then credible for me to declare to your self. And this I
say, that whiles you Mecenas are, a Maro shall you never want: VVhiles you
endeavor to be an Achilles, an Homers deuoire shall never quail to blaze your
praise: a virtuous Cyrus will cause Zenophon to be eloquent, and Alexanders
valute will make Curtius a good Antiquatie.

But least you think that Plato is turned to Gnato, and my philosophy
changed to flattery, I will rather refer my self to time and trial, then
vainly vaunt of loyalty, where there wants (ah) ability.

This therefore (right honorable) is my humble request, that in this war
betwixt NATVRE and FORTUNE, you will vouchsafe to be the vmpeere, whose fortune
is prosperous (long may it last) but far inferior to your nature, whom all men
admire. And if at any time hereafter, you see virtue suppressed by casual
calamity, you will lend your assistance to the rescuing of Virtue, knowing,
that Caesar was more commended for his courtesy then his cheualrie, and that
the only Virtue which made Theodosius the Emperor, surnamed the younger, so
famous, was bounty to the best and affability to all. Haud facile emergunt
(says the Poet) quorum virtutibus obstat res augusta domi, and oftentimes
shall you see inestimable virtues endarkened with the vale of a russet coat, as
the mithridate of Medicines (triacle I mean) is extracted out of the bowels of
most poisoning woormes.

I speak not this for mine own lucres sake. For as Isocrates says in his
pareneticall oration to Nicocles, I am not so baseminded to merchandise books,
although my want may stand in need to your Lordship, and urge me to crave your
favor, when my merits deserve no meed.

I am not ignorant that there be some, who will be as free of a good word to
Philotimus, as a poor man is of his harvest: who, for that they hate me
unadvisedly, will indiscreetly beat my dog: and because they envy Melbancke
with a peasantly despite, will dispraise all his doings, though they be for
their profit.

Plain dealing is a ^ewel, (though they that use it commonly die beggars) and
therefore will I speak plainly without a parable, though my simplicity may
impeach me of folly. If they like it, let them use it, and if they loath it,
let them refuse it: If they spite it, let them not spare, so they will promise
to amend it: and when this promise has perfourmance, then will I cancel all
my malice: But then I fear me they shall experiment, that it is easier to spy
a beam in an other man's eye, then to pull a moat out of their own: that it is
harder to see a great stain in the mantle that is behind them, then to see a
little wrinkle in a gown before them: and less difficill to say in an other
man's edifice, this stone lies not square, then either like Vigetius and
Columella to prescribe true rules of Architecture, or like Besaleel, Ooliab, or
Hiram of Tire, to buyld an house by good Geometry.

The buckler to break of all these blows, is your Lordships favorable
patronage, which if you will deigne to bestow upon me, I shall safely be
defended from all their garboyles. The burdens which they lay upon me with
all their might, of them your little finger shall easily unburden me: Their
malicious fire wherewith they would stiffle me, your very breath shall quickly
extinguish: One dram of the quintesence of wormwood will repel any poison,
and one good word of your good will, thall refell all their morninges.

Thus wishing your Lordship as many prosperous years, as virtuous properties,
and no fewer virtues, then the Sea has sands, And commending both me and mine
to your honorable protection, I humbly take my leave this 28. of November.

Your Lordships most duetifull to command. Brian Melbancke.

TO THE WORSHIPFUL and my very friends, the Gentlemen Students in the Innes of
Court and Chauncerie, and the university of Cambridge.
THe worthiness Gentlemen of your demerits, and the daily benefit of your
friendly courtesies, incite me to proffer you my simple service, and humbly to
entreat you to vouchsafe it acceptance,

I Heartily wish (Gentlemen) that Phylotimus had the property of the tree
Coccus, which bears ten sundry commodities all at once: or that it resembled
the herb Pauemis, whose emeralds gloss refreshes the sight, whose violets
favor delights the smell, whose sovereign juice does comfort the heart in
most dumpish fits of direful melancholy: or that each of you here in my little
garden, might gather a poesy of the three Phoenick flowers. Mirth, Money, and
Melody, the vvorst whereof may serve to deck a Princes garland.

But alas poor toy (if pity might prevail) it rather represents
grateful Tholies thankfulness, then presumes to present you with glozing
^ullies eloquence, and more submissiuelie craves pardon for this too audacious
enterprise, then loftily looks for praise for an undeserving travail.

Yet Brass is good Goulde in a Brasiers Shop, Pottage daintty fare at a poor
man's Feast, and murrye sweet beautye in a Blackamoores face: And therefore I
hope hautily, that if I inuit you to my homely banquet, (where you shall have
single fare for double cost) you will not thank me so ironeously, as Socrates
on a time did a poor honest man, who bad him for good will to his best, though
bare, supper. I pray thee, quoth he, who made us so familiar, that thou
should thus saucily incroch upon my courtesy, to inuit me so unmannerly to
a needy beeuer?

I have been pregnant, as once was Valentia, almost double the time of a
vvomans account, and now at my childbyrth, with the same Valentia, I am
brought to bed of an halfe-shaped Monster. I grant that for want of plenarrity
this time of vacation, I am vvorthelye fallen into your lappes, so that, you may
lawfully refuse to admit me to your favor, and give the benefit of your
friendship to some more meritorious.

Credit me Gentlemen, (I know not by what haps, mishaps I should say)
Phylotimus has been longer in publishing then in compiling, and less time in
penning then in printing: wherein he is not unlike all manner of fruits, which
attein not maturity so soon as they bad, neither are always reaped when they
are ripe: or rather a kin to the Bears confused Brood, which she is longer in
framing to a perfect shape, then she is in conceiving and producing.

Well (Gentlemen) now you have it, give it a vvelcome, read it with judgment
(as I know you can) peruse it with patience (as I hope you will) and mind as
you find, I require no more.

I know that ventrous Will does never sail surely, where practiced skill
does not hold the Helm, and that brainsick youth does never reign well,
where settled age does not bear the Bridle, and that it is more meet for my
vnmellovved years to be employed in the study of Phylosophicall axiones, then
take upon them to instruct others in literature.

Yet have I tried that the youngest pullet is both toothsome and vvholsom, and
that the timeliest harvest makes the best bread: now adays, that Parrat is
very young that will not prattle, and that Cock very bad that crovves not till
his age: the young cat cries mevv as well as the old one, and youthful
Aristippus will be regardant to Philosophy, as well as old Plato is a
professor of Wisdom: if June yield flovvers, May vvil yield Cherries, if
there be blossoms in the spring, there will be fruit in Autumn, and amorous
Plato in his adolescencie, is a grave Philosopher in elder years.

For the excuse of my obscurity, vvherevvith I have endarkened my style, I
aunsvvere, that I am not of Perillus his mind, that thought himself a
glorious Orator, if he were plausible to the Populer sort: I would rather
imitate our master Arist. who writ his Physics in so intricate a style,
because he vvoulde not vouchsafe every refuse Coridon the imparture of his
mysteries: And I doubt not but amongst you (Gentlemen) I shall find more of
Oedipus his line, then of Davu^ his lignage, more that can assoile a doubtful
ambiguitie with their learned resolutions, then vvil inuert a good meaning
which they cannot amend, and turn that to scoffing which was made for no
Skoggins.

If I have used any rare and obsolete words, they are either such as the
Coryphees of our English vvriters, Chaucer and Lidgate, have used before me,
and now are decayed for want of practice: or else such as by an apt
translation out of the Greeks and Latins (which Crassus in Tullies books de
Oratore allovves for lavvfull) are fitly contrived into our English language.

For that I have not kept one uniform manner of style (much like the Queens
Mules that never change pace) I think that I am no more tied to one order of
vvriting, in the universal tractation of my treatise, then a Gardener is bound
but to sovv one sort of seeds in a great plot of his Garden ground. He (says
Hermogenes) that adornes his whole Oration with no other trope, but a svveet
subjection or an anomination, may be thought a trim man in the ears of the
multitude, but in the judgment of the elegant Orators, he shall be known as
rude in his art of Rhetoric, as the butcher that scalded the Calfe, was in his
craft of Butchery.

Good Gentlemen, pardon the faults that are passed in printing, omit the
escapes that are committed by my negligence, and take that well in vvoorth,
which I wish were more then well. It may be Gentlemen, that my english Tables
of all Arist works (which some of you have too long expected) shall shortly
make you amends. Although all of you know that my Kingdom never consisted in
the English tongue, but if ever I had any thing wherein to vaunt, (as God
knovvs I had nothing) it was some small skill in other languages.

Thus not doubting, but you Gentlemen of Cambridge will give Philotimus
friendly entertainment, for that Melbanck his master was sometime a Scholar
amongst you (with what credit I know not, but sure I am in more then ever he
deserved, and in such, as for the love he bears you, he never intends quite to
forsake you) and that you Gentlemen of the Innes of Court will favorably
censure of Philotimus, because I may ere I die be a Soliciter in your law, I
humbly take my leave this 28. of November.

Yours as your own to use. Brian Melbancke.

George Wastnes Esquire, in commendation of the Author.
Vinum vendibile non eget haedera.
Feign would I praise my dearest swetest friend,
Yet do I fear my praise will work disgrace,
How shuold I praise where none can ought amend,
Nor any Momus this his work deface?
If Fortune would agree with natures gifts,
If God and Fortune once would deigne to smile,
A mirror of a man, were Melbancke than,
Whose wit, whose art, whose virtues, now surpass.
In these his youthful years to have such skill?
And now to virtues lore to frame his will?
What learned age with ours once dare compare,
Since Melbancke lives, whose learning is so rare?
God prosper thee (my sugared darling boy)
God give thee once thy hearts desired joy,
And if I live, my love shall prove no toy,
But thou shall find that Wastnes is thine own. Adue.
Thine as his own, G. VVastnes. Erras, virtus fortunae famula.

Philotimus.
The war betwixt Nature and Fortune.
An Interlocution of two distressed wightes, whereof the one more hardy,
harteneth his fellow. Pandolpho, Periander. MIne own best beloved and dear
Periander, well met, long have I sought the, and feign would have found thee,
and glad am to see thee, sweet Periander.


Per. Pandolpho, more heartily welcome then shall appear by thy cheer, though
better be away then have so sle^der entertainment: yet if wishing without hope
might increase my wealth, or sorrow for my want excuse my good meaning, neither
should thy pains be so coldly rewarded, neither shall my nature be suspected
of nigardize. But shadows without substance make brau^^alne fare, and sayings
bear no seals, but deeds be indented.

Ay me therefore and wellaway, and cursed be my miseries most waylfull wight,
whose remedy is patience, and that's a pinching pain, whose comfort is death,
which never will consume me. Sometimes it approaches with his manacing
torments, but still prolonges my life to augmente my martyrdom: I can be no
worse, and therefore I fear not: I shall be no better, and therefore I hope
not. Well Pandolpho, repose thy self in this shady brier, a semelie palace
for so dainty Cates, and in stead of music to make us meriment, recounte our
driery destinies as becomes our dole.


Pan. Good Periander to grieve thy self for these trifles, were to prevent me of
my purpose, and prove thy self unkind: for my coming was to lessen thy woes
with perswations, and thou sighest as soon as thou see me, and to continue
our mutual affection, and thou accountest me as a stranger. I am of
Themistocles his mind that had rather have a man without money, then money
without a man. And of Timolites the Graecians, that had rather have a root
which might hereafter budde and bring forth fruit, then flourishing blossoms
without a root, which must needs decay and wither.

It is not my belly I make my God, but it is thy good would feed and fat me.
The Elephant lives by the air, the Fish by the water, and thy Pandolpho semes
to revive in enioyeing thy presence.

Thou gratest on thy stomach because thou art hapless, and ratest on Fortune
because she is spightfull, either slothfully like a sluggard forgetting
Philosophy, (a sovereign medicine for all such maladies) or cowardlye like a
recreaunte yielding Fortune the conqueste, a faynte hearted crauyne if her force
be not feared.

With what madding mood art thou distraught (unfortunate Periander) when as
those distresses which the best have not escaped, thou alone so scornefullye
disdaynest, or the grief of these casualties which are common to al, thou
seelye soul do wreak on thy self?

If with Quintus Catulus always wise, but once forlorn, thou had rather
spille thy life, then live with shame, it is desperate folly. If with tears
thou meanest to move Fortune to pity, as Iphis would with mourning his stony
hearted lover, as she was deaf and would not hear, so Fortune is blind and
cannot see. If thou art loath ye others should be plagued with thy freting
pangs, and wilt with Ephigenia offer up thy self, one for all to appease ye
Gods, though they were well pleased with her sweet Sacrifice, yet they have not
vouchsafed a promise to thee for acceptance of thy service.

Belike Periander, (be it spoken by thy favor) thou have some ouerwening
surmise of thine own surpassing qualities, and conferring thy conditions with
others misdemeanour, can hardly brook these sharp calamities: thou either
think thy self more worthy then any man to be exempted from these
shattering storms, and yt is haughty arrogance, or else for these brunts that
you art worse then all men, and that is abject peuishnes, that either the Gods
should spare the for thy gifts, and that is self love, or yt they repent them
of their former graces, and that is broad blasphemy. Cease these sighs, cheer
up thy spirits, seek means of mirth, make end of moan. It is an argument of a
good man to be afflicted with crosses, but to bear them patiently a type of
perfect virtue. Art not thou ashamed Periander, that old wives accustomed to
pinching, can fast three days without grudging, That the boys of Sparta may
be tried with scourging to know their courage, yet never tired with stripes
because of smart, Nor ye rascal wrastlers at ye games of Olimpia, offer to
withdraw their heads from the block, or once cry oh at ye stroke of the
hatchet for fear of reproach, and yet thou, thou I say, whose wit is famous,
whose learning excellent, rather a pattern to follow as thou do seem to
others, then fit to take advise as I do deem by thee, should be so simple to
be blinded, or so weak to be daunted, or once to be dismayed with these vain
toys?

Thou art now a stranger from thy native soil, and so was Diogines: to whom
when it was scoffingly objected of one of his enemies, thou art now Diogines an
exile from thy country, nay rather (quoth he) my country is an exile from me.
Was it not a natural accident, fatally incident to best Athenians, most
unnaturally to be bannished from their thancklesse Cities, whom tenderly
they had cherished in all their extremities? much like ye snake that Esope
speaks of, which infected him with poison yt pampered her in his bosom. Did
Marius a Roman of renowned reputation, yet hotlye pursued to a foreign nation,
refuse to to crave aid of his seely clientes, whom erst he had saved and
sheilded from death? But lavish expenses and vntimly bravery, have worn out
your wealth, and bred unseemly beggary, and broching your coin without discretion,
it runs on ye lees to your utter destruction.

Tush man take heart at grace. Was not Cincius a gentleman of large revenues, so
far miscarried with ouer-lashinge affections, that for stately bower
gorgeously trimmed, and sumpteously decked with cloth of Arras, fit for an
Emperor, the gastlye hellishe dungeon must be his lodginge, berefte of all
sight and sollace of man, more fit for Pluto then Cincius a Gentleman,
where bracelettes, and owches, and ringes of Goulde, his amarous ornaments,
must free him from fetters and iron chains too troublesome Implementes, and
so was constrained by mere necessitye, for stynte of strife to sell his
patrimony.

Hard is the choice, and dangerous the chance, when either in pining
melancholye we must live without company, or making league with friends stand
in hazard of treachery. But so sweet is the heavenly sympathye of minds, and
so barbarous sauadgnes to live in no society, that we often contracte us with
those we like and trust, and find them by just trial more then villainous
traitors. Thy wit was to sharp not to be courted with friends, and thy
credit to light not to be inueigled by deceit. In all these thy bitter
plunging perplexities, (Periander) I know there is none more corsiue to thy
conscience, then that the swearing protestations of thy fawning friends should
turn to falsed faith of trotheles foes: that those which with dailye
soliciting sued for thy frendshippe, neither dying nor living but by the doom
of thy sentence, should now be so far from redressing thy distresses, their
own vowed Laelius, that they shun thy presence like some notorious fellons,
to the gaulling of thy heart, and discredit of thy person. But these are no
novelties but to such as be nouices, nor greater wonders to move admiration,
then birds which in summer do build their nestes in England, and in breme
frosty winter make other coasts their harborow.

Ancient antiquities can afford like examples, and it is good to follow our
forefathers. Did not Naso cry out with scritching and howling, that waters
should give heat, and fire make cold, and every source and every river run
back to his head, and flow to his welspringe, and all go astray with a
contrary course, backward, preposterous, and quite against nature? The reason
was, because those his familiar companions lincked unto him by dutiful oaths,
would neither entreat Caesar to restore him from banishment, nor defeat his
enemies of ye pursuit of their pray, nor releue his bare living amid the wild
barre^ Getes, nor visit his poor wife and children at home, with the least
supplye of their needful wantes. When Gisippus is on the hoigh in his
pontificalibus, and poor ^agged Titus lies groueling at his gates, how humbly
can he beg an alms of his master, when lofty Gisippus will not look a side?
Doves use no houses but yt be goodly to the vewe, mise haunt no barnes but that
be stuffed with the harvest, and no point penny no pater noster.

In this matter let me be a mirror worthy marking: for although I am a man of
the meanest making, yet a great torche may be lighted at a little candle, and
in this thou may be boulde to behold my advise, and show thy self wise, in
taking hold of my tackling. I can sit ye whole day long, and make me spoorte
at the spyte of the dizarde muffled Godesse, to see what game she makes at the
grefe of her subjects, this day lifting them up to her loftiest throne, and to
morrow throwing the^ down to her lowest foote-stoole, chopping and changing
from right hand to left, giving him a fan in his hand ye bore a Scepter
before, and laying a yocke on his neck that had a diadem on his head.

I deny the (Fortune) and defy thy frowninges, and dare vaunte with him in
Tusculans questions, that I have vanquished thy vanities, and intercluded all
thy passages, from the approach of my presence. I am poor, so have I peers, I
am homly, so am I honest. Marcus Curtius, Attilius Regulus, Valerius Publicola,
grave in their practices and praised in their graves, lived virtuously at Rome
with small scantling of living.

Fie Periander, so proper a man withowten manhood, thy stock so good and thy
stomach so badd.

Thy parents be dead. Death is natural, and Gods good pleasure, and the Iem of
their Joy, and greueth it you?

By your folly spendall is your store consumed, and by your God the sendall it
may be restored,

Your friends be fledd. In deed you brought them well up till they were flidge,
and therefore no marvel though they be flowne: but thrine you, and they will
strive for your frendshipp. There be too many friends that make great
semblaunce, and this has learned thee to discern their dissemblance.


Per. Ah peace Pandulpho, thy memory breeds my moan, and sight of pre^^^ times
brings sorrow in. I am more grieved with the reckning of my wrongs, then I can
be releued with thy weight of thy reasons. Thy counsel is Philosophical, but
my folly will not consent, thy physic is good, but my disease in desperate.

It is no dismal day, no week, no month, no one misfalling by chance that
mangles my mind, but infinite inconveniences that last all length of time,
increasing in number as they do in continuance* My fears must be fedd till
life shall faide, and I am constreined to languishe in anguishe.

As for thee, thou art less bound to Fortune for proof of prosperitye, then
behoulding to God for thy patience in aduersitye.

Entice me not with tales to follow fained fancyes, enforce me not to swymme
and shoue me on the shelfe. Delay works my decay, or ease me with aid, or
cease to vpbraide.


Pand. had he the might that has the mind, to rescue thee from myserye, thy
gaulling wooes should quickly be appaulde, and s^eringe sorrows turn to
sugar songs: but stay my figboye for a day, which once shall dawne with
glimmering streaming golden ^aies, and shedo this cloud of care.

Our fether-footed Phoebus, whose glymsing waine wends many a league, to ad fine
to his task, is sore attainte with toil and swinke, ere he have paste the
heavenly cope.

First before he take his Journey, he must wait the hours leisure, who are
placed as porters at heavens gates.

The morning way is steep upright, so that in coolest of the day, the
steedes have much a do to clymbe against the hill.

Amid the Heavens, they girde it out with splayed wings, and ouer-stripp the
Eastern wynds, whose hugye height augments the terror still.

The Evening way does fall plump down, that Thetis who does lodge faint Phaeb.
within her wide vaste surges, does stand in fear, least from ye heaven he
hedlong down should glide.

Besides all this, the firmament ay whiskes about with sails swift recourse,
and with his rolling drives the stars their breper course to shift, yet does
Phaeb. keep his beaten path, and does withstand the stoutest brute.

By blind bywayes, and ugly shapes of monsters must he tread: between the
dredefull bulls sharp horns, against ye cruel bow the which ye Aemonion archer
draws, against ye ramping Lion armde with greedy paws and tusks, against ye
Scorpions venimd claws, and eke the awry crabs crooked clees he weals his way.

Through all five zones direct he must not ride, but out askew a broad by
way, that bends on the side, contained within the bonds of three the midmost
zones does ly, which from the Northren beer and Southren pole does ^lye, yt is
his ready way: not mounting over hy lest he inflame ye heavens, nor drowping
over low lest he adure the earth, the golden mean is safest: not to the right
hand overmuch least he be driven upon the wauy sealed snake, nor to much
wresting to the least, lest to the Altar he be pusht that pitched against it
stands.

It is no moment of time yt brings mountains of treasure, but labor and
leisure, prudence and policy, must do ye deed.

Jupiter in his mood spared not his own son Vulcane, but by ye foot threw
him down from the skies, wherein ye air he tumbbled too and (...) a whole day
through, and fell at last with bruised bones in Lemnos i will, at which soul clap
he caught his haultyng haunch. Jupiters steedes of heavenly race, did sometime
need the ierke of golden whip.

The smith cools his iron to straight it and strenghthen it, and God corrects
thee with his rodd, to make thee recke more of his read. Thou have heard I am
sure, how Phoenix, Ajax, Ulysses Achilles, Patroclus, Antomddon, all grecian
Lords, have upon necessity abased them selves without any muttering to execute
ye function of sculls, to make ye fire, and broche their meat, yea worse then
this.

It is not with thee, as with those who have defect in the principles of nature,
whose pains are no boot to obtain the^ bliss, unless some vibootye do happ
the^ by hazard. Thou art not now to be made of the mass, but to be cast in ye
mould and burnished a new: you have exterior matter and interior form, and if
thou had a little pollishing, you might be published for perfect.

The heavenly bodies ar sooner healed then man's corrupted carcasses, as wounded
Mars immediately was cured by Peon physician to the Gods, and Hebes her sweet
baines.

Then since thou have infusion of celestial influence, thou may quickly have
conclusion of these terrene afflaightinges, and God in a tryce can make thee
thryce happy.

Those who^ virtuous constancy does control, says Horace lib. 3. Odisse 3.
are neither removed with vulgar Rumor, nor greatly fretted with threats of
Tyrants: and though that hills and dales should mete, do never change their
moods. But since that we are hear in this vacation of business, to imploye our
deuoyres to deuoyd our ill plights, I will recite yt a story to ye purpose, of
Melanthus his inditing and his poor posse.

Per. of Melanthus his, our old play fellow, and now only plaining fellow?
gramercye by my honestye good Pandol. we shall now be over pearears of his
closets close commentaries, which he has kept so obscure from all our
scanninges, either repining ye others should reap good of his travails, or
mistrusting his ability and misco^struing his good qualityes. But I pray you
of what or whom is this treatise comprised, or what opportunity had he to
compile it? Pand. two days ago I received his letters of salutations, not
forgetting therein thy recommendations, and therewithal he sent us this
present, as a preservative to prevent us of ioye-wrack. The contents of his
epistle were these, yt he having spent his thrifte on that baude Misfortune
common to us three, and having cra^ed his brain with the abstruce study of
some intricate quirkes, he thought that ye composing of this was a relaxation
to his mind, and that it being compassed would be a refection to us: with this
certificate moreover he gave me intelligence, yt though it might seem a fiction
figured by his imaginatiue conceit, yet it was not framed without allusion to
a proper subject. Herein shall then see decyphred by the princely gallant
nature of forw^rd Philotimus, and the pinching galling thwartings of froward
fortune, and by some pamplets penned to the same effect of the said Philotimus,
what bitter debate does ever abide, betwext good Nature and dame Fortune.


Per. Good ^an. let us hear this work, that will prove us wise because we
are unfortunate, and then perhapps by this wisdom we shall contemn fortune*


Pan. I am pleased.


Per. then I I hope I shall be eased.


Pandolpho his posie. Sapit qui suflinet,

The war betwixt Nature and Fortune. The historye of Philotimus.
NOt far from the confines of the south part of Italye, abuts a certain
City surnamed Mantua, neither contemptible for baseness nor desolate for
barrennes, but so well fraughte with traffic for all affairs, and pleasantly
situated for the verdure of the air, as both the poor by pains may here
find relief, and he that is destitute of other delytes, may yet have these
objects to refreshe his senses.

The cheefe-taine and governor of this City Mantua, was a noble young
gentleman called Cle^^ritus, who latelye had espoused his long betrothed wife,
the princelye gallant paragon Lady Castibula, daughter and heir to the Duke of
Bononia.

These two imps in their royal ruff, newelye surprised with uncouth Joys,
for a long season and tract of time, during the space of full five years,
with many an embracing and sporting pastime, ledd such a Lordlye life, as he
would wish that can wyshe aright, and you not knowinge it may possiblye
imagine. Of countenance and office among their compeares, though rather worthy
then willinge to bear any swaye: for competent wealth, of superfluous
abundance, when less might have wrought their settled contentation.

Now, when all things were patt for every purpose, and nothing amiss that
might be amended, to the absolute finishinge of a perfect Felicytye, the
greatest matter of all, in which the fruit of their labors, the staff of
their age, the end of their life were ioyntlye comprised, were all this while
a wantinge, children I mean, wherein we are as it were regenerated and borne a
new, even budding after death by the springing of our seed, which canonizeth
our memory with euerlusting eternitye, by continuance of their lineage, and
propagatio^ of our petegree. Which one backwarde let amongst other good
successes, was as it were aiarring discord in a sweet Diapason, a prickinge
nettle in a pleasant nosegaye, a foul mayme in a fair man. Yet they measured
Gods might not by the aptness of their age, nor his fatherly providence by
present occasion but referring his power to his maiestyes pleasure, never
repined against his judgments, but prayed for his mercy.

So in process, he which makes an end where he does begin, and plentifully
blesses the perseueraunce in trial, when fullness of time had expired her
date by Gods predestinate appointment, and teeminge at length was now unlooked
for, which heretofore had so been longed for, made them on the sodeyne
marvelous fertyle, to the accomplishment of their hope, and advancement of
his glory. And where before they were punished with sterile frutlesnesse, they
now seem ouer-laden with a burdenous charge.

But as all waters run in to the sea and it no bigger, and all stars borrowe
light of the Sun and it no dimmer, and all ye Gods do drink of Nectar and
yet it always over flows: so the n^mber of their children amounting to a
multitude, whose great expenses must needs be proportionate to the quality of
their calling, they were neither impouerished (an extraordinary event) neither
impaired of their wealth (a wonderful conseque^t) But as that Emperor, among
others that were summoned to his hye assemblye of parliament, espieinge an aged
gentleman attended with a troop of worthy bloods in account xxxi. which by
all inquysition were found to be his sonns, and taking their original from a
worshipful house, could not for their quantity have sufficient portions
allotted unto them out of their fathers lands to maintain a credit requisite
to their race: as his heart meltinge on them to see such an ofspringe in
ieoperdye, of beggerye, made them certain conueyances of his crown revenues,
to uphold their port: even so Gods omnipotency yelding such an issue, to the
great procurement of their parents pleasure, did not any whitt diminishe their
former profit, which still was made auneswerable to the charge of their
children. These grew up and prospered in such likely forwardness, being
courtlye brought up to break them of rudeness, and clearklye instructed to
furnishe them with knowledge, that manners and learning marchinge with due
equipage in the order of their lives, the precysest carper would judge them in
both exquisite, but in whether of both they would rather exceed (such resolute
expectation was of rare effect) that none durst give his verdit. They were
lovely and affable to the coursest inferiors, diligent and attentive to the
prescriptions of their rulers, so seruisablye obeysant to their parents
behestes, that for rod of correction to control brainesicke youth, lawful
for parents, and awful for children, they were mildely nurtured with meek
education, having suffitient incitements of their own inclination.

At whose good disposition I do not greatelye muse, although so good devotion
be little in use: yet is such a blessing worthy to be blazed, where by God both
is praised, and all men are pleased.

Mitio and Demea had but two to look too, which both were misled with
hair-braine lust. A postinge messenger bringing hastye tydinges to Dragoras
that his sonns had overcome at Olimpia, dye Diagoras, dye quoth he, for thy
sonns this day are both victorious. Such carke they take to tender their young
ones, which are subject to every cross overthwart of perverse fortune, much
like him that thought himself past peril, when he escaped Scilla, and by and
by offended against Caribdis, or him who being flitt from his fever lurdaines
fytt, the next day quaketh with an other qualme: or the dronken besill that is
reared from the grownde, and Incontinentlye rusheth into the channel.

These things are necessary briefelye to inferte, whereof a large treatise
were to small avail, and as great Indecorum it is to make a long preamble to
a short narration, as a war^de with an head bygger then his body.

And now (Pandolplio) prepare thy pair of ears to receive my Historye,
distill thy trycklynge tears to bewail my Tragedye, O let thy heart sweat
dropps of blood to record his auentures.

And thou Melpemmone the mournefulst Muse of nine, if I have ever daunced to
thy doleful tunes, when the alluringe timpanes of other sportinge Muses could
not once change my coppie, and shrynd thee for a Saynct in the bottom of my
heart, when the service I did thee has turned me to smart: then deigne to dropp
thy heavenly dew, and therewith sprinckle my rudest pensell, not to vaunt of
art, for my heart is not so vain, but to further my pretense in my purposed
description, grounded on no gain, but intending thy glory.

I require no guerdon of the pleasant hedded youths, whose humor this fits
not, nor any reward of the comfortless wights for whose cause I write this,
the one I fear me will scarce bestow readinge, the other is too needye to
bestow any recompense, the toil shall be mine, the thancke shall be thine,
and some profit perhapps shall redoune to others.

Then mighty Goddess, if love have no leisure to be troubled with toys, nor
thou no pleasure to meddle with tryffles, yet reject not the publishinge of his
piteous plight, so relygious a Catholycke in thy sacred Cerimonyes, or at least
respect the honor of thy portly deity, which the world shall brute through
the brunt of his myserye.


Per. What horror Pandolpho affrights my cr^sed heart? what manner Proaemion is
this devout prayer? me thinks I seem to hear his woeful plaining voice, me
thinks I see him tear his curious tressed locks, and feelingly feel the
impression of his sorrows. Ah God, that thou should make man to thine own
similitude, and give him over without thy purueiance.


Pan. War wing, quoth the huntsman to Bowman his hounde: what Periander, shall
the kyndnes to caytyues abate thy courage to God? art thou such a louinge
woorme to succourles creatures, to robb God of his due, which thou ought not
do, and pay them a debte, which thou owe them not?

Beware of Jove and lightning, and fly far from them. Though Venus were
assistant to Paris his adherentes, yet for contempt of Iunoes demand, both
Paris was slain, and Troy was sacked. Midas for faucines against Apollo, was
dubbed for a dolte with a pair of Asses ears. And Phaeton I guess if he were
alive, would not be to busy with Phoebus his chariott. Purchase not to thy self
Lycurgus his scotchlade, who for his rashness to the Gods gaind a wretched life,
and got a painful death. Upon the mou^teyne of Nisa, Lycurgus advised the
women to do Bacchus due service at his festals, with slips of vines upon their
heads: but he so pricked them forward with his sharp twible goad, that they
let fall their holy crowns, whereat he did rejoice and sport his fill: yea
more then this, to disgrace the God himself, he thundered out his threats, and
caused him to fly to escape his snares: at this offense the Gods did loure, and
forthwith did revenge the same in bereauing Lycu^g of his sight for a certain
time, and to punish him aright, and fulfill their will at full, in setting
wreakfull date to his wicked life. Juno boiling with rancorous hate against the
remnaunt Troianes, the good soothfast prophet presaging Helenus, exhorts Lord
Aeneas to win her by submission, least he weaue to himself a web of woe, by his
dissolute defiance. To disanul ye kings decree is to be attainted of treason.
For Pythagoras scholars to ask a reason of any one article of his doctrine,
was accounted amongst the^ almost heresy, and dare you impeach Iehoua of
fleting inconsta^cy, not to continue his gracious goodness, like a treuantlye
Poet in framing his fables, that begins to lagg in his latter description?
or can thou convince him of careless regard, like the brutishe serpente
that killes his brood as soon as it is borne, and brought into light? Or
go thou about to attach him of ignorance in the mystical workemanshippe of
all mankind, which has made him more perfectlye then Science can conceive,
even after the likeness of his spottelesse Spirit, and softereth him with
food as the earth does her plants, when he deserves to be copsse and cast
into the Fire: and when he gaddeth without a guide after whooring inventions,
wandering in a laborinth of his imaginations, when his kin is unkind, and
friends do forsake him, and he in cold shiuering is ready to famishe, he
fetches him home upon his shoulders, (O careful shepherd) and penneth him
in the fold with his chosen flock (more careful then a shephearde.) Shall
the pot ask the potter why he fashioneth it so, or the anuile the smith why he
strikes it with the hammer, or thou search a reason of Gods severe
punishmente, whose name is in Scripture. I am that I am (so incomprehensible is
his majesty) who renders his reason I will that I will (so incapable is his
wisdom?) If any man have tasted some extraordinary mercies, it was his free
will, not their deserte: if either the gentleman of whom the history mentions,
or thou Periander have felt his heavy hand, it is your merit, not his
desire. I reprehend not thy presumption, but reprove thy despair: for
prosperity puffs up with contempt of God his bounty, and adversity pulls
down to distrust his favor, Therefore Periander.


Per. Peace Pandolpho, stay thy wisdom, thou have beat ye child till he
cries again, now give him dugge, and burn the rod. I was to bold because I
was blind, and who so bold as blind bayard? But give me leave to recover
this fall, and rashenes no more shall give me the foil. Though I was incensed
with a too patheticall motion, to utter that retchlesly, which I now repent:
yet was I not driven to hellish desperation, taking Gospel for no Gospel,
which promises remission. But I will make no apology for my passed defaulte,
least thou think it apostacy to persiste in my error: now if it please
thee, proceed in the premises.


Pand. The recantation of thy sayings is a requital for thy escape, and
therefore I proceed.


The sequel of Philotimus his history.
OF all this their joyful progeny, their first begotten son named
Philotimus, did both in feature of body, sharpness of wit, and towardnes of
mind excel the rest. In so much, that as when Jupiter and Juno were at
contention, whether man or woman were more prone to lechery, they elected
Tyresias, by whose arbitriment they would be determined, because he had been
both manne and woman: so if there were a controversy, which of the Gods were
most worthy wonder in their excellent works, I think none more indifferent
to decide the matter, then this young gentleman: in whose courtly complexions
(so sweet was Philotimus) and courteous conditions (sweet lovely Philotimus) the
express Images of their heavenly Deities were so liuelye portrayed. And yet
if Philotimus should give his censure, which of their powers he thought most
puissant, though he have felt their mighty operations, and so might judge: yet
do I ween his best resolution would only be silence. For as an Unicorn
having most strength, does least know it: so he having greatest pith, did most
pity himself. Notwithstanding that the Gods being corriuals in the framing of
him, and never one suffering a fellow cockmate in this joint labor, they all
doing best without comparison it was hard to judge who did the worst, even ye
worst being placed in the superlatiue degree. Which his parents marking, and
meruailing at it, thought themselves especially bound to be careful on him,
lest either nature wanting nurture, it should be mard in making, and so the
Gods offended with their wilful negligence: or his good disposition being at
liberty, having no leader but his own discretion, they might reap less
pleasure in vewing his good luck, having taken no pains in his bringing up.
They therefore knowing that a young colte must be used to the bitt before he
come to the saddle, that sprigs sprout the better if they be lopped, choose
rather to be niggardes in pinching him of pence, then procure his nicenes with
vain prodigallitie, rather liking a wan look that comes of moderate diet, and
bewrayes a painful student, then a fresh ruddy cheek that is died with
Bacchus his buries, and breeds suspicion of lust.

Well, the Sun and the Moon and the seven stars, and all else that I can
name, according in an unity, to adorn with their Trinity this blessed
gentleman, he costed and posted with such light-foote speed, that coting and
bording all his coequalles: with whom he was conversant, he was ye gaze at
which all eyes did spy, the worst repining, the best envying, all admiring
this uncouth straungnes, and knowing the possibility of his wealth by descent
of inheritance, thought the revenues of his virtues would be inualuable.

His father seeing his diligent industry, friendly and freely counterpeyzd with
incredible increase, taking him aside on a certain day, brake with him in these
terms.

Although I am not ignorant, that liberty to the subject to do what he list,
does cause his losenes and the Princes contempt, yt the apprentise made whole
factor of his masters Merchaundize without any account, may leave him in the
lashe unless he be the trustier, and that giuinge the bridle into thy hands, to
use the rain or ye spur at thine own pleasure, may be a means of thy rashness
and my repentance: yet knowing that a horse may be brought to the brook, but
will not drink except he list, that an Orator may use his coulors of
Rhetoric, but cannot persuade unless you be pliable, that virtue is
voluntarye without constraint: and hoping besides, that Wit can discern, and
Will will desire, thy contentation, and my consolation, (which are one in
effect) I am content to do with thee, as King Henry the 8. of that name in
England, did with a Bishop of Canterbury, that is, make you commissioner in
your own cause.

Yet with this caveat, yt you deal not with me, as books make mention of S.
Beda his boy, who made his blind master preach to stones for a pastime, and so
thou trying my gentleness, abuse it with wantonness: much like the prelates in
Constantines time, who countnaunced by his majesty, as men of great
dignity, did at length checkmate the Emperors highness, usurping his
supremacy.

It is ill waking a sleeping dog. There be certain springs in the country of
Archadia, whose excessive cold breaks all vessels and mettalles, yet the
horn of an Asses house keeps together. In France, there is a Well of such
chilling coldness, that it will convert wood into stone, and yet oftentimes,
flakes of flaming fire have been seen to issue from it. It is hard for my anger
to be incensed against thee, yet may thy stubborn rebellion enkindle my cold
choler.

If powdered fish or flesh be soked in salt bryne which is newly made, the
brine does sooner draw the saltenes out of it, then the fresh water cold: so
thy vnweeldy crookedness and contemptuous, waiwarde will, may draw thee like
out of me in thy he half and make regardelesse consideration of thy thriuinge.

It is difficult (Philotimus) to quench my queint love, unless as a torche
that is turned downewarde, thy bounden obedience turn to sirly negligence, and
impossible to pluck up the roots of my care, so long as thou have a branch of
filial fear.

Yet misconster not these dreads as misdoubting thy well doing, but take them as
a pledge of thy fatherly duty. For as Stratonicus never went to bed without his
livery, (yt is a cup of bear standing by him) not for that he was always
thirsted, but always mistrusting least he should thirst: so I give thee this
charge, not as fearful for thy fall, but I tell it the at large, as careful
of thy standing. But if once, Well I will stay my self till occasion be given,
for though I cast the a bone to bite on, I would not have the choked. To make
short harvest to my seed long a sowing, this I have to say.

Thou know it was a time, when the three Aethereall Madams quarrelling about
the golden ball, chose Paris for their vmpeere, a shepherd then in Ida, to be
judged by his award, whereof each of them promising a large reward, to corrupt
his judgment, and he intending to get the fairest, snatching at the bayte, got
nothing but the hook, and thinking he had got a goodly young heafer, it was
nothing but ye devil in a cowes hyde, as Richard Farneyeares son was once
beguiled. Much like, as though Ulysses rejoicing in the smoke of his country
Ithaca, as a sign of his nere approaching, should have found at his arrival
his country on fire.

The same Goddesses and their rewards do I propounde to the, expecting that
thy choice will be charier then his, or if thou aymest at the same mark, come
nearer the white.

For true it is and an old said saw, that timely grows the tree ye good
ca^mock will be, and he that will be an old man long, must be an old man
soon, and he yt means to be singular in any kind of trade, must begin betimes
to inure himself with it. Therefore Hercules being young, consulted with himself
what course to take, and Cato being old, learning Music and Greek, wished he
had begun in his former years.

Now therefore descend into thine own conscience, and deliberate with thy
self, to which of these three thou meanest to dedicate the springing tide of
thy spring time.

Whether the first of all to Pallas, and to live at the University, there to
attain the profound and sound knowledge of the liberal arts.

Or honor sacred Juno with solemnizing her marriage, where looking to thy
tillage, and the grafting of thy plants, and tenting ye pruning of thy
ouerspreading trees, thou shall not only amplify thy possessions, but as ye
world goes now adays be preferred to promotion: which way if thou can
brook, I will provide such a match, as shall counteruaile ye honor of thy
house, and the humor of thy years.

Or if none of these will fit thy fancy, will thou vow thy self to Venus, and
court it a while? where heed must be taken that thou be not to he addy, for
there the trod is tickle and ye footesteps slippery, where many a proper youth
thinking to rise alofte, is magnified til he look as high as Lincoln,
climbing up by a ladder and an hempten coard, higher the he would by half a
yard from the ground.

It is a property common to most youths hunting at the inhauncing of credit
and iolitye, that if they see some advanced to wealth and estimation, rather
by casual then usual means, they think by imitation, to gain the like
succession, when gapinge for greediness with Aesops Dog, they scatter the
meat they have in their mouths, while they scratch for the shadow which
deludes them with show.

As for Pallas, she is dainty, but not squemish, hard to be found, but easy to
be entreated, to be far fetchte and dear boughte, but that we say is good
for Ladies.

This pilgrimage will be werysome, and yet it must be warye. For through many a
roaring wilderness, and stony scarry rocks shall thou make thy voyage, where
hips and hawes must be thy pittaunce, thorns and brambles thy pallet and
cabinet, the fire to warm thee, the scortching of the sun: thy clock to
time thee, the scritching of ye owl: and then thy pleasant prospecte to
recreate thy mind, a vast confused Chaos withouten shape or form: bellowing
and braying shall be thy music, teene and melancholye thy desired companions,
and when nights dark mantle ouerhailes ye sky, at calm quiet midnight when
all things are whust, and nothing now sturring but pale dame Cynthia tugging at
her teame to bring the night about, full many a Calcas dream shall rome in thy
head, and Morpheus present the with vgsome sights, where in place of a watch to
limit thy rest, either grimme grisly horror shall break the of thy sleep, or
a ball in thy hand (which was Alexanders wont) shall awake thee falling from
the in ye beginning of thy slumber.

These are thy iourneyes, this is thy soiourning, troublesome and tedious,
lothly and yrksome, but now take heed.

For stalking on with redoubled pace, for slowing no time to abandon the place
of this abode, suddeinly unawares on thy right hand, a flowring meade clad with
lusty green, (wherein stands built a regal portly palace) presents thine
eyes. In this palace, far from above and a lofty vewe, out of a goodly bay
window, salutes thy sight the rarest But (...) O Philotimus, now dread and
dolor both assail my heart, I fear the lines that foulers set, lest they
ensnare yt in their net. For from thence gazeth out the seldomst sweet, that
ever nature cast within her mould, with lilly white hands, and arms
outstretcht, with Iuory neck, and bared breast, and goldylockes flickering in
the air, her countenance roseall, embellished with alablaster, intermedled
yfere with party coulors: the virtue of her thralling looks so bravely gloste
with glimsing grace, that thou would wish no other books to read or look
upon.

To thee she rushfullye addresseth her complainte, the trickling tears bedewe
her dented cheeks, with many a sighfull sign she pleades for pity, and
cunningly prates (as she can ye feate) ye tears of truth may once appease her
plight, Good Sir respect her piteous plaintes, whom Fortune sierre so sore
attaintes, that ah for want of joy she faintes, (some pity good master.) And
meaning to plant her brinnish plaints within thy tender ears, hark how the
strumpet can strain her voice, to delighte with her deceit. (...) (Disdain
me not without desert, nor leave me not so sodeinly, so do the stony rocks
repulse the waves that rush them violently.) Thou standinge at the gaze, (as
the Dear they say at the smell of an apple, or rather in a maze, to see so
stout a kite stoop to thy fist without a lure, thus you hearing and seeing and
saying nothing, she will not lose her penny worth for want of good cheaping,
but thus again begins. What trylle the ball again my Jack, and be content
to make some play, and I will lulle the on my lap, with hey be bird now say
not nay.

But never trust thou these training toys, and ever restrain thy loving
glaunce, tread on the even path, not once slipte aside, for fear of Had I
wist prove a fool.

Thus when with fained tunes she has chirpt her yelden lays, and perceiues
that fauning can force no fancy, she will assay an other way to flap the in
the mouth with flimflam floutes, to dash the out of countenance, As that for
one: It is no fire that gives no heat, though it appear never so hot, and
they that run and cannot sweat, are very dry and lean godwot: but since I
lend my love to loss, fancy (says she) farewell, adue dastarde, a calfe be
your comfort, go mask among meacockes, a fig for your reasons.

But run thou on without return, least in striving against the stream thou
happily ouerturne, and if her iestes be joy to her, let her gibes bewray a giglot.

If thou pass these pikes and chance to escape scotfree, as thou may if
thou will, and must if thou do well, let not the sequel of thy journey be
directed with security, but intend a good end, and be watchful in thy ward,
and stick to thy tackling providing for more storms. For an ague has many
shifts of fittes. Jupiter has more liknesses then one wherein to transfourme
his feature, and when he cannot speak to Danae in the form of a man, he will
fall into her skirts as a golden shower, to quench the flame of his affection:
so coosoning Lust has many counterfit crankes, and when with Crocadiles tears
hardly wrested out, she can not move thee to ruth, she will make a
Metamorphosis of ye device, and shaping a new coat of an old blanket, bleare
thine eyes; if she can with stale sale-ware. For listen (my good son) again:
unlooked for, the most dulced melody that mind may imagine, more delicate to
thine ears, then honey to thy mouth, which thou would judge, I dare say, to
be ye consorts of the nine Muses, surpassing the modulation of Achilles his
viole, which he wan at ye overthrow of Aetions kingdom, and far surmounting
ye Philosophers harmony, which they forge in the heavens by a measurable
motion, and a just proportion of number. There thou shall see a squint eied
paramour, gingerly trip it, and minionly minse it, with dainty disdain of any
man's love. The fine divine concubine, soot nice and coye, (screuerence on her
peticote,) will scarce look askaunce, and all this (Philotimus) to bring thee
to ye bent of her bow. For women they say do ye which is forbidden them, and
fish bite more eager when ye bait is pulled from them, and men then are
soonest attatched with love, when women keep aloof with disdainful
dalliaunce. But remember thy lecture my good child, and always shun such
bitter sweets. Argos naytheles his hundred eyes, was napt with music, and
brought to hedd with Mercuries pipe, Ulysses would not once give ear to
Calipso, lest as castles that come to parlie do oftentimes yeald, so he giving
ear should be won to her whinings. Then do you resist with defiing alarm,
that never witching words thy marble ears do charm, stop thine ears and
make no tariaunce, trusse up thy packquet and trudg on thy way. Play with the
nettle never see nimbly and it will sting thee, touch ye pitch never so softly
and it will defile thee, and lend but thy listening to her warbling notes, and
trust me my son, before thou try it, her chaunting will enchaunt thee.

For hark awhile and I will decipher to thee these two deludinge trinketters,
maugre all their glorious gloss, wherewith they shine so sheene, do but
disgrade the^ of their robes, and they are the most unsavory creatures, Foe
filthes, I loathe to think of them, and thou would swound, if once thou
dyddest see them, our Curtizanes now adays, may well be their children, who
seek to color their ill-favored phisnomies, with painted confections of the
apothecaries coining. Well sir, these two of whom I was speaking, in show are
two, in substance one, and if thou art willing to know their names, in one
word it is Lust: which what a vice it is among Palaces apprentises (scholars
I mean, whom I cannot but moan) I am sorrye to know, and ashamd to
acknowledge: but to set thee foreward in thy attempted enterprise, and teach
thee sufficient cosmografie to conduct thee in thy voyage, this I will foreshew
thee.

The stormiest blastes are ouerbloune, thy suptelest enemies ouerthroune, only
with one thou have to encounter, which will make a great value of his small
valor, and boast it out with bumbaste bragges, as ready to pick a quarrel
without offense, as swift to take his feet for his own defense, if he be
withstood.

Mary if thy heart fall a kneelinge, foredone with fainting, and thy quailing

for presently you perish: he is not of Cneius pompeius his manlyhode, who

A Gorgon like clown he is to see too, with gourdy gutts, and a graund
liricompaunch, made in ye wast like a row with calue, a cobbler may keep his
shopp in the lower region of his belly: a terrible tartarian bearde he has, a
notable harbor for the crablouse: a boisterous thundering voice, good to chyde
beggars out of a noblemans court: his nose may be a comfort in distres, and a
light in darkness, and serve for a linder box to light a candle at in winter,
if he cast up his nose against the sun with open chapps, by his teeth may be
discerned what is a clock perhapps: his garland is twyned of venomous snakes,
his attire made of poisoned vipers, his assaults be furious, his countenance
ferefull, no ruth, all rage, does reign in his kingdom he is comenly called
in one term, Despair.

The way to delay the heat of his stomach, is this.

Thou remembrest Maroes mention of the Troiane Aeneas, who after the intombing
of his father Anchises, desiring to visit him in the Elysian fields, got the
prophetess Sybilla to gyde him his way, and a sorcering golden spraye of
Dianas tree in Sycile, for his pasport to Charon the churlish boteman, to ferye
him over the lake. By these two he^ps did he atchiue his appointed purpose: so
if thou can please thy-lyues, lady Pallas, betimes in the morning saying her
service, with her grace for a preface at dinner and supper, and square thy life
to her direction, she will allow thee two servant, Diligence, and Hope, to
attend upon thee: the one to add have to thy business that thou loyter not in
the way, the other to add speed to thy skudding pace, that have make no wast.
For at the very sight of thy faithful Hope, this monster Despair is utterly
vanquished and vanishes from his houlde.

After this, thou rowest over a drenching gulf, which will purge thy stomach
of ill humors, and make it more fytt for Pallas her fare, where though thou
art tossed with whirlinge tempests, yet as long as diligence is thy loadesman,
thou need no other Palinure. For at length thy roughe tide will bring a
safe road: and then enjoy thy joys, for thine is the victory, and let thy
silver song, be so triumph.

Thus thy stormy showers, have bread fragraunt flowers, thou have won goulde,
now wear gould, loc what it is to labor?

Jason adventured to cross the seas, and by the means of Medea, gained the
goulden flece: but if thou do mean to give this adventure, and wilt follow
my advice, my counsel shall coin thee no goulden fleece, but a thing more
precious, and yet no precious stones, but a thing more worth gould then either
of them both.

For lo the royal seat of thy sovereign Pallas, so well appointed with all
kind of ordinance, and champions in pay to leuy any siege, that shall be
laid against her bulworkes, that neither policy by trayns, nor prowes by
force, but only those, which have deserved it by service, and she does deigne
to enterteine can come within her howlde. At the very portall, the first
liberal Art, as her function requires, does keeepe ye entrance: she examining
what profit thou have prowled by her profession, will admit thee to her house,
and so inseueralty, being orderly canuassed a all ye Liberal Siences in their
offices, if they find thee correspondent to the dignyty of ye place, they will
all subscribe to thy supplication. Then have thou access to thy princes Dame
Pallas, who after the suruew of thy worthy exployts, will crown thee with a
garland of her own green laurel, then shall thou wear the wreath of
worship, (as Sysamis says in an epistle to Theotimo) which mauger envy, and
mallyce, and all the spite of fortune, both in life, and after death, shall
extol thy name.

Thus have I discoursed the whole course thou must take, not vaynly to affright
with dreaming illusions, nor to terrify from learning with displaying the
danger, but that nothing happen vnbethought, without a ready buckler to award
the blow: for store is no sore, unless it be of sores. I have spent more time
in this first argument, because more pains with some expense, and greater
pleasure for recompense, are to be found by this the^ any of the rest.

Pallas has played her part, now harken Iunoes pageant.

The world my son turns westward, and begins to decline, and men are more
froward then in years of yore: the longer it lasts ye less while men live, ye
longer it lasts, ye less godly men be: the one is Philosophy, ye other
Divinity, both grounded upon too true experience. Yt is well therefore to make
hay while the sun shines, when wind is at willto hoyse up sail, to enrich
thy self in these vnmellowes years, yt hereafter taking ease, thy toyling may
cease, and thou enjoy ye fruit of thy hands. Unless thou weaue a web for
thine own wearing, thou shall have an hard peniworth to buy it else where.
Cloth is so dear, nowadaies (Philotimus) yt commonly they have two faces foe
one hoode, otherwise then it was in ye second King Richards time king of
Britteine, who calling for a pair of hose of the brauest fashion and most
sumptuous to put on that day, asked what they cost: one toulde him forty pence,
go quoth he, and fetch me a pair of a marcke, wherein thou may see, yt
either things were cheper, or me^ wiser then they are now.

Once have a smattering of the sweet of wealth, and thou will need no
persuasion to persevere in getting. An Emperor of Rome being loath to lose any
commodity, that might be saved, gave a strayt charge, that every household
within the City, shoulo save their urine, whereof in time he levied a great
some of money: his son standing on his pantuffles, and of a more
gentlemanlike mind, then his father (as most sonns are now a days)
secretlye and under correction, admonyshed his Father that it was a derogating
from his maiestyes highness, to make a gain of so vile excrementes, the very
sent whereof was so loathsome unto all men. The Emperor perceiving his sonns
nicetye, stood not to debate the matter with excuses, but incontynentlye
fetching a bagg of goolde out of his closet, held it to his sonns nose, and
demanded of him how he lyked the smell: he answered without pausinge, that
it was a very pleasant smell: then hereafter quoth the Emperor, be not so
hastye to condem the Lawful and honest gain of urine, whereof this comes. I
amplyfye this example to this allygation, for that I neither would have thee
deferr opportunitye to profit thy self, nor think any means too mean, if
they be honest

For Venus, I am to young to know her sleightes, and to old to learn them. If

Indeed every thing has a beginning (as the parasite told his master, which
said he took his servant for no liar, and now had sound it contrary) and
therefore we must work by line and leisure, and not leap over the style,
before we come at it. No man I trow, will take upon him to write a volume,
before he can set his letters, neither learn his letters before he be of some
years. To exercise in Rhetoric (says Crassus) before he have discipline,
will make him ready to speak, and barbarous in speaking, as he brings example
of himself: and to commit wealth to him which yesterday came out of the shell,
and yet has neither learned by reading, nor use; how to add to it, neither by
either of both, knows the sweetenes of it, which would make him keep it,
does much in my mind (under correction be it spoken) resemble him, that leaves
his glove by the way, and bids his spaniel bring it after, that is a rude
whelpe, and never taught to carry.

wings: which Bladud king of England aduenturing to do by art, brake his
neck. So did the men of Gotam tie their rentes in a purse about an hares
neck, and bad her carry it to their Landlord: how well they were served, I
cannot but smile to think, neither at that, but at their follies.

Were it not very absurd for one that has no skill to begin to build an house,
and lay the foundation, saying that by building he would learn to build? or
for maids being scarce borne, to begin to bride it, and say in so doing they
would learn to wiue it? I wis it were, and most absurd.

But indeed to give any great regard to wealth at all, more then to expel
cold, and sustain nature, is nothing but vanitye. Alas what praise is it to be
covetous of that, which makes no difference of good men from bad, says he in
his Ethickes, and to say truth, rather a note of ill men. For Plutarch I
remember has these words. It is very probable that every rich man either
has been wicked, or ye heir of a wicked man, which though it be not true, yet
the Philosophers words do never want their weight.

It is good say you, to be stored with diverse sorts, because it is constant in
nothing but inconstancy.

Of diverse sorts it needs not, when one may suffice, of diverse sorts it boots
not, when all is uncertain.

For ye formers proof, let Marcus Curious serve, a Roman captain against King
Pirrhus and the Samnits, who at the spoil of any town, would take no more
then he gave his soldiers, saying he was an evil citezin, that could not live
with that, which satisfied others. When the servants of the Samnites brought
him gold for a present, as he sat seething rapes in an earthen vessel, he
refused it, saying, he had rather be Lord of them that owed the goulde, then
be vassaile to the desire of so contemptible pelf.

For the second, I have heard this reason alleged against Aristotle, to prove
a terrae motus, that is, an Earthquake, diffused throughout the earth, which he
denies. When the parts and the whole (says mine author) are all of one
quality, and the parts does move, as all may see, the whole likewise may do
the same: but the parts of the earth, and the whole lumpy mass are of one
nature in respect of firmnes, therefore etc. and since false fortunne say I, is
the gouernesse of of all wealth, if one part is frail, it is something like
that all is fragile.

Epaminondas was wont to forsake all presents, saying, he would do that was
right without rewards, and for rewards he would do no wrong.

When the kings fisher lays her eggs, the seas are calm till they be
hatched, and then with roaring fury carries them all a way the ore is made much
on for his hide, the Elephant for his tooth, and so shall I for that, which
they would wish my death for.

He that will be welthy, must conform himself to the fashion of the world
(says Plutarch) look a man in the face, and cut his throat.

Wealth is the harvest of fortunes flattery, which who so means to catch,
must be as it were a glass wherein others may see their conditions, much like
an ape, that mops and mowes as his master does, to make him sport. He must
laugh when they laugh, so shall he be merry, swear that they answer, and
that's a good figboy. If with Caligula one speak like a fool in ye senate
house, he must say with Domitian, that his eloquence shows him to have
laien with the moon.

The cunning Fowler is clothed with feathers, the craftye Raunger in a Deeres
skin, Alcibiades was at Athenes a Scholar, at Lacedemonia a Hunter, at
Tisapherne a carpet Knight.

But trust me, to gain all the wine of Calidon, ye fishes of Hircany, the
dainties of Media, I mean not to make my self an open president, for proof
of these matters.

For as well may he stand still, whom the Devil drives, as he leave of,
that once assayes to gather wealth, as you your self for-warned.

Sore eyes may not vewe the light without a scarf, nor ye credit of
cormorauntes show it self without their coin.

He that bestows rewards to insinuate himself into friendship, is like
Bath-kepers ass, that brought his master fuel home to make fires, and he
himself lived with smell of the smoke.

Those second sort of liberal men (as Aristotle terms them) whom you feed
with gifts, gape for them by custom, as they do for their die^, willing to
pick a quarrel, if they be not continued, and ready to cast you of, if you
stand need to them.

If you your self (dear father) would disclaim in this case, or give it over
by non-suite, and on other atturneye retained in your room, I could find him
sport by my little Philosophye, if I were disposed to play with his nose, or
cast pearls before swine in steed of brass.

he might trick his speech with a few superficial colors, but all his statly
