The question of marriage is abortiue, and therefore must needs be a weakeling
too timely marriage says Aristo. in his Pol: filles the common wealth full of
dwarfes, and women: and therefore he thinks it good that men should live sole,
till thirtye seven years, and women till 18.

Homer scares me from these matches, who brings in Jupiter and Juno ever
brawling, the Massagets told Pompey they lay with their wives but once a
week, because they would not hear their scowldinges in the day, nor their
pulinges in the night.

If my wife were naught, I might wish her long enough at the devil ear god
would fetch her, and if she were good, I should fear ear she died she would
be turned into a fury, as Iphanassa the wife of Melampus, was for her bewtye by
envious Juno: it is more agreeable to my years to be termed ser boy, then an
honest man.

Among all forts of conceyted fellows, I reverence the Essenians, as most
co^tinent in pleasures, and contented with nifles, for they abhor ye company of
women, and detest ye possession of gold and silver. Thus Venus is wanton and
ever is wanting, treasure is tickle, and a iuell of ieopardy, marriage marrs
all, if it be out of season. I utterly therefore renounce these two Goddesses
and any content with the loss of a double game, to post it over to Pallas: yet
at our parting blow, to reco^cile my self with meek intermission, I heartily
wish they be not offended, and to make sure my atonement without fear, I will
deal with them, as the Romans did with Vulcane: for having received some hurt
of fire, they thought it good to hold a candell before the devil, and speak
him fair while their feet were in his mouth, and give unto Vulcan the honor
of a God, mary they shut him out of ye city to bannish him their company, that
he came no more there: and if that can please them, I can brook to call them
Goddesses, so they release me of their service.

Poor Aglus for his vprightnes in small substance, was preferred by Solon
before the cuffine Craesus. Agesilaus said, he had rather be king in Sparta,
then God in Theseus. One goinge about his business with an haulter in his hand,
chanced to find monye which a miser had hid, which money he took, and laid
down the haulter in the place: the old huddle missing his monye at his next
visitation, took the haulter and hanged himself, and venus herself passing by
Eurota, was enforced to beg some aid of Pallas and use her lance.

I will not blow retreat to every train, nor be a scholar and a traitor,
as Critias was to Socrates, I have not so soon cast my Philosophers head,
neither have I been hitherto bathed in Heraclitius his floode, to be changed
to a new nature.

In old time they were forbidden to expound oracles which had any botch in
their bodies, and they which would inuest themselves in an house of religion,
should impart all their goods, and give them to the needy, I therefore with a
courteous couge, humbly take my leave of Juno, and bid Venus adwe with a gentle
Vibaccio, nothing dowbting, that as when Hanibal laid seige to the city
Casilinum, one mouse was sold for two hundred pence and he that sold the
mouse dyed himself for hunger: So I for greedines to learning in this hard
world giving over my claim of wealth all at once (as Antiochus at one push lost
all Asia) shall in time feel sorrow and small bear run full near my heart.

In deed a lean fee befits a lasye clarke, yet may I well compare Pallas to
Amaltheas horn, which yields what fruit soever one desires, although, as none
knew the use of Hercules his poisoned arrows, but Philoctetes, so none knows
the benefit of Pallas, but her faithful servant.

It is written of Plautus, that spending all he had in plaiers garments, he was
brought to such poverty. yt for living he was feign to serve a baker in
turning a Querne, from which when he could be dismissed he writ comodies.
Policrates was so happy, that he sought misfotune by casting a ring he had of
inestimable value into the sea, which one finding in a fishes mouth, restored
it to Policrates, insomuch that wishing mishap he could not have it, yet
afterward was he hanged by Orontes a persian: notwithstanding I hope that
though I renounce my profit, and request in some sort my evil fortune, yet as
I shall obtain what I wish, I mean learning, so I shall miss that I do not
crave, I mean beggerye.

Iustinianus the Emperor descending of so base a stock that his uncle by ye
mothers side was a swynehearde, was advanced by Pallas to his immortal
dignity.

The labors I shall support ere I come to the port, do nothing quail my
kindled courage. Euclides could every night go to Socrates at Megaera, mount
Athos was made nauigable by zerxes, Caesar made two great bridges in one day,
and who has not heard of Hercules his labors, why then should I fear so
small a pains.

In a certain battle betwixt the spiders and the flies, the sielie flies
seeing them selves ouermatched began to pluck in their horns, where there
general inciting them, bad them decline their eyes from their enemies force,
and look to themselves to save their lives. Catiline in his conspiracy,
encouraged his soldiers with this argument-that they should look on the
standard, the silver Eagle, which was the old ancient of their mother Rome,
and sight for it. A pleasant pray entiseth many a thief. A lover of Cassandra
who was come to aid Troy for her sake, seeing her hayled and dragged by her
golden tresses, inflamd no less then Hercules with Dianiraes shirt, flange in
headlong among their enemies, neither could be restrained by any fear of
death, till he had rescued her.

Caesar at the river Rubrico, dowting of his passage, a young man suddenly
appearing all in white apparel, snatched a trumpet from a stander by, and
leaping into the river sounded it, by the which instigation Caesar gave the
adventure to pass the river. The Graecians somewhat couwardly in ye wa^
against the Medeans, seeing the shape of Theseus, as Coronell in the vauntward,
were by and by incensed te battle.

All these jointly, and every one severally, I allege, to show what heat ye
blazing hew of Pallas (which methinkes I see a far of) does breed in me.
Eneas having lost Creasa his wife in his escape from Troy, retired back again
amids ten thousand enemies to seek her, and when he could not find her by no
pryinge in every corner, he lifted up his voice inuironned on every side with
his foes, and loudlye cried out, Creusa, Creusa, neither ever would leave
ransacking til her ghost appeared to him and told him she was dead. How often
did ye said Eneas forage upon his enemy Turnus? how often did Turnus and
Eneas trauerse their skirmish for their dear Lauinia? It is a shame to give
over says Seneca when ye thing sought does recompense ye pains. I therefore
am purposed with an absolute determination (my duty to you reserved) never to
tire till I have found ye holy land, where pallas houlds her imperial seat,
which happy hour when it shall arrive, the^ shall I think my self in person
glorious, in doctrine wise, in deeds heroical, vnboweled of grief, entrailed
with virtue, embellished with a memory surmounting mortality.

The lord Cleocritus hearing this munificall speech compiled by his son, with
such dexterity in speech, and pregnancie in with (a right certain ensign of a
generous heart) was rapt with admiration, and almost captiuate with so ioious an
agony, yt he could not covertly conceal it with a guileful dissembling, but at
length all repleat with pestring joys boulted out these words.

A gospel on ye mouth (philotimus) and gods blessing be ever thy protection
for as there was no better token of winter, then to see phocion go shod: so
there can be no more manifest mark of valiant magnanimity, careful for him
self and his countries behofe, then to see youth hang up ye bitt of wanton
time, not like ye foolish lark, deceived with swetnes of ye call: although as
the same phocion was he who^ Apollo meant was contrary to all ye city, so this
is a contrary guise to ye frequented trade of all green years.

In deed (philotimus) though I vn^oulde the whole far^ell of dangers which
might seath thy journey, and teach thee a stopgaliard in the pilgrimage of thy
voyage: yet truth it is, as you of thy self art well advertised in remouinge
all offenses, which might escandalize thy courage. It ^ares with Pallas, as
Suetonius writes of Octauius ye Emperor, yt never any ambassadors came in his
presence, which at ye first sight were not touched with fear, and after in
communicating did not adore and love him because great was the majesty,
wherewith he did receive them, and after sweet were ye words, wherewith he did
dispatch them.

Yet if yt wilt not in inconstancy imitate the soldiers of Germany, whom
old Antiquaries have regestred to begin their assaults with choler, and
continewe the same with fury, and after with requests of collusion, to suffer
their sharp spurrs be blunted, and recalling their intents, fall into a relaps,
Thou must follow ye Roman victorers, who in their triumphant celebrations
first visited the temples, embruing ye aultars with fore-vowed sacrifices,
before they undertook any other solemnities in their triumphs, revels in their
sports, affairs forth weal publiques. For as it was inacted by the martial
laws, to be no less then the loss of his life, yt entered ye captains
pauilion without his leave for a warrant: so it will be prejudice to his
success, ye attem^^s pallas, induced with a toie or emulation, and not invited
by devotion, first get her benevolence with invocation of hartye prayers,
which pallas will be so much the more prone to license (if your tongue be not
too laxatiue, and utter more then your ha^t can digest) by how much ye more
oportunitye of time is now convenient

For as alexius afterward emperor spoke in an oration he had to ye senate, ye
sea that now may be spurned, sometime may not be touched, and Coonts that be in
season betwixt Christmas and Ca^delmas, after ye prefixed date expired may be
vagarant where they will without any man's eating. Notwithstanding philotimus thy
aptnes be quadraunt on every side, as fit as a die, yet you art not to learn yt
a great harvest, must have a great tillage, ye milk must be bruised before it
be butter, and lastly as to have is good happ, so to hold fast is a great
virtue otherwise you may for a time bleare men's eyes with a flourishing gloss,
but at ye length when ye cock shall crow, and ye day begin to dawne and you
shall be urged to publish thy skill, and bring it to light, it will befall unto
thee as yt did unto Veturnus Turinus, who being about ye emperor as one in
great favor, had egres and regres, neither hatch before door, noe meek before
his snow^, to intercept his patene access: which such as as had any suit to
dispatch with the prince perceiving, as men ye cast water in the sea, sooner to
win the haue^, or as fulsum huswiues greasing the fatt sow in the tail to
make better bacon, so they bribed him that was abundaunt in all possessions, to
have an end of their long trauersed suits: he something saucie with his liege
the Emperor, because gains were sweet, would now and than step into
presence, finding communication of other matters, and coming out of the
chamber, would give them this and that answer, thus and so I have said in
thy matters, and thus and so was I answered the Emperor having intelligence of
his vilianye, caused a foster to request him to move the Emperor in a certain
case, which he promised nothing niggardly, for such courtesy cost him nothing:
but all were served with one hand, he as the rest, and the rest as young
courtiours: by which witness Turinus convinced of his guyle before ye prince,
and other his robberies by exact inquisition found out, he commanded him to be
fixed aloft upon a stake, and under him to be set green wood and wett straw,
which being set on fire pestered his nose with a most cruel smoke, that in
short space thratled him, a crier with a loud voice making proclamation, he
that soulde smoke, is smouldered with smoacke.

The morrall of this, is an exhortation to thee, to take pains, not suffising
thee with ye show without matter of substance.

Hymeraeus ye poet writ against Helen, and not long after recanted: such
small persistaunce in thy consaites I would have thee avoid, neither as the
sun the nearer it mounts to the pole, ye slower it moves, so thou, when thou
art gone some degrees nearer learning, grow more sluggish in thy procedinges.

I would not have thee merchandise degrees, as some are accustomed now a days,
but win them by labor, and deserve the^ by learning Iulianus ye Emperor
buying ye empire of the Pretorians, scarce enioied it a xi months, when he
was deraind, and slain, and his wife and only daughter put to exile.

Young Philotimus daunted at his fathers so vehement exhortation, made him with
due reverence this answer.

Too much drought does shred the leaves, and too much moisture chokes the grass

If you had given me no fore-warning, I had not peradventure been so
circumspect, and yet to earnest speech does sometime agarise me. The rude
Sythian had rather hear ye neigh of an horse, then Orpheus his harp, and a
vain fool is he yt had rather have a pl^usible peale of ye populer sort,
deluding them with a counterfeite show, then with desert to be a king within
himself, without this ringing rumor.

If I employ not my endeavor, let me have ye execution of Draco his law, ye
deprived them of life, which would not labor, or be used as the drones of
Hetruria, which were reputed as vacabonds, they condemned to exile, and their
goods to the fiscall.

I do acknoweledg that now or never is the time to be wrought to ye rules of
litterature. If Mithridates had not spent his primetide in study, he had not
perfectly learned 22. sundry languages, and there fore was it well provided
among the Romans yt no man should bear office till he were 30 years old,
alluding to this principle, that his flowring season contained in former
years, should be reteind in attending good discipline: and this have I already
learnd, that though Arist. will not admit a young man to be a scholar of
morrall philosophy, because he can hardly repress his extrauagant affections,
yet he means says my master of young men in manners and not in years.

Well then Philotimus answered his father, since this is the determynation of
thy Imagination, thou shall have a cote cut after thine own pattern, and go
to thy chosen university.

But because as Laelius says, if one should be rapt into the third heaven, and
have a revelation of gods intricate secrets, and had no body to impart the^
too, his holy inspiratio^ would be converted to grief, neither can we repose
our familiaritye, in any man's company, so well as his which is like our
selves, I not will only provide a tutor to ouersee you, but allott you a
companion to drive away tediousnes, yt as Sulpitius and Litonus were wont to
study one thing, play both at one time, and mutually tend their aides each to
other: so he to you and you to him, may enterchaungeblye be abettours to your
studyes, hoorders of your secrets, and ministers of your pastimes. A gentleman
he is of no base blood, neither discrepant to thee in nature, nor different in
years, and thy daily playfellowe Aemelius, of whom being left orphant I am
the gardaunt, to bestow as I will.

Philotimus glad of this fellowship, because of al me^ he did most fix his fancy
on Aemilius, though living together they had many iarrs betwixt them, with
bonnet vaild, and knee bowed, greeted well his fathers purueiaunce.

Of all things they meant to have speedy dispatch, so that philoti. sent to the

Now you shall understand gentlemen, yt as the ripest wits are commonly
infraunchised in all follies, so philotimus sauns all care of his late
protestation, having passed the term of 16. years, was as forward as the
best in acco^plishing his humor.

For as the unicorn is so strong, that he is not taken with any might or power
of hunters, but for to take him a maid is set there he shall come, who opening
her lap, the unicorn leaves all his fearcenes, and laying there on his head
falls asleep, and so is taken and thirled with darts of hunters, as though
nature had given him no armor to defend himself withal: so Philotimus which
was never gind with other parturbation, was by times and too timely, enthralled
with beauty.

This may be a cause of the small a bode in his bliss, the green blade yt
shoots too early is soon bitt with a black frost.

There was a gentlewoma^ by style, but as I have heard and partly guess by her
slippery dealinges, giving no peremptory judgment but by conjecture, a rare
iuewell scraped out of a dunghill, gotten by stealth by the wicked aspect of a
beggarly micher, when her mothers husband, her father by name, could not see
for horns growing over his eyes. but the fault you will say was not in her,
but in her mother, which in shufling the cards shufled in a knave too many This
gentlewoma^ almost of equal age with noble philo. her father being deceased, was

occasion philo. sometimes in company with her, and often conferring her feature

proved himself a carped knihht* and as plato speaks of ye soul which
ascending after death up to the heavens, and meeting with natures semblable to it
self in all respects, stays there, and not before: so she seeing his posse,
corresponde^t to her velle, met him just in ye midway of all his love, gave him
pat for pat, love for love, as long as he would.

Now sir, Philotimus being comd home, and ready to depart the next morning,
sent his page secretlye to his mistress, requesting parley with her before his
journey, ye time he would come, should be when all were at rest, about ten of
the clock in the night. The boy dispatched it roundly, and she granted it
willinglye, yet something nicely, as maidens do their honesty.

Philotimus asking the boy how he had sped, forsooth quoth he, Danger yonder
kepes the door, and mistress Modesty the house. I knocked so softly at ye gates,
as if they had been my grau^dfathers soul, then comes mistress Antigone with
her whispering who is there. I told her who, and with what arraunde. As Soon
as she had certified her mistress, she toulde me her mistress would have you
come, and in stead of green rushes, she would provide a banquet made of three
let is (letters I would say) w, e, ll, which mente by my construction, that
you should be well-come.

Aurelia (for so was this gentlewoman called) made none privy to this counsel
but her woman Antigone, who immediately after these news, prepared by her
mistrisse appointment a very delicate banquet, of such drugs as she had in ye
house. Philotimus at the time prefined, accompanied with his page, repaired to
his castle of health, who being couertlye admitted by Antigone into mistress
Aureliaes chamber, found her sadly sitting upon a bench, with her hand for a
pillow to her heavy head. Philotimus panting for joy of his close entry into
this palace of pleasure of his mistress residence, and wondering at her powting
sadness, spoke in this manner.

All hail my best Aurelia, what mean these doleful dumpes? Is all as you
would, or do you hope for better? and receiving no answer or look of her
again, my mistress quoth he, is in Archimides his contemplation, studying how
to entertain an unbidden guest, and stepying forward, softly seized upon her
arm Aurelia quoth he, what study you on, when you study of nothing?

She shoaring up her eyes, as one newly awaked out of a slu^ber, what
Philotimus quoth she, you are welcome home, I pray God be with you, I am glad
to see you merry, I pray you commend me to all my friends.

Aurelia said Philotimus, is in a waking woman's dream, she dreams of cheese,
and speaks of chalk, speaks of my presence, and craves my absence. But
though I come like ill weather, unsent for, yet farewell frost is not so soon
said. Her answer is like Apollo his Oracle, that may not be assoiled but
with doubtful interpretations, and therefore I will assay to anatomize her
meaning. I am as welcome to her, as water into a ship, and if I will be gone,
Gods blessing be with me shall-be mine alms.

Nay, stay there Philotimus, (answered Aurelia) Juno turned them that troubled
the head of the fountain into frogs, therefore recall your ignoraunte
Paraphrase, neither note so fast in the margent, without better understanding,
least for corrupting the text, some penalty befall you. But seth I mean to
be angry with you, I will show you the cause of all my garboile. Your late
compacted journey, was not so smothered in hugger mugger, but that I had a
little bird, that brought me news of it: and when I heard it, I was aggreued,
as sick as a chick, but much more was I angry, when being in your mouth, (as
Plotina said) you spit me out like a sluttish morsel and being your next
neighbor, and of a little acquaintance, you would not remember all this
vacant time, to let me have notice of your intent, but left me desolate, taking
your flight sodeinly, and now when I have you, you are gone again by this time:

Alas said Philotimus, the bound must bend, if thou do but recount the
contents of my goodwill, which I know thou have capitulated in thy memoryall,
thou will rue the intestine war, which all this while I have endured in my
fancy toward thee, and the duty toward my father: at length I am comd to
ransom my self from evil suspicion, to represent my gratefullnes, and
present my service to thy good consideration.

Philotimus, I suppose, had the virtue of the herb Nepenthes in his phisnomy,
which could sack sorrow, and make his mistrisse as merry as a maid might be,
to whom she answered in this sort.

If any tongue in the world deserve to be gilded, it is thine Philotimus,
for thou can so portcolize thy faults, that if they be black, they are
engrayled with blew, and if they be read, thou make them white, wherein, as
in other properties, so in this you resemblest the fair lilie, which being of a
scarlet coulor within, is in outward aspect as hory as the snow. But I remit
thy crime howsoever or whithersoeuer thou wentest, though it were a feritting
for other men's conies. One dram of courtesy with you, may serve for a whole
pound of discurtesie, I had rather be woonne with an apple, then thou
should say I would be lost with a nutte, a beck of yours, is as good as a
Dieugarde.

Philotimus seeing his mistrisses melanchilie so soon chewed, and disgested,
thus replied. Either age or use has given me might to bear your gibes
(Aurelia) I cannot wish you less then my sovereign princess, you are so
merciful, yet by this that you say, I can turn my self from white to red,
from red to white, you may imagine, I am no chaunglinge, like the brood of
the Cameleon, who can change himself into all coulors, saving red and white.

I will say to you, as one said to Fabius Cato, who being a man little in
stature, was soon angry, and soon pleased. Since your chimney is so small,
you must beware to lay too much wood upon your fire, least your chimney be
always smoaky. There Aurelia made a Colon, bringing in her parenthesis, which
was this. Whereas we have offended each other without desert, let thy Squire
Parmenio allotte what judgment we shall suffer for our passed trespasses,
Philotimus was pleased. Parmenio answered. I thank our Ladies son, that I
am comed to the preferment of a judge, were I so ever, I might perhaps prevent
my destiny, which my fathers last legacy bequeathed unto me, and my coat
armor blazed by the Herauldes, does plainlye prognosticate, for thus say
they. Two trees rampant, with a tree iacant, and a knave pendant, in a green
field, is the badge of a younger brother.

Aurelia does not judge my conditions by my coat, for then I were to base for
this court, but a fox though he have not so gawdye a skin as the Leopard, has
more wit then the speckled fool.

Lord (quoth Aurelia) what a monster art thou, a man in my eyes, and a fox
in my ears? Parmenio held on, not hearing what she said.

If it were the part of a Judge to respect parcialitie, I should percase
remember my masters Evening brawlinges, and Morning whippinges, which many a
time God knows, poor I do feel, hear, and understand. But I would you
wist, Parmenio has emulation for trustines, with Anthony the Orators
servant, who being rackte and torturde to give testimony against his masters
adultery, when he had played fast and louse with a pretty wench, would rather
endure to die, then make an Apocalips of his masters priuities; therefore with
warrantize of my upright dealing, now to the purpose.

For as much as my master has offended you (mistress Aurelia) with his
ungrateful negligence, and you have grieved my master with your sinister
suspicion, whereof the one he has made auriculer confession, and of the other
you are not acquitted, I pronounce, that for pour bypartite sins, both of you,
till to morrow morning at six of the clock, do penance together in a pair of
white sheets.

They both smild at the boys conceit, and Aurelia not able to utter one word
for laughing, faintlye foltered out as follows. O God, O God, O Parmenio,
thou do me so much good I can not abide thee. Alas said he, I hurt you God
thank me, but when coltes cry wyhye, they are commonly lustye. I will not
kick quot^ Aurelia, least I seem to be gauld, but thou art t^o h^sty for a
parish priest, that consummates a marriage, before the banes be asked, and
bringes us to bed, before we be married.

Their banquet was ready, the board covered, and Parmenio and Antigone stood
in stead of sewer and seruitours. Parmenio seeing the night overtake them, and
acquainted with his masters hamour, who had rather have been talkinge with
Aurelia, then feeding of these viaundes, made so great have that he spild a
broth on her gown. Very well said Philotimus, so could I have done my self.
Me thinks you should, said the boy, when I do it before you. Aurelia smiled
at his answer, and pacifying his master, toulde him that since the effect
was so good to make them sport, the cause must deserve no reprehension. Nay,
quoth Parmenio, you have no honesty in your victuals. No, (answered Aurelia)
frowning at it, how than? No forsooth quoth the boy, never an herb called
honesty. Thus they merely past the while, the page willing to cheer them with
jesting.

But my young Lady was no Epicure at her meat, still staring in Philotimus his
face, and ministering meruailes to her self of that, which was no novelty for
her to view.

Parmenio marking how she played bo-peepe, spoke to his master in this sort.
If mistress Aurelia feed on your phismony song, as she does this night, she
will make you look with a pair of Lenton cheeks: whether it be for sparinge
of her cates or no^, I know not, but all this night she has neither other
sustenaunce nor taste, but such as come from your amorous countenance, to her
gloting eyes.

I have heard say Aurelia, that the nourishmente is always like the body
nourished, and if it be so, I hope there is some sympathy betwixt his looks
and my liking.

Par. In-deed that which nourishes, turns into the substance of that which
is nourished, and therefore I doubt not, but anon my master will find the way
into your little substance.


Aur. I trow nay, Philotimus will not do so, he is my best child.


Par. When you have borne him, and brought him forth, you may lawfully call
him child, and he you mother.

His master thanked him that he was so diligente a broker for his pleasure.


Par. It is not for disability in you, and that which I say, you must seal,
wherein stands the virtue. Princes you know in parliament houses have their
speakers, to declare their pleasures, and case themselves. If you have a letter
of good tidinges, it is no matter what varlet be the carrier, so you know his
hand who has signed it and sent it. mistress Aurelia takes no keep how great a
bragger I be, so you prove your self a valiant rider.


Philot. had done his deuoire, Aurelia was sufficed, and ye table taken up,
Antig. and Parmen, cabbished together into a bye lobby where they refreshed
themselves with the relics of their reuersion, and Parmenio played his reakes,
whose short colloquy you shall hear seiunctly.


Par. Now mistress Antigone, you have labored till you sweat, and I have
toyled till I am dry, therefore if thou be at leisure, I will be so bold as
drink to you, if you will give me leave.


Ant. I am not at leisure to give the leave, but take thee thy choice of a
thousand thanks.


Par. I have a su^^e to you, but that I fear the repulse, and a whelpe that
first does miss of his game, does never after prove worth an haw.


Ant. A Butchers curre does never alter his nature, as for water Spanyels, we
have no need of them in this place.


Par. If you have learnd the eight liberal science. I mean edgging. I will
set a spoke to your ^^gge Antigone, but good my wench, ^ooke with thine eyes,
and pity wi^h thine h^rt.


O quoth Antigone, I love the terribly, but it is a chief point of art to
dissemble art. I love thee well, but I trust in God thou shall never know it.
Parmenio seeing himself thus ridden, g^ue her a love tap upon the cneeke, as
though he would have bette her for mocking him, I will beat the bush quoth
he, and then peradventure I shall catch the bird: for whips and fair words
are the best to win women.

Antig. Indeed, thou art more like a stale to deceive women, then aproper man
to win them by might, yet such battes will scare the foul away.


But there rest they in their meriment till the next morning, and return we
to Philotimus, who is courting his mistress, recording oulde love without
vpbraiding, and requesting a contracte without resistaunce.

Aurelia although she were not inferior to him in fancy, yet did she doubt
though she were young, that youth would turn with the wind, for that as
Tully says, they love ardore quodam adolescentulum, and stood aloof in
denying his suite, insomuch that Philotimus very pensiuely protested she killed
him with obstinacy, their conference I have described dialogue wise.

Aur. Has not she action that kills, and he passion that is killed? How can it
then be, that when as he that loves does work, and ^he that is loved suffers,
she should kill him?


Philot. Nay, rather he that loves suffers, and she works.


Aur. But has not he free choice, to love or not to love?


Phil. He has.


Aur. Then he killes himself that loves.


Phil. The woman killes not because she is loved, but because she loves not
again. For whosoever may save, and will not, kills. O Aurelia, be not so
stern to him that has elected that and none else for his felicity, lest
Nimesis ye goddess, who wreakes all injuries with sharp reuengment, take my
cause in hand, and plague thee, though she help not me.


Aur. What would thou have me say?


Phil. But three words, that as I am thine, so thou art mine for ever.


Aur. This is a short song, with a long Epiphonema.

A matter of marriage requires deliberation.


Phil. But I have deliberated too long.


Aur. It is Love, not Wisdom, makes you think thus, and Love is blind.


Phil. No, no, my love sees, for thou do not seem such a one, because I love
thee, but I love thee, because thou art such a me.


Aur. Thou art not well acquainted with my manners, if thou ^addest worn the
shoe, thou might judge where it would hurt ^hee. Virginity is a fair
thing. Is it not a better sight to see a fresh Rose in the stalk, then
withered in a Gentleman's ^a^^^?


Phil. Had you rather have nothing but flowers in your orcharde, or the flowers
falling of, have your Trees swelle with apples?


Aur. But chastity is a good thing.


Phil. Therefore would I marry a chaste maid, and it may be a time we shall live
chaste, in the mean time we must learn it: or we cannot attain to all at the
first.


Aur. What? get virginity with violating of it?


Phil. Yea, why not? We learn to abtaine from wine by drinking little and
little. If virginity be so laudable, the^ it is not lawful to marry, but say
not so Aurelia, least like an unkind ^i^d, ye defoile thine own nest, accuse
thy mother of dishonesty, and thy self of basterdie.


Aur. I shall lose my liberty.


Phil. Nay, thou shall be Queen, and I King, and we will govern our family.
Is not your mind tied to your body, yet would it not be loosed, because it is
there willingly.


Aurelia desirous to shift of this talk, found every crafty hoal^ to thrust
her head in.

But it may be (quoth she) we shall be barren.

Phil. As though you are not so now. And it is better to live, to die, then
never to live at all: If we have joy, it shall be double, if grief, thou shall
beat but a part.


Aur. Our children may miscarry.


Phil. That lies in us: for if we be good, they will be good. Tigres are not
borne of lambs, nor doves of ravens, nor had children of parents that give
good example.


Aur. These things are hard.


Phil. Because they are good, and that is the cause that you are so hard,
because you are so fair.


Aur. Alas Philotimus, why do we reckon our chickens before they be hatched,
and trouble our selves about these matters, which may time enough be talked on
seven years hence? We are too little, and to young, for the delights of
marriage.


Phil. Tush Aurelia, a short sack has a wide mouth, and young kids lether
will stretch, and well thou know, a woman without a man, is like a ship
without a stearne, when I have left my affection here at home, it will be a cause
I shall sit at my study, not wandering abroad with wavering wool gathering
thoughts. And as the Dromeda, which runs a hundredth miles or more a day,
can long continue his course and running withowten meat, and after his
tedious iourneyes contents himself with brousing on the date tree, and a few
stones thereof: so can I endure pains all these our vnmellowed years, in hope
to possess thee as mine hire.


Aur. Thou art not like the Crocodile that wantes his tongue. Phil. But I am
like him in this, yt I would live either by water or land for enjoy the. I
think thou have the precious stone Sardonix, who has the propertye to make
one lowly and humble in their doings as thou art. which way look thou Aur.
not affording me a good countenance? my I read is dough, if ye be of the
nature of the bird Kalader, which when a man has been long s^ck, if he shall
dye turns a away her face from him, and if he shall escape death, streth her
sight upon him and behouldeth him cheerfully?


Aur. tell me this, would you have me married to a dead man as you are?


Phi. The herb panace can revive the dead: the touch of the saphyre can rid the
diseased of the grievous sore the Carbuncle: Orphens his harp raised Euridice
his wife from death: and your ^^^ning I know in this point will be as current as
neds, so your ^^nsent be not awanting. I give you the supremasie of my soul
use it as you list.


Aur: yea Philotimus the Eagle is called queen of all other birds, yet she
stirth from the little kind of swallowe which is all black, not daring to fall
on her pray till the swallowe fly abroad, and on the contrary, our hollow
father the Pope is cleped the servant of servant, yet he rules the roast
within most parts of Christendom: I now am misteris and you servant, this
order will be inuerted if we covenant th^^.

Nevertheless taking this frivolous talk for firm truth of your loyaltye and

grounded upon long continuance, if it some time chance to quayle it will
spring again in the same for me, I am content to yield my self a vassal to
your desire,


Phil. Fie pleasure, fie, thou cloyest the with delyghte. Now Priams sone
give plane, thy belens how is stainde, O Troylus weep no more, fair Coes^d
thine is loth^ye soul. Nor Hercules thou haste cause to vaunt for thy sweet

lest proper dart at sight of her should wound his breast. O Gods, and fates, and
rolling heavens, can I my loves dear love forget? God knows I wish I could
requite, but God confound if I forget.

I usurp any maistrie over thee? nay if thou will so far debase thy self as
make me thy fellow we will bear the bridle euenly with equal peyz.

Aur Pertinax in beginning of his reign thinking that the heavens were a mete
burden for none but Atlas, nor the imperial crown a congruent garland to none
but to those of divine excellency, defect whereof he bewatled in him self,
and the surplusage whereof he admired in others, would have instauled the^
Consul Glabrion in ye empire, and have placed him in the chayce of estate. In
whose refusal Glabrion persisting answered him thus. Thy humble humility
which thou showest pertinax, in the lack of merit of the Empire, makes thee of
deserving sufficient to be invested in the Empire.

Neither must I insult over thy subjection, but in lieu thereof, give thee a
counterpaine of my heart, neither is it proper to our sex to rule, but a
property in yours to proffer service, and because it should not stink for
stalenes, you leave it of at day of your marriage.

A picture portrayed in wax shows as fair as one engraven in marble, but
continewes not so long, thorns bear to the sight as fair flowers as the
geliflower, Crystal seems a precious stone till it come to hammering, and it
may be, though I hope better, that this love of yours is good for a flight, and
then casts of her bells,


Phil. use not, as you muse, and good enough. It is no shame to luke a fall,
but a shameful fall to lie long. It grieves me not that thou use objections,
but gauls me to the heart that thou will believe nothing. I am no master to
clock ill meaning in ^ire words, but thou do turn me to a manner to loose
my tongue for very sorrow.

Yet will I be as ready to spend my spirits in thy service, as am dull to spare
my speech in mine own defense. In hostag^ whereof, here take my heart in habit
of my hand, to bestow at your pleasure, but if you will give it hospitallity
in your breast, I will do as much for you in the like matter, she grasping his
hand, he offered to kiss her, which she r^f^sed. It is an evil cold (quoth
he) that may not lick his own fingers.


Aur. I may not kiss, or I must keep my virginity sound for you.


Phi. Does k^ssing impair virginity.


Aur. I pray you would you have me to give kisses to other men?


Phi. No, keep my kisses for me.


Aur. You boar me in hand, that the greatest part of your heart is in my
custodye. If you lay lips to lips, it may peradventure all flitt into me.


Philotimus did not day the matter, but used that force which uses to be
welcome to all women, when they make resistaunce as though they would not be
ouercomed.

Both parties pleased, many an interchaungable oath they took, that sooner
Zanthus should recule, sooner Hearts should feed in the air, sooner Eagles
fly the doves, then they would part wtth ought but death, whilst any
Grasshopper should feed on dew, whilst any Be should seed on time, whilst
any Turtles love their mates, they would not faint in faith, and after death
surumours heart should still concomitate corses ghost, and with these words
briefly concluded all their mistrust.

Aur. When love is 'cloyed, the royle at grass may run, I seat.


Phil. When hoof is hole, the hackney still is hired, thou know.


Aur. When corn is sold the market clean is done.


Phil. The Chapmans daily need will stul uphold the market.


Now nights dark mantle began to avail, the day bright star, dan Lucifer
icleeped, by office forerunner to Radiant Phoebus bedecked the sky, and none
but it. Now was the dolinge hour comd, wherein Philotimus must depart, for
fear of discryinge.

How often did they pray the slyding Night, so linger not a while? How often
did they curse the lyght-some lyefe Aurora, for her quick return? saying that
it was casye to know the cause of her earelye rysinge, because she lay with
the old man Tithonus, but if her lover Cephalus might have been her
contemporant, she had not been so hastye.

Now like the warblinge lark or other ayerye chyrpinge byrd, which all the
night is tongue tied, and descants it at sight of dewye morninge, so she
which all the night was as dayntye of a word, as Democrytus of a tear sunge
in the morninge a song of three parts, her tongue auouchinge permanent
constancye, her eyes bemoninge their hastye partinge, her hands beatinge her
tender breast, in remembrance of long sequestratio^.

She which before seemed to warm her self at Philotimus his fire, and learn
love of him, now her smoulderinge heat burst out to open flame, she whose
conduite seemed to be stopped or pipes cut of, now gushed out into seas of
tears, those hands that thrust him back before, begriped his hands, embraced
his corpse, and bound her fast for ay.

Aur. Thy heart I fear me is yet a wielidng, but if thou will plant it, as thou
have professed, within my breast, do not doubt but such grafts of grace will
grow of it against thy return, that for every corn thou shall reap an eere
of fifty grains. My good Philotimus, remember her that cannot forget thee,
think of her in absence, who will have thee and thy personage imprinted in
presence.


Phi. Aurelia, though I were Caluitius f ye orgot the names of his most
familiar acquaintance, or more forgetful then the doterd Messala, who forgot
his own name, yet cannot the remembrance of the be bloted out. thou shall be
my speculation, my meditation, my familiar with whom I will conferr, my joy in
prosperyty, my solace in adversity, only thou and ever thou, my sweetest
Aurelia.


There he gave her a ring, in which was ingraued the precious stone ye
Emeraulde, which might make her mind-fal of him, and recreate her sight with
the green gloss she in guerdon, bestowed on him an Amatist, whose force was
to drive away al evil thoughts, and sharpen his understanding at his book:
when he beheld the Amatist, I think, quoth he, the force of this Amatist has
kept you wakinge all this night. for such operation also do Lapidaries ascribe
to it.

Aur. philotimus She had not need to sleep, that wakes a quick corse, lest

Phil, Mine own Aurelia. whose joy I was, whose pleasant sweet conceyts are

The poor gentlewoman not able to wreste out one word for weepinge (for where
the fire is most the flame is least) At length bespake thine ever Aurelia,
or never mine own, wysheth thee better fare; then I have at this present, and
my self greater care, if it were for thy pleasure.

Thus thrice he took his leave, and touched the chamber door, and thrice he
did return, and took his leave again, and when he parted, Lord loathe he
parted, and sometime stood, and sametime went, and still looked back to passed
joys.

Permenio (quoth he) how might I bear this cheek yet of a prince and not a
pawn, and that is my comfort? a thowsande times I curse the hour, wherein I
went my saint to se, and eke ten thowsande times I bless the stoure, wherein
I saw so fair a sight as she now I have her, but I may not enjoy hertell me
thy counsel permenio.

Par. Is this your inuectiue against Venus, and your promise to Palls, that
latelye you made to your father, you would serve well in stead of a
wethercock, to show a man in what coost the wind stands, or else I hope you
will prove a good Astronomer at your study, you are so wetherwise, turninge
your tail into every wind.


phil. permenio, thou neither know me nor my love, nor the meaning of my
choice, Venus is changed, then why may not I? Venus is not Venus, my Aurelia
is Venus whom I will worship and adore as the primate of all Goddesses. my
fathers propose was, whether I would leave all other things and wholye

swaye, which then I denied neither yet do repent. For neither will I abandon
pallas, to be an hunger-staruen slave of Venus, neither can I do Pallas her
right homage, unless my Venus, the golden Aurelia vouchsafe to be my guide.

Venus might blush if she were not impudent, and Juno be ashamed but that she
is a Queen, to see or hear Aurelia named.

The graces were not gracious: if they wanted Aurelia, neither beautye worth
praising, if she were away

Triumph then you Graces, that you have such a subject, and vaunt no more
Venus, but glory in thy Lady, she is the Son to every gallant wight, the
rest but twinclynge stars to wanderers in the night, for her Dame Nature
howles and weeps because the moulde that cast her shape is lost and gone, nor
ever can the like be framed again.

Such a pearl in a blackamoores ear would make him whytt. Such a paragon
would cause Galen shake hands with Chastytye, such an Alexis would make
Coridon go madd. One word and then no more, she dead, the world were done.


Par. I have heard that a club bound about with olive is a good cote armor,
because a violent weapon is ensigned with a mark of peace: aunswerable to this
say I, that a little infirmity covered with warines is very tolerable. One in
Bizantio, which now is called Constantinople, having an vrcheon, who by nature

denn) would fore-warne his neighbors of storms, from what costs they came, so

he came bye, he was reputed half a prophet: you have redd of the simple

thought wise, bought certain slaves boundmen, whereof some could say Homers

should rap them out, which made him be thought learned among his gestes, who

late match, (though you can hardlye serve Gad and Mammon but be bewraide) yet
you may study on pallas and practice on Aurelia, and never be perceived.


Phil. I remember one askinge Hannibal, what his purpose was to do the next

back knew his intent, he would disrobe himself and burn it, and I warrant

Par. I? as close as a close stool.


phil. Fo parmenio, I smell thy lie stink hither have not I heard chee swear

par. That perjury was for mine honesty. and this fidelity is for my
alleageaunce toward you, hope well and have well.


philotimus at these words took truce with care and put on his holy day look

Here I cannot pretermit to insert the complaynt of the Lady Castibula^, which
^hee had concealed before her sons face, and closelye in her chamber

Ah thrice unfortunate is she, tha^ ^^nce anhappie mother, and better it is be
to a barren covert barron, then a fertile mother of toward children: the one
lives in hope to shake of shame in time, the other dies in doubt to lose ye
fruition of her wombs fruit if the boy should be sick, which Jesus
forefende, can I be there too comfort him with mine own brothes, or see what
confections his physicians do minister? or if the youth want money, which is
youths wont, can I be ready with a fresh supplye to give a restoritye to

any such matter I shall send him angels which were no messengers this
seven years, who I doubt not but will fly without any wings.

But leave we the pensive mother, whose sonns sure saftye was all her thought,
and whose care of her son was sometime comfort to him, not by the cause, but
by the effect.

Philotimus dreaminge on his last nights work, ryd all the day long heavier

by land, but they must imbarke themselves, and launch it out by water, thus
did he leeme to be conueyde: in shipe fraught with remembrance of pleasure
past, with scaldinge sighs for want of gale, and steadfast hope that was his
sail.

The question was this, whether it went botter to take pains in youth
afterward to enjoy pleasure, or first to reueil him in the leaze of liberty,
and in age be pinchte for his former sins.

The defense of the first part was given to Plulotimus, of the second to

I feel my head so far (...) it this da^^ neither to please my self with

The hot stomach of a goose eats watery meats, and tastes no laurel, the
Peacock when he has lost his tail, hides himself for shame, till it grow
again, and I having for the time lost my best ornament, I mean some odd
foolish crankatiue conceited humor, and burning within, as cold without as a
clock, love neither this morning to confer with company, neither to taste any
meat, unless sadness be the sauce. Notwithstanding knowing that the crying of
the bird Fulica in the morning, betokens some tempest to ensue, and that the
eating of an Hearts flesh in the morninge, augmentes man's life, I will not
yield to sorrows summons, lest it be an Argument of my imminent ill fortune,
but be as hartye as I may, for the procurement of my solace.

And so much the less I need to doubt, by how much the more I am gardayne of
the truth. For truth is like a friend which in most cruel encounters of his
friends misfortune, does then most friendly bewray himself, or like Hidraes
heads, which the more they were coped, the more they grew, although for a
time the Sun may be shadowed with a cloud, the Fire may lie raked in ashes,
and Truth may be smouldered with malice, or dared with houering Fraud.

My friendly foe Aemilius, I will not use many words to aggrauate much matter,
but comprise as much matter in as few words compyled, as shall support and
prove a sentence to thee, already approved of all.

I once red these words, of the great matron Mesia Phoenicia, in l^tters she
writ to the sacred Senate, touching her Nephewe Heliogabulus. Many times age
does bring forth fruit, where reason did not yield so much as leaves. But I
say, never shall eld have leaves of lives pleasure, where they do not blossom
of the fruit of youths pains. Rapes and Turnups shall be sweet if their
seeds be soaken in honey water, before they be sown: but crooked benumd age
shall wast in woes, where the platform and foundation of ouergrowen years,
were not grounded with sour travail.

Vices may vanish, but their effects cannot perish, the felon may by hap be
dismissed from the rope, but the burnt in his hand must by law show him
guiltye, though distemperate harebraine youth escape sensual surfettinges of
wine and women, (whereof Cicero says that more cast their last gasp, then dye
by course of nature) yet in after process they bear the badge of beggary, eat
the meat of sorrow, sup the water of affliction, suffer hunger as a Lion, seek
their pray as a Lion, ramp abroad like a Lion, and fade with famishment, as
seldom does the Lion.

At a feast, the first service is of grosser meat, and the delicate eats
reserved till the last. When you break a colte, you first beat him for, his
wildnes, and afterward being weldy do cherish your hobby.

Though labor be loathsome, yet is it wholesome. Rue is most bitter, yet being
eaten fasting it cleares the eyesight. ^ Olive is not sweet, yet a plant of it
set, and a year or two after an impe of a Rose-tree grafted in it, it will
bring forth a sweet green Rose at Christmas. Brimstone and Veruaine are no
honey, yet bind them to thine hand or arm, and thou shalt never have the
crampe.

It is not with the pleasures of youth, as it is with the Juniper tree, which
will never putrify or stink, and has his branches continually grene.

In vear, the husbandmen lop their trees, to the intent that afterward they may
grow the better: so let youth cut of his disordinate affections, that
afterward he may prosper, and florishe more gloriously.

The plowmans toil has hope that makes him till: Love has a sauce that makes

I speak of my theme, as Aristides spoke of profit. For Themistocles on a
time sainge that her had very profitable counsel for the weal bublick, but
such as must be privately discussed on, not in open audience, the Athenians
gave him Aristides with whom to confer. Themistocles told him that the navy of
the Lacedaemonians which was conveyed to Githeus, might be closelye burnt, which
done, the force of the Lacedaemonians to be broken, and their hearts
discomfited. Aristides returninge with great expectation to the council
house, answered, that Themistocles his counsel was very commodious, but
withal dishoneste, whereat the Athenians not reputinge that profitable, which
was not honest, relected his counsel not having heard it.

My sentence is, that the pleasure which is not adjacent to virtue (as no
youthful pleasure almost can be) is to be fled and not followed, rather
refusinge her follies, then chusinge her defense.

When there is intestine war betwixt Reason which by nature is crowned with a

It is not the outward munition of a well it need city, or the conjunction
of compacted houses, that makes a good incorporate commonwealth: for then
should the whole corporation of Peloponnesus, have been encompassed with one
wall, and some cities there be so great and huge (as Babylon, whereof the
third part knew not, til two parts of ye city were sacked and captiuated) yt
they cannot possibly be reduced to a good common weal, No more (good Aemilius)
is it outward sensitive voluptuousnes, that inuestes a man with the type of
true happiness, or ennobles his name with any dignity.

How much better were it for us to do as M. Seius, which sparing from his

A mirror whereof may Bargulus be, the thief in Illyria, of whose toil and

There is a certain Italian history, ye tenor whereof is this: a gentlewoma^
on amoured of a young gentleman, whose garish gledi^e persuade a yelding bent of
will, sought with good courage to accompany and court her, in place where he
might plead with out other judge then her self* her neighbor feets, and other
vulgar tongues, so tatled of their privy conuenticles, that her brethren forth
with knew the counsel, which they were not called to the young gentleman on a
time in quiet well ycought (a queint commodity as he thought) a lonely with
his misteris, her brethren, as the harm of mishap would, took him napping as
Mos^ did his mare, I will not say between my ladies legs, they with trenchant
blades, bolne with ire, lokinge bigg, like Marquesses of all beef, with
griefull eine, clinging about him, protested, they would bowl his bellye, if
he did not confine his knavery with other bonds: but to this composition they
came, that if he would condiscende to lie a whole year within an old rotten
hagg (who was more like to consume with canker, then pleasure a gentleman) and
do to her as he would to their sister, he should have egress and regress with
his gentlewoman, and the choice should be his, with whom he would first begin.
When my youth hard this, glad he was of the one part, but which should be in
the first front he knew not, so shiuering fear flitt too and fro among his
flushing veins.

The Queen of a compame in a merry meting of gallants for disporte, having
this question propounded, gave resolution, that it was better wisdom to begin
with his misteris. and so he should be sure of his joy: and be better armed to
endure the groaning crone, partly cloied with former pleasure, partly with
remembrance of his first customer, and who knows said she whether he shall
live a year or no.

Neither is the drift of my devise directed to an Apology of elds unsavory

Heavy is their happiness, unless they be happy because they are so persuaded

mourning Tons, constituting their felicity in vain speculations. How many

of his spheres. Do not you think that if Isocrates his Soul might rise

It is disputed of all the Schoolmen in Philosophy, and of Arist their

not been conducted thither by ye Senses. Of thi^ I gather, that your solid
pleasure which you do limitte in the minds muses is first derived from the

Now you see how old ages senses be be-dimmed, in so much that they have no
taste of their meat and drink, unless as it is in Zenophons symposion,
rorantia et minuta pocula do now and than relieve them, which taste of theirs,

Their eyes do look by a pair of spectacles, like one through a cazement, so

If it convey such inconvenience with it, as the overthrow of towns, and
cities, it is better to gorge our selves with the society of it in the
firstlinges of our days, when we have no charge of importance committed unto
us, then in riper years, when our own credit and country commodity stands
us more upon, to have impediments by her corrupting. For there is no man says
Seneca, but in one part of his age, if he be not intercepted by unseasonable
death, he feles the battery of his perturbations.

Old men are accustomed with great self love, to repeat youths pleasure, as
a recreation to repress sorrows, and abandon pensiuenes. Lovers (says the
Italian Castalio) are solaced with lookinge up to the chamber window, whence
of yore they have seen their desired Saints, and old cramped sires in their
stoop gallant age, when the Medium can not be a mean to receive ye object,
because the Organon of their sense is quite perished, they feed and nourish
themselves with the recourse of those blooming days, when in youth red h^rring
was a king: in this property, like Tymon of Athens, which being reviled abroad
for his insatiable nature, would at his return home open his cheste where his
old gold lay, and comforting himself with the sight of yt set it at his
heart, and cast their words at his heels. Except you think every man as

rope as he made it, never lookinge behind him.

It is said, that an Orator must begin well and end well, because these two
parts do tarry best in the Auditors memory, though he enterlace the midst
with baser matter, but you quite forgetting the precepts of your own faculty,
wind up your Oration with a palpable error, inioyning youth not to read
Poems, or gaze on pictures, or be present at Comodies, till his steps be
stayed with ripe judgment, and faded follies: when as in very deed, we are
taught of the best authors to acquaint us in our first elements with such
pretty slight toys, as Aesops Fables, and Hesiodus his, which stir our
capacities to a readiness, and our manners to a conformity.

Auaunt therefore with this thy drouping sauadgenes, which thou prescribest
youth, as a mediation to felicity. Wherein thy reioicing is like his, which
being on the gallows with a rope about his neck, bad them have him commended
to all his friends, and desire them to be glad of his welfare, for he was gone
to heaven in an halter.

If the persuasion of these few words, have brought you to a comprimise, I
have said enough: if not, whole volumes will not retort your stiff neck, to
bring you to my bent. Though I should weary you and my self, with an huddle of
more words, yet could I be no more effectual, then I have been in these. A
good Painter shadoweth a great counterfeit in a small room.

My hope is therefore, that as the river Zanthus turned the fleeces of all the
sheep that drank of it into yellow, of what color before soever they were,
or as Parisatis left of to love King Artazerxes, when he saw ye beauty of
Cyrus: so you repeating my reasons after the way of a retreat, wilt be converted
to an other opinion, loving thy self well, but the truth better, resembling
Bellerophon, who being entertained of Praetus when he left A^gos for slaying of
Bellerus, would not at the entisement of Antea, Praetus his wife, defile his
bed, more regarding his honesty then his ease, and thinking it villainy, so to
counteruaile curtesy, though it were her own entreaty.

Here be a bundle of reasons quoth Philotimus, gathered on a heap like an
vrtchin under an apple tree, in which thou have the property of an artificial
liar, I mean a good memory Aemilius, (for so goes the proverb) a good liar
must he memoratiue.

Parrhasius painted an erected statyre, and on the top thereof a Partridge, so
lively, that other Partridges pende up in a cage a little by it, would often
conte^d to fly to it, as though it had been a live bird, to the great wonder of
all men. Upon which occasion, Pasihasius marking the art of the bird so much to
be meruailed at, that it abated the excellency of the pillar, got license to
take it down: So may I say, that he which shall hear any one part of thy
Gration, will not on the contrary desire to hear any more, neither need search
any further to find out absurdities.

Trust me Aemilius, I should think him that could persuade with these reasons
of thine, to be instilled with some of Perseus his iugling Divinity, that
turned Cepheus into a stone, or him that would be persuaded, to be as foolish
as Phineus King of Archadie, who at the prouokement of his second wife, put
out the eyes of his first wives children.

I am not so austere as the Stoickes, to deprive a man of all affections. I do
disanull the Lacones, whom Arist. 8, pol. cap. 4. reproues, for bringing up
their children too hardly, with too much pains and trauaill, that made them
deuoide of all humanity, and hardened their hearts with blood suckyng tyranny.
I praise not those labor some and illiberall arts, whom Arist. calls
Vanausous and mistharnicas 8. pol. cap. 2. and Tully sordidas and mechanicas.
1. off. wherewith men's bodies are so^lde and made filthye. I name none, because
Satyrs and open nippinges of imperfections, which in old time, when Lucilius
and his crewe writ, was lawful in Enterludes and writings, to the shame of
the offenders, and the affrighting of others is abrogated, and they that now a
days are in most authority, and bear almost the greatest countinaunce among
us, scrape up their wealth, as a Cock does the corn out of a dunghill, and win
their renown be such occupations, yet there was an ancient law in Rome, that
a Miller, a Smith, a Baker, and a point maker, might not be a Senator, I do
not commend the violent excercises of those champernours who although they are
made stranger by their practices (as Quintilian says) lib. 12 Yet is their
natural growth prevented, abhorring from the likelihood and form of men.

I like not the custom of ye Carthaginians, by which they wore so many ringes
in their chains, in sign of honor, as they had been at victorious
atchieuements, nor yt of the Scithians, whtch prohibited any one to drink of
their festival quaffing cup, that was troled upon holidays, but him that had
slain some enemy, Nor that of the men Iberi, which provided so many ensigns
to be erected by the sepulcher of the buried, as they had killed men. The
custom I say of none of all these should be current in my kingdom.

For the cruelty of the sword (be it spoken by the patience of all ruffians) is
not to be ballaunced with the honor of peace. In deed Sipio wan fame in
beating down cities, but Cato Censorius got immortal memory in reforming the
peoples ma^ners by his sharp penalties. Although Pausanias and Lysander
enlarged their country territories by valor, yet were they not comparable to
Lycurgus his laws, from whence as from an Oracle, the first devise of their
enterprises, and the discipline of their manners were diligently fetched.
Neither was M. Scaurus a civil magistrate, inferior in public profit, to C.
Marius a warlicke captain, nor Q. Catulus an officer of peace, to Cn. Pompeius
a professed soldier.

I as far dislike barbarousnes in living for defect, as pampred wantones for
excess. Therefore thy misunderstanding, is as foolish as his litterall
paraphrase, that reading he must pull out his eye if it offended him, when it
was a little bloudshot plucked it out of his head, supposing if he did not so,
that he should go to hell fire

Whereas thou reasoneest, that right pleasure consists in external senses,
because they are the conueyers, and as it were the carriers of it to the
internal parts, thy proof is as bad as Ulysses hi^s in the strife betwixt
Ajax and him for Achilles his armor. There ^e lays claim that not only the
title of the armor is claimable, but that the praise of all Achilles his
prowess, is proper to him, because by his despatch ye armor was wrought of
Vulcane and brought to Achilles, and for that by his wisdom, Achilles was
brought to battle, as who should say, there were a care in the cart or the
horse, that should bring the monye and other necessaries from thy father, and
that they for the carriage deserve the thanks of thy maintenance.

Thou preachest a goodly spell, to infringe ye interdiction of youth from
hearing of plays, wherein the intent of thy speech, and the event of thy
speed, may well couple the in a comparison with that witles courtier in
Lodouicus his court, the eleventh french king of that name.

for so it chanced, that Lodouick being troubled with hurly burlies at home,
removed his court to Burgundy, where by hap in his hunting he fell acquainted
with one Conon, a silie clown of those quarters, to whose house (as great
princes take much pleasure in such simple souls) he straying from his company
would often resort and eat rapes. His insurrections appeased in process, he
returned home, Conons wife was earnest with her husband (hoping thereby for
reward) to present the king with some rapes, in remembrance of old
acquaintance. Conon condtscended, took his staff, and filled his wallet: but
being hungry by the way, eat all his rapes saving one, which coming to the
court, and waiting where the king should pass by, he presented to his maiesti.
The noble king acknowledged his old host, took his gift well in worth, and
commanded it to be kept among his best jewels, and him to dine at his own
table, and perceiuinge after dinner that Conon wished to be walkinge homeward
to Sislye his wife, he gave him a thousand crowns in lieu of his rape.

An odd gentleman about the court, seeing so base a present repaid with so
ample recompense, thinking with a shift of a another gift to obtain the like
gain in coosning the king, bestowed on him a great horse of no small
valuation, imagininge that if the king requited the rapes with a M. crowns, he
should be enriched with great possessions. His highness perceiving the shaded
drift, called a counsel of diverse noble men and gentlemen about him, and
seriously consulted with them, as of an importa^t matter, what he might bestow
on this courteous gentleman, to make him restitution with amendment* One
answered this, another that, the gentleman held in hope of goulden dales. At
length, the king rounded one in his ear, that he should set him the rape which
lay fair wrapped up in a piece of velvet in his closet which brought, the king
gave it the gentleman, tellings him there was a jewel of a thousand crowns
weight, in consideration of his horse: my joyful gentleman with knee bent, and
hand kissed, and ten thousand humble thanks to his noble lieg Lord, received
the reward* thereupon he departed the presence, longing to unfold the velvet,
where he looked for mounteines of mines, which as we say, brought forth
nothing else but a sielie dragled mouse, a worthles poor withered rape. Thus
he that with his craft would needs be an ingrosser of great merchandise, was
himself forestalled of his market, deluded of the king, and deryded of the
whole court. So Aemilius, thy words and good proofs agree together like harp
and harrowe, thou meanest to kill me, and set a dagger to thine own
throat. Thou alledgest Esop, and Hesiodus to the confutatton of me, and they
turn contrary to thy self confusion. I do not gaine-saye, but honest poeams
and fables are lightsome and lawful to every studious youth, and therefore
says Aeschines, and semblably Cicero in his oration for S.R. Amerinus, that
such poetry was fained to frame our fashions. The virtues of which being
praised, is a percing dispraise to these bawdy commodies, more noisome to their
auditorye then brothel^ ^tewes, unless to such, as have wearied wantonness with
experience, having as well will to leave the had, as they have wit to choose the
good.

Thou do attribute so much to senses, that as Alberna, for wishing all her
children might be like in color to ye crows (such love she bore to black)
brought forth ravens in her pr^gnancie, so thou for misusing the heavenly minds
delight, and yelding all to sense, art clean deuoid of all understanding,
which comes from the mind, and art led only by sense, like a brute beast.

But meaning no more at this time to build Castles in the air, nor wast my
words to a deaf man, Harpocrates the god of silence, a god to whom I have
ever done ear this, and will do ever my bounden homage, shall convince thy
Comus, the god of intemperancie, of hateful viciousnes in words, and hurtful
loosenes in life.

It makes my heart bleed to see thee so wise to wickedness, fearing thy fall,
which I will not divine, and devising ye means to fear thee from falling For
me to relinquish such apparent evidence and lean to thy bastardly reasons,
were to leave the hal and come to the kitchen.

I promise you (quoth Aemilius) I do not doubt but by Evening that we come
to our lodginge, your loftye stomach now haughty, then hungry, will be glad
to crepe from the hall to the kitchen, and this angry bleeding with the pearce
of my propositions, and with your own maliciousnes, for the grief of your
overthrow, will be well inoughe staunched with the leg of a Rabbet.

It fares with me, as it did with the estate of king Onomaus, to whom it was
truly prophesied, that duringe the singlenes of of his daughter, he should live
and reign in triumphant prosperity, but most vnluckely, as soon as she
married should be deprived of kingdom and life: so I, whiles my arguments were
in my own hands, was by any man's Judgment a patron of verity, but as soon
as they were coupled to your constructions, I streightwaie was by your criminal
collation, in substance superficial, and in opinion erroneous.

An Oracle pronounced to Pelias, when he should see one barefooted do rites to
his fathers ghost, then he should stand in danger of death, and I meeting with
a naked man shall seem by his brauing to be overcome.

Whereas you burden me with impotencie of sense, I answer, that I have as
little as an other, and as much as I use: yet thou love me well, that take
me up before I fall, to give me warning of falling.

Notwithstanding, not to halte before a cripple, nor dissemble with my feigned
friend, these thy Arguments resemble Ladies, which are fit for sheets, and
good for nothing But the Devil loves al colliers, and thou selflike reasons of
thine own warping, like the rusticke Shepherd in Maroes Aeglogs, which being
in in love with his less Phillis, preferred the chessnut tree before all
other, because she loved it. My sweeting Phillis chessnuts loves, while Phillis
the fine shall love these nuts, ne Venus myrt shall dare compare, or Phaeb his
laurel match these nuts.

Every color became Aristippus well (says Horace) ye nightingale, never
wantes Song, says Bacchis in Plautus: neither while you can shape your self
a shift to shade your knavery, will you ever want proof for pursuit of this
purpose.

Thou art more dainty in thy phrase, then trusty in thy speech, and less
pregnant by policy to prove thy folly, then prove with Hostillitie to deprave
my meaning. But thy impudent raylings which thou think thy protection, are
against thy Inuectiue a sufficient purgation. For a galled Horse never wincheth
till he be touched, and a Dear stands at the bay when he is hard beset, and
Menechmus Sosicles then feygned himself mad, when he knew no other means to
avoid his harms. Tullye says, when the people gave Gabinius an applaudite
upon the Stage, or else where, he feared least he had committed some evil,
and if thou had chirped to my Conclusion, I should have thought I had not
done so well.

I wonder how thou can assay to shame me with thy railings, and not blame my
Tutor, who allotted me my task. In this me thinks, thou resemblest Ajax his
Wife, that losing her Husband in the Ship called Argos, wished the Fur
beams whereof it was made, had never been hewed in the wood Peleius, when as
they were not the cause of his Destruction, but the wild fire thrown into the
Shipp: or those that praised one for presenting a Gift to Diogenes, and
mentioned nothing of Diogenes that merited the gift, or rather in my Fancy,
thou art most like Will Summer, which being hurt with any, though he
stood a Furlonge of him, would always strike his next fellow.

The Dogs which were watchers in the Capitol at Rome, for discrying of
Thieves, if they barked at a rightefull occasion, had their ordinary allowance,
if they quested in the day time when no filtching could befall, they had their
legs broken: the same is inrould of the Geese, which were to give watchwoord
by their gagling: So Philotimus, if thou have said as thou should, thou art
a good goose, if not, I would not have thy legs broken, which may stand the
in stead ere thou die, to run from a skirmishe, but I wish thy Tongue well
mauacled, to give no cause of such mishap.

The first part of thy Treatise has little reason, the second less sense,
the third scarce any life at all. Thou increasest in thy grosenes, like Fame in
Virgill, which is ever the stronger the further it goes: or like our holy time
of Lent, which (as Cassiodorus recites out of Socrates the ninthe Book of his
History) was first but three weeks, and by little and little aspired to forty
days. Thy Arguments are drawn from the disport called Hopenniho, wherein all
must say as one says, and do as he does, for all thy confirmation is but an an
exhortation to frame an imitation to other men's liking.

We must stand at reuersion of others men's judgment, and like sielie grooms,
gather their crummes when they have done: As who should say, because Apollo
Cianeius, and Fulgentius, the Bishop of Emv^is, never eat flesh, nor drunk
wine, therefore every man must be dieted after this manner: because some of the
old Seminary, have betaken themselves to wilful peuishnes, as foolish Hermits,
or Ancresses, and devoutly spend their times in holy Orizous, therefore
forsooth we must do so. This follows as well as that in Beuis of Hampton:
Some lost a nose, and some their lip, and the king of Scots has a ship.

Art thou ignorant (good Philotimus) to how many misaduentures our learning
is obnoxious: and how feeble Memory, the keeper of it, is lost with age, or
perished with a knock? Georgius Trapell. A man very learned, quite forgot in
his age, every iott of his learning, as though it had never been he. The
Emperor Claudius so halted in memory, that talkinge in bed with his Wise,
incontinently he would become unmindful of himself, and ask for his Wife
why she came not to bed, and having done to death a Noble man, ye day
following, made great inquiry for him, as for a trusty Senator, to determine
with other of his Counsel on contriuersies. Thou wouldee press us with
burdens till our backs be broken, and then we shall lie in bed and be kept
warm: imitating the inhabitaunts of the province of Carinthia, who having any
small mistruste against one for Theft, straightwaies by their Custom carried
him to Ex- to execution, and the third day after, straightly examine all such
as can give evidence in the case, whether he were guilty or no: If he were, he
hanges on the gibbet till he rotte of by pieces, if he were not, they bury and
honor him with solemn obsequies.

But the way to make a Parrat leave pratinge, and the leave tatling, is not
with talking to you. I will here pitch my pauileons, neither fkirmishing any
more with so desperate a dastarde, nor waging battle again, though thou dare
me with urging.

Then belike (quoth Philotimus) you are a right noble champion, as glorious as
Erastus, that burnt Dianaes Temple, and ran away by the light.

But I am content that thou shall be the scowld, and have the last word. I
fulfill the Camels humility, which then sloupeth and kneleth, when he is
charged with a burden.

Now Parmenio, and Clitepho, it restes for you to decide your masters
variannce, with your even judgments without partiality.

Parmenio stepping forth with as grave a grace, as might well become Epidicus,
Plautus his parasite, thus bespake.

Master mine Phlotimus, and Senior Aemilius, your discretions, are sager as I
suppose, then to sound the depth of your affections, by light flying words,
which neither can sink, because they want force, nor stick in your
stomachs, because they want weight.

If words should way the will, or speech direct the disposition, Atreus for
playing a tyrants part in a Tragedy, might well be demed a manquailer, and
Terence for writing his louinge lays should be impeached for an Adulterer.
Corriualls that combat so bravely in their listes, that neither matcheth other,
and both surpass, may triumph that their valor is counted peareles, and
embrace each other for alliance of quallities. Were it meet that Ennius
excelling in Epicks, should dispraise Cecilius a Comicall Poet, or despite
Pacunius for his woeful Tragedies.

Take heed therefore, and be not quarrelons, strife is soon sown, but hardly
weeded out. Impacable Ate the Goddess of Sedition, thrown down from Heaven,
had wingrs at will to further her speed, in dispersing discord, but the three
Sisters Litae, Jove's high offspring, that united peace, which she had dissolved,
were left a loose behind her far out of sight, not able to keep pace with such
a swinger.

The singular art of M. Aemilius, has left such a sting of admiration within
my breast, that I honor him for learning, whom I loved for manners, and assure
me of his rarenes, for which I ever hoped. His words so well couched, his
moderate gesture, his countenance correspondent to his fil^d Tongue, might
make him seem truthfull to those that are fools, and prove himself witty to
those that are wise.

I think it no modesty to prattle further of his praise, or of the credit he
has woonne But to give plain specialtie of his curious cunning and augment
the prailes of his clerckly work, I give Judgment o^ his cause, that it is in
deed wrong, and seems in show Truth by his learned Apology. If I have
offended him, I have also commended him, the one he deserves, and I look for
no thanks, the other I deserve not, and he will do me no injury.

Clitipho whose censure was by course to follow his fellows, whose judicial
majesty he thought surpassd modesty, began a tale of Jack a nail, which I
am afraid you will not tarry till you have heard.

It was an halt King, and a blind Queen, and they got a lame son, and he
would go to the nine ends of the world to seek his fortune, and when he was
there, he was there, he met with a pilgrim, God give you good even, which is
the way to Poclinton, a pokeful of plummes, he clamb up into a Thistle tree,
and cut down an Hasyll twigge, and broke his head till it was whole, and when
he came home, he was as wise as a Woodcocke.

Here is a dry Tale (quoth Parmenio) laughing heartily at it, and well deserves
a water pot: What Is this to the purpose?

Nay quoth Clitipho, it is never dry speech, when there follows slauer so
fast down your lips. My purpose is not so profound, but sense may be pickte to
prove you a noddie, nor your foolish laughter so sage as your sentences and yet
concordant to so opinionate a fondling. Whether think thou better sport and
more absurd, to see an Ass play on an harp contrary to his sex, or hear the
little Robbin descant his mean note agreant to his kind? Thy stately
rhetoric, is such a non sequitur to thy boyish rachalisme, that better I
cannot lay ape^t thy absurdityes, then by intrusion of this frivolous fable,
less liked of Parmenio, and more agreeable to his personage.

Thy exhortation to those, that know twenty for thy one, is as if thou
should bring Owls to Athens. By my troath Parmenio, construe it as thou
list, I cannot abide to hear the Deuell talk of holidays, nor the pezant to
preach so diuinelye. But you will prattle no more of his praise. To prattle of
his praise, is a pretty phrase, and well compilde I wis. Nor of his won
credit. Take heed what you fay, if he have won it, why does he not wear it?
For you think it no modesty, you might have said duty, but that you are more
in love with modesty, then modesty with you.

My verdite concerning our masters, is as wide from yours, as both their
co^me^dations ar, be-yond our possibilities. Had not thou rather being put
to thy choice that thy master would give thee a lease to support thy prime
with merriment, then serve seven years in laborious drudgery, and then stand
to his pleasure what he would bestow? If thou had Parmenio, reuolte from
thy resistaunce, if thou had not, God send thee thy choice.

Permenio not able to digest this hard meat, and loathe to make broyle or
stain his gravity, returned an answer in this sort. That pate of thine,
that can infatuate so well, and make a man sottish if he be but simple, must
needs it self be well repleat with grosnes. Much like the wels which be in
Norwaye, whose licour is so gross, and extreme could, that if wood be cast in
them they turn it to iron.
