Tell a mare a tale and she will iadishly winch. Lidius courteously bringing
bees honye, was for his labor stung of the bees, and I franckly giving thee my
fairest talent, am laughed to scorn of a roperipe rachell.

I have so far past the bonds of honestly, that if thy vaineglorye be joined
to my surquidrie, of an inch it will quicklye extend to an elne Yet had I
rather confess my self sorry, for offendinge a fool without a fault, then
stand upon the defense of that, which no man can iustlye find to be faultye.

As for you Clitipho, your are nothing peuish proud, an arrogant patch is one,
and you an other, if you will not give over your ignominious brawlinges against
my modesty, my modestest part I promise you, that for his shamefastnes does
sildo^e show him self abroad, will have an action of the case against your
nose. I will not ride an Ass to death, but thus farewell good nody body.

Their tutor and governor partly agreued, yet moved to admiration on every
ones part, more then he would make semblaunce, and fearing the young gentlemen
would not discontnue their discord, unless his persuations took some better
order, mutilated their scouldinges, and thus began his counsels.

Philotimus and Aemilius, since your judges have pronounced that both have done
well, and cannot determine whether has done better, be not angry for the
victory, which they will not give you, but be pressed to preserve your gotten
credit. I hope that the great joy, which your few words have bred me, shall
not fail to further your timely towardnes. It was Democritus his hap walking
abroad to take the air, to mete with protagoras the^ a badger, bearing a
bundle of stix very curiously compacted, toward the City. Democritus musing
how a rude and rural fellow, could without any knowledge of Geometry, use
such art in laing these sticks together, desired him to unfold his fardle,
and try if he could with the like method knit his burden together again.
Protagoras fulfilled his will, and used the self same art in his second
binding, which when Democritus saw, by the promptnes quoth he (my son) which
I perceive in this light matter, I see thou art initiated in the foundation of
far weightier sciences, and thereupon taking him from his servitude, and
teaching him Geometry, Protagoras became so excellent as he did: so say I,
with premonition you be not proud, though I praise you to your teeth, that the
aptnes which I see in this Disputation of pastim^, gives me courage to
conceive some future felicity.

For this matter of variaunce, because a Kingdom divided is not long
established, and friends falling out are hardly attoned, be content to claim
no interest, but bear the bridle euenly.

Nay said Philotimus, a little chafed with arguing: It was Solous opinion, that
rewards and punishments were vphoulders of the City, and therefore since one
must needs do best, let your censure of our doing, give him reward of the
best. For an Aristocratia must never take place, where the Monarchy may
possibly govern the whole.

Aemilius as one ex Academica, thinking himself able to mantaine any
controversy, and somthinge moved with the indurate stubbornenes of his friend
Philotimus, had him prove that, and hent the honor.

Philotimus briefly concluded in this manner, Jupiter has the dominion over
all the Gods It is monstrous for one body to have 2. heads: all ye members
are ruled by one heart: all the parts of the Ship stand under one keele: cranes
follow one crane: the heard has one shepherd: one bushe faith the Proverb,
can not harbor two Robin redbrestes: more wars have been raised, because
many would command, then by their disobedience which would not be
commanded. What fierce fightes were those betwixt Scilla and Marius, Caesar and
Pompey, Augustus and M. Antonius, Galba and Othos, Othos and Bytellus, Bytellus
and Vespasianus, Seuerus and Iulianus, Seuerus and Albinius? Tyranie, is worse
then usurping of the peers, or all the commons aduauncing themselves, and
therefore a Monarchy is the best.

Aemilius answered: The similitude of God may not be drawn to man. Liberty
being the greatest gift, that the Gods bestow upon men, by what reason should
one man's authority take away the liberty of so many men? One man's judgment
is soon subdued to evil affectes, as one bucket of water is sooner poisoned,
then many several vessels. Though one crane guide al, yet not always one and
the self same Crane, but now this, now that, the comparison of your Shepherd
will not hold. For if the flock be great, the Shepherds must be many.
Again, the whole flock is of a sociable, agreaunt, and uniform nature, never
disdayning his fellow, or Lordinge over him, neither feeling the yoke of any
government. One City has been governed many hundred years by a Senate,
that ruled more quietly then any King, having kings of many Provinces under it.

Philotimus replied: It is not liberty to live as we list, but by the law and
ordinance enacted by Nature, some to govern, as the most wise and virtuous,
some to obey, as less excellent in gifts. And because it semes hard, that
one should rule all, the Gods have promised to rule the hearts of Kings and
Princes, as Ulysses says in the second of Homers Iliades. He says also in the
same place: Great fame and high renown, are things deaurate, and of weight,
if they be marshalled in a King. As it is easy for one vessel to be infected:
so is it easier to find one absolute man, then a multitude meet for an entire
Empice. Every one of the Kings that were subject to that Senate, ruled his
several Province by himself, as well as the Senate did all in one. Lastly,
there is one chief in every Senate, to whom the rest do some obeysaunce,
and in whose sentence they stand and rest.

Here Aemilius saw himself in the lapse, and almost drawn dry of all
replies, and weried with perturbaunce of this civil dissension, with these or
such like words yielded himself.

Whether victory be an appurtenaunce of a rightful cause, or a subtle
conueyaunce of thy slight matter, has bleared our eyes, I know not: or
whether enorme causes do want solyd matter, or a trewantlike barrister must
needs be pitched over the peark, now it boots not: but to co^discend to our
covenant wherewith we are combined, as I grant me ouercomen in our latter
discussion, so I give thee the Trophies of all our disceptations.

Philotimus, who was ever more covetous to be accounted famous, then willing to
hear his land exalted, returned his friends courtesy in these words.

If there be any credit in taking these praises, which I have received,
what honor must be in the gift of this credit, which thou have bestowed?
And since you are so willing to depart with all your glory now of a sodeine, I
will not do like ye alehouse clerckes, that divide the world into four parts,
and take three to themselves: for I will not only make partition of the whole,
but bind my self grateful to requite it as I may.

By this time they were come to their lodging, which now they neither looked
for, nor all this while had thought on, where repose they themselves till the
next morrow.

The morning following, as they rid parling of such matters as were obuious,
Senior Mondaldo, which never misspent time unfruitfully, interrupted their talk
with these words.

PHilotimus and Aemilius, since we have nothing at this instant, wherewith to
be well occupied, and you were staulled with yester days Disputation, I will
prescribe you certain Inductions to be performed at the University, which if
you follow, you shall neither repent you of your present attention, nor repine
at the prospering of other Gentlemen: and because contrary to ordinary, you are
grounded in the liberal arts, which is not usual to freshmen, when they first
come to study, I will set you down no childishe samplers, as though I mente to
impleade you of Ignorance, but such as serve Gentlemen of your estate, and
may beseem Students of a good standing.

First abase not good letters in your imagination: for their art is more
behoofull for the government of Gentlemen, then their want is material for a
private Subject. When Camillus triumphed over the Frenchmen the day of his
victory, he wrote these words in the Capitol of Rome: Thou have been mother
to all Wisemen, and stepdame to all Fools.

Be studious in your learning, but not sottish in your labors: for moderate
pains employed in due times, increases knowledge without bodilye damage, when
intricate endless triuious toylings, plunge the wits, and crucify the carcas.

Trifle not your times in vain settes of words, but let your greatest
travails be Philosophical Institutions: for it is not hard for the Tailor to
shape a fit garment for a straight body, but it is impossible for a crooked
body to be framd by his art to fit a straight garment. It was one of Socrates
his Emblems, that he is eloquent enough, that delivers his mind plainly.

Among all other Arts, forgett not the Mathematikes. for the Etymology of
their name, exhortes you to learn them.

Turn over the volumes of ancient histories: for so you being young without
experience, your knowledge shall stretche further then your fathers
remembrance.

Have a sight in all Languages, though it be but superficial: for so you
shall be Citizens in all Countries, and be able to calculate their conditions
and manners: and that which is not specified in some of our Languages, is
explaned to the full in an other Tongue.

For precepts to be eloquent in any one Tongue, I can assign you no
resolute certeintie: For neither are all Orators taught by one master, (says
M. Cicero) neither do they use the same ways and means. Sweet were the
sauce would please each kind of taste, straung were the vein that some man
thought not vain: yet that which is generally received of all, is conuenable
for you. First in your entrance to every Tongue, read those Authors which are
sound without blemish, as Caesar, Tully, and Terence, in the Latin tongue: for
Liuye and Salust, chiefteynes of Antiquaries, being generally perfitte
touching their whole woorks, but mancate here and there with some scattering
faults, are not to be neclected of the Students in Eloquence, but to be
diligently read of those that have judgment. Secondarily mark their phrase and
Elocution, and then that which is commonly lightly regarded, be painful to
make a sweet round number. An example of this number, does Sturmius point out
in the beginning of Tully his Oration for A. Cecinna, the very first
Sentence, of which, because Tully speaks somewhat, and Hermogenes at large, I
omit to treatise any thing at all.

Of all other things, be charie of your company. For we commonly say, that
like will to like, and gather suspicion of ones disposition, by his compeeres.
When great Scipio came from the war of the Poeniens, better accompanied with
dastardlye michers, then with valiant Captains, one Oruetus a famous Orator
said unto him: of troth, it is great shame to thee, and small honor to the
Senate, yt thou have overcome the wise Affres, and art so wise thy self, and
of the blood of the wise Romans, and will be accompanied with these Fools.
I say unto thee, that thy wit is in more peril here in Rome, then thy life was
in Afric. The frequenting of P. Mutius his house, made the young Gentleman P.
Rutilius, be thought honest in life, and learned in the Law. The small
acquaintance, which M, Celius in his youth had with Catiline, was after in a
controversy concerning him nere, something prejudicial to the vprightnes of his
cause.

Let your companions be at least your coequalls: for yt is comely, insinuating
to your betters, if you may without presumption, and affable to inferiors with
a sober submission.

Use the company of your equals with emulation, be directed by your betters
with imitation, contemn not the basest for any occasion.

Learn wisdom of the ancient, but affect not their gravity: gather wit of
the wantons, but detest their corruptions: learn manners at the Court, there
are few at the University.

These lessons, with a request to print them well in your minds, shall suffice
at this time: what books you shall read, and in what order, we will assign at
our iourneyes end.

Philotimus with a gracious bashfullnes gave him thanks for his good Lecture,
with protestation, that one thing wherewith he had comforted him, in saying
that their procedinges were more forward, then theirs are usually which come to
an University, he either feared was not so, or else much meruailed it should be
so. For I have read said he, that no man was sufferable to enter Plato his
School, unless he had good knoweledge in the perspections of Geometry, and
then I muse why our masters of Florence, should be so far ouerseene, to admit
either Children that be over young for their studies, or ignorant trewants
that be too idle to learn, or blockeheaded doultes that have no capacities.

Senior Mondaldoes answer to this, shall be Counsel, for I think it better
to rest in the midway, then run my self out of breath, and get no goal.

To make recapitulation of all their communication they had by the way, were
more troublous to my pen, then now it can in tend, and therefore intermitting
all interlacing of many a good discourse nothing unprofitable, I will bring
them now at length to their wished University, where thus much I can say in
the young Gentlemen's behalf, but in especial of Philotimus, that in
application of his book, he was as industrious, as if his living had laid on
his Learning, and so curious and circumspect in mainteyning of his credit, that
he only was the man, whom all men admired. In all points of learning
Philotimus was commended, in all comparisons Philotimus excepted, in all
exceptions Philotimus preferred.

His life was more virtuous, then his line was noble, and more notorious was
his virtue, then the brute of his blood, and his name better credifed, then his
personage acquainted.

In one and the only thing that belonges to an Orator, in sovereignty of
style, and a sweet and treatable pronunciation, he exceeded all others of whom
I have read. It is reported of Tullye, that for one sentence brauelye
delivered, all the people at once cast up their cappes, and gave a shoute: Then
trust me (Gentlemen) I do think, that if Philotimus had lived in those days,
and spoken to that people, that at every Period they had been amassed.

His manners were mild, and very amiable. For though he were of a choleric
sanguine complexion, whose operation does use to make his Subject sodeine: yet
he could so well refrain his choler, and make exchange for cheuisaunce of
curtesye, that these Sentences were often rolling in his mouth. Arist. says,
that Those are fools, which cannot be angry at an occasion: But Plato says,
that Those are more fools, which will be angry with out great occasion. I well
believe Arist. for he seldom lies, but I will follow Plato, for he thought it
sin to lie.

His body was decently made, and featlye framed, containing an absolute
constitution, and conuenience of liniaments: his head not a slope cornered, but
round and globewise fashioned. His hair auburne or chesten color, and so was
Hectors: his forehead smooth and vnwrinckled, beautified with comelye
eiebrowes, and such were the brows of Alcibiades, and gallantly garnished
with a pair of amiable eyes, not hollow, but delightfully standinge out,
cherefull to his friends, and churlish to his foes, and such says Heliodorus
were the eyes of Theagenes, his cheeks roseall like Phoebus rising in the
Oriental sky: of stature he was semely, neither dwarfish like a man cut of at
legs, nor a lungis like one that stands upon stiltes, but just in the
mids, wherein consists virtue His port and state of body bolte upright,
his gate framed to comeliness, not nicely affected, nor curiously
counterfayted, as it were plaiers, and disguised masquers, who by a kind of
vpstart gate, unwisely ween to win commendation In community of life he was
very jocund, neither to ba^latiue with flattery, nor to whust with morositie,
which Arist. 2 of his Ethics cap. vii. terms the two extremes of courteous
humanitye: familiar in communication with gentle mildnes, seasoned with
pleasure and a reverent gravity: without prattling and tatling, without biting
scoffs or vpbraiding taunts, pleasauntlye conceyted and merry with honesty,
using therein no filthiness or ribauldrie: and as he was most far from malapert
scurrility, and scenicall gesture, so was he free from sulleyne stern
severity, and stoicall indolency.

This for the most part was his behavior. In tongue he was sylent, in this
resembling Cato Vticensis, which never spoke but being urged: in countenance
sad, and yet not so sullen as M. Crassus, which never but once laughed in all
his life. From company solitary, but for this only cause, for which Sophocles
in age would sequester himself vz. to quiet his mind with vntroblous
co^tentation and increase the gifts of knowledge and learning. And as Hermes
Prismegistus says, that Plotin us the philosopher, made as it were to all
ornaments of virtue, choose him a secret place of rest, where he might be far
from human conversation, and bestow his time in divine contemplation, free
and feareles from all the insultes and counterchecks of fortune, and that he
contemned all titles of honor, and all possessions of riches, accounting that
true honor and wealth, whereby a garnished mind does know the oryginall of
it own authority: even so was Philotimus affected, that trade did he
trauerse, and that mediocritie.

For he often would say, that since no man might enter Dianees Temple at Craeta
(where Dedalus has eternal memory, for his worthy monuments) unless he went
in stripped and naked, it was probable that none were docile Disciples of
learning, but such as cast of the care of worldly pomp.

His diot was moderate, his dreams not vain and reauing, joined with idle
talk, as commonly theirs are, which abou^d with read choler, which Iuuenal
well describes in his 7. book: With grislye dreadful Dreams by night their
heads are vexte, with ghastly visions in their sleep yelad with vgsom shapes.
For of choler are enkindled burning Agues, and fantastical Imaginations,
doting of burninge of Towns and Houses, of murders, of hurlye burlies, when
the fumositie of the choler strikes into the brain. The continuance of the
disturbance of this, is voyded by vomit, sweat. and euacuation by siedge,
which may be done with Radix pontica, and such things as provoke urine, as
these herbs: Alkakengie, Sperage, Gardeyn Parsely, Annyseede, Fenellseede,
and by the forbearing of all hot, fat, and sweet meats, which are very apt to
be turned into choler, except reisons, licoras, and by eating such things as
will quallify the heat of the blood. Sleep also must be provoked by Lactuce,
and sallet herbs, that do humect the brain, and all the parts of the body,
and Venus, anger, unseasonable labor, long fasting, must be avoided, and
sckanting our selves of victuals.

His Dreams were such, as after the first sleep, the brain not stuffed
with fumes, do presage some news approaching. As Valentinianus dreaming that
he saw his wife altered from her merry glee, and wear a mourning gown, the
next day had it so brought to pass by his sodeine death. And Augustus his
mother dreaminge that she brought forth a Star which illustrated the whole
earth, had it verified in the Acts of Augustus.

To prosecute the Chronicle of Philotimus, be on a certain day, hearing a crew
of litigious ruffia^s, belching out their fuliginous fomentations against him,
and insulte with hateful spite over some of his doings, thinking no body had
heard them, but they among themselves, covertly stood on the other side the
wall, and gently and friendly admonished them, as though he had been one of
their own companions. I beseech you Sirs, speak low, least Philotimus hear,
for this is sometime his walk, where he takes the air. And arguing on a time on
some Sophistical points, with a Gentleman named Papius, who stood very
stiflye to his own assertion, and ouerheated with chafinge and anger in
disputing, condemned Philotimus for an heretic in Philosophy: then (quoth
Philotimus) I appeal to Papius when he is sober, to debate the dissension.
Which meek answer appeased Papius, and revolted him from his error.

It was native to Aemilius his nature, to counteruaile all injuries with a
contrary counterbuffe, and never to die in the debt of his trespassours.
Hereupon would Philotimus expostulate with him. What if an Ass or an Ox should
kick or spurn thee, would thou think it manhood to strike again? But I
will advise thee of a good medicine in such a case. Go to morrow morning, and
kneel the down beside the great Oak in Siloes wood, and look whatsoever it
bids thee do, that do, and nothing else. Aemilius thinking that Philotimus
meant to work some enchauntment, went thither to see his devise, but when he
came there, he heard nothing but aniayerie murmr among the Trees: at his
return home, Philotimus asked him what he heard. Aemilius in a pelting chafe,
thinking himself derided, answered: I heard as much speech, as thou have
honesty, and that is as much as an egg has otemeale. Then quoth Philotimus,
say to thy accomplices as is commanded thee, and that thou say is nothing at
all, and tho^u shall never damnifye thy self with such effluence of
choleric sominges. He quoth Aemilius, that will know the construction of your
meaning, must conjure for it before, or use such herbs as Cynocephalia,
which Apion used in calling up ghostes, to know of them where Homer was borne.

Philotimus his friendship and Aemilius his, by this time was perfect, and as one
God could not vadoe that which another did, so what either of them avouched,
the other would not disanull.

His joy was most full every way, till time it pleased God to visit Mantua, and
therein his fathers house with a grievous mortalitye and plagne, whereof ten of
his brethren and sisters died, so that only one sister named Fuluia (besides
his father and mother) was suruiuor. Of these news philotimus heard nothing
till the residue were amended, the town and his fathers house clear from the
plague, when the Lord Cleocritus and the Lady Castibula, meaning to solace
their sorrows with his presence, sent for him home.

About this time was philotimus greatelye troubled in mind, he knew not why,
and the very same night his fathers men came to Florence, he dreamed
fearfully. The servant were unwilling abruptly to show him these vnwelcom
news, and therefore foretelling Mondaldo and Aemilius, desired them to use the
matter so, as their young master might not take it to much to heart. The next
day, philotimus rehearsed what fearful dreams he had, using these words. If
dreams divine, or heavenly visions be not vain, and Gods be just, whom we
may trust, that no errors train, some luckles hap approaches.

He feared lest as Sextus was forewarned in his sleep by a dead man, of the
heading of his father, so these apparitions should forhight some drouping
danger

The burning of his right ear stunted him likewise, for yt it is one of ye
parts which Saturn an evil planet governs, and so much the rather he was
aggrised, for that he had not heard of long time from his father.

Mondaldo hear took occasion to check him for his dreads, discoursinge what
calamities the best had suffered, and how peaceblye they had borne them, till
with his pholosophicall catechisinges he had fortyfyed his mind against all
all the force of Fortune.

At last Mondaldo, after much talk, demanded this Question: whether if he had
diverse precious Jewels in a Ship, whereof two or three Gemmes were of more
valuation in his accounte, then all the rest, and the Ship should suffer wrack
these only saved, he would more lament the loss of his Jewels, or rejoice
at the safety of his Gemmes. Philotimus answered, yt though a little grief
daunt a great deal of joy, as a little leaven leauens a great trough of dough,
yet if his Gemmes were of such worth as is supposed, they would be a good
comfort to so bitter a corsine. Then quoth Mondaldo, rejoice, that in Gods
visitation of thy Fathers house, he has taken to his mercy ten of thy
Brethren and Sisters, and yet of his great mercies has left thee thy Father
and Mother, and thy dearest Sister Fuluia.

At these words Philotimus scritched out, and as a distraughte Beolemer,
vnuisedly raued against all the high Aethereall powers. O cruel Gods (quoth
he) feed on my friends, yea feed your gluttie hearts, and ouergorge your
gulpinge weasants with my griping smart. In Corses ten, I ten times dead,
am carried to my Grave.

Here Mondoldo rebuked him, and told him, he knew not with whose folly to
counterpeize his frenzy, except with Heracleates Dionisius his, who having
learned of Zeno to be hart-hole in all afflictions, as soon as he felt a little
pain in his reins, bad Discipline adieu, and fainted in his sickness.

Well they directed their Journey homeward, where after the Lo. Cleocritus, and
the Lady Castibula, and M. Philotimus and Fuluia, had in tracte of time
assuaged sorrow, sittinge on a day after Dinner, at the Table with one Telamon
his very friend, and other Gentlemen, Cleocritus began to appose Philotimus and
Aemilius in their studies, and inquired of them the state of their University,
whether yt were, as some boldly would slander it, decayed in study, and grown
to unseemly insolency.

Philotimus answered, that it could not greatly be blamed though it were
so, considering the small regard was had of Learning, yet comparing that he had
experienced in his time, with that he had heard of others in their time, he
thought it was like it self at all times: and if there were any abuses it was
not his duty to tell School tales. Papirius his mother (quoth he) demau^ding
of him what was done in the Senate house, he gibingly answered, that Husbands
might have two Wives, thinking it lawful to answer that with a lie, that
was not meet for her to demand with modesty. Myrmice the maid of Athens,
that Minerua loved so well, for displaying the Ploughe that Minerua made in
despite of Ceres, was turned into an Emmot. The same Minerua was aggrieued with
Aglauros, for saying her secrets. The Romans bearing the Minotaure for their
ensign, hid the hinder deformed part, and published that which resembled a
man: and therefore (said he) if you be inquisitiue of any crime in Florence,
which is rather to be amended priuatelye, then reprehended publicklye, my
answer is, Nihil scio, nisi nescio.

Every one at the Table commended the witty answer of Philotimus, yet
Cleocritus willing to be resolved in his question, urged it to Aemilius, who
gave him this answer.

You are not ignorante (Sir) what copy not only of zealous preachers, expert
lawiers, learned phisitio^s, but also excellent yo^g men in all arts and
tongues, do daily flow from our University. Yet this will I say, which neither
can be hid, for that it is commonly known, neither should be hid, for that it
requires reformation, that they have as little respect of persons, as any
Judges need have. For the veriest stunted fool, distorted criple, rudest
loobbin, and the hob-hansomest man may be for his fee admitted to their
society. In the cooptations to their Fellow^hipps, in the Commencements for
their degrees, not he that best deserves, but he that has best friends,
oftentimes has the most suffrages.

Indeed this must I needs say, and sigh when I say if, that if one beautified
with frugalitie of wit, and all manner good habits that can be placed in the
Predicament of Quality, be either poor from the beginning, or by some
casualtie be impouerished, he shall not only be debarred of his preferment, but
the least falt he can commit, shall be imputed to him as a notorious crime,
and if in word or look he offend, it shall be cast in his dish in time of his
petition.

For the study of Liberal Arts, it is almost decayed, and in their place
Toys wear an hoode to cover their nakedness.

For our Preachers, though some be most exquisite in life and doctrine, yet
know I diverse which are so far unable to maintain a controversy in the
Schools, that saving a few Catechismes and short Epitomies, which may furnish
them with some Divinity out of the Pulpit, I think they have little more
Divinity then your Lordships mule. In the face of the world they are searchers
of sins, exhorters to punish sins, inueighers against the sparing of
sins, and yet they themselves in private corners, and so forth.

And these I warrant you, will sooner be incumbentes in a good Benefice, then
they that better deserve by the frugalitye of their knowledge.

An other sort there is, by whose behavior, you would judge them
Metrapolitans of the whole Realm: so stern they are in looks, so Maiesticall
in pace, carrying up their ioltes like hoigh headed malt-horses, so sirlye in
words, so coy of their cappes, contemning their inferiors with a peazantlie
despite, and flattering their betters to get them liuinges.

I think they would imitate Days, Vulcans Priest, who was one of the richest
of all King Priams subjects.

And for their lordly minds, it may be, they take warrant out of Plutarcch. For
Plutarch in his Problems supposes, that hence comes the doing of our hattes,
for that, who in ancient time did Sacrifice to the Gods, held during the
said Sacrifice their cappes on their heads, when Princes and great
personages, to do fit honor to the Sacrificatour, uncovered their heads,
to the intent it might appear, they reckoned themselves baser the^ the Gods
and in respect of their functions, inferior to the priests.

Let their excuse be this, or what they list, but I hold them accursed that
offend in the premises, though I will not accuse them to bring my self
into briers.

Peace for shame (quothe Philotimus) what an unmannerly Bird art thou to
defoile thine own nest? I can iustifye said Aemilius, all that I have said,
and more, if need require. But I had better slander them truly, which is no
Slander indeed, then flatter them falsely as thou do. Not so said
Philotimus: For Diogines termed Slander a wild savage beast, and Flattery but
a tame one.

Telamon and other gentlemen at the table were importunate to hear this matter
of Flattery and Slander canuassed, and therefore Aemilius began as ensues
against Flattery.

A contention betwixt Flattery and Slander whether is the worse. Aemilius
against Flattery.
O Most dissembling crafty vice Flattery, not ocularly to be seen, and yet a
palpable coosener. Clisophus one of the train of Phillip king of Macedonia,
because his master sometimes had the gouts and halted, he would limpe with him
also as a co^passionate, and when the king was well, he was well, finally what
passion soever pressed the king, he expressed the same in imitation. Nicesias,
when he see the blood of Alexander gushe out, would exclaim. O what noble
blood is this, it is ye blood of Gods, not of a man. O that gnat which tastes
of this blood, must far surmount other gnats.

Such an insinuating stinge is Adulation, that Hercules wise and wary, was hood
winked with the pleasant clawe of Cercopes, and Jupiter incensed with auckward
spite against his daughter Pallas and all the Grecian pack, with Thetis her
loutinge Flatteries, and humble stick speech with touching of his beard, and
culling of his neck, and kissing his knees, and tears wrested out with much
wringing.

great men of the world are like great pottes (quoth Diogines) which he they
never so great, you may carry them about by the ears. But I would they might
say as Pindarus said, that he had already rewarded the travail of Flatterers,
because he had not made them liars: or would do, as Diomedes did to the
notable Harper, who promising him a talent for playing, and he demau^ding it,
answered: I have already paid yt thy day. For my ears were but filled with
an empty sound, and I have tickled thine with a bare promise.

It is good bowing the body to catch others on the hippe (says Aristippus for
defense of this fault: who being reprehended for kissinge the kings feet,
when he solicited a suite, answered, I am not worthy blame for stouping, it
is long of him that has his ears in his heels. See what Flattery can do
Philotimus, what say you to Slander. Philotimus answered.

Philotimus his Inuectiue against Slander.
THE Slaunderers, have as many heads as Hydra to inuente wickedness, as many
legs as Tholus to run to naughtines, as many hands as Typhoeus to put it in
practice, fifty men's voices like Stentor the Grecian, to be the cole carriers
of many a loud lie. They are furious Centaures, by formed Scyllaes, huge
Cyclopes, fretting Furyes, monstrous Harpies, as cunning in shooting their
fiery venomous shaftes, as the men Abantes were in throwing their darts, as
gredy to devour men's credites, as the great Bore of Calidon was to depopulate
the fruits, which Bore Diana sent thither, because Oneus the King of the
Country, gave each year of his first fruits in Sacrifice to all the Gods,
and not to her.

A Flatterer can insinuate with weightines of sappy words, gravity of
Philosophical Sentences, and oftentimes make the Truth serve his turn: But a
Slaunderer, is an vnflidge nestlinge unable to fly, an unnatural abortion
borne before his time, an imperfecte Embryon not able to persiste, without
supporting of others.

This Envious Slander, kepes her house in drearye, yrksome, dimmy Den,
repleate with loathie stinking silth, and frozen joy could deuoyd of Fire, and
Air, yet almost chokte with smouldering smoke: and there she sittes fast
gnawing on the flesh of snakes and toades: there slothfullye she walks with
lumpishe leisure like a snayle, her lips are ever pattering, her chekes are
bolne, her face Saturnian swart, her never sleping eyes do look askew, her
three rowed teeth are fued with filthy dress, her goumes are waryish blewe,
her festred gall has made her stomach green, and all beuenimd is her
spirting tongue.

The hellbound never lau^hes unless at others harms, her body is as lean as
any rakes, she freats at others good success, as if her self were harmde, yet
seeking to anoy and work ristresse to other folk, her self she does destroy.

In night, when sunnie streamy light is cladde within Hells black mournful
weed, when none does see nor hear (for why? her face she dare not show, nor
once avouch her name) she stalks to them abroad, that are themselves most
de^elish Hipocrites, and most suspicious of others demeanor, she whistles
this or that into their patent credulous ears, and forgeth all God wot. If any
circumstaunce of where, or when, or whom, may make a probable Argument, she
tells it with a grace, and binds it with an oath. This mandat does she give to
all her brokers, ye at every iumpe they steal a yard, and at every Declaration
add as much more, and after this she eft returns from whence she came, and so
God send her to the gallows.

If any say that it is she from whom this tatling is deduct, she stares and
startes abacke, and swears amain it is not she. Her throateboll swells with
puffed veins, her heart it sweltes with flaming fury, her eyes began sparckle
fire, there stands about her smeared chappes a lothlie foming froth, she
poysoneth all she deals withal with this infection. That like the bablinge
Echo, they shall never restrain their wambling Tongues, but strain them out
when others speak, to carpe and cut of the best part of their meaning: she is
more venomous then the slimye Serpente Python, with fiery blaring mouth, and
forked wagging Tongue: whose combrous harm did mean to charm Latonaes seed.

Now Aemilius what say you to this slander, I doubt not but by this time you
think your flattery a saint in respect of this devil.

This question was left doubtful Cleocritus and the company rise, accounting
this small time after dinner well spent.

The unkindly love of Telamon to Castibula his friends wife.
NOw (sir) understand that Mounser Telamon soiourninge and commoning with the
Lord Cleocritus, had suffered his freewill to be witched, and gind with the
beautye of Castibula. which though he saw adverse to all humanity, yet was the
heat of his lust so unquenchable, that it smouldred reason, and burned with
beastlines. He had protracted the disclosure of it hitherto, and perceived his
procrastination to be perilous.

Castibula having set sorrow apart for the death of her children, and relieved
almost with her lief old joys, as she walked one day in her verdurant garden
alone, after other ordinary communication, which he made as it were a portall
and preludium to his cause, he boorded her in this sort, yet not in bourde as
the consequence gives evidence, but in hard earnest, which was the earnest of
her death.

Telamon. O madam the mistress of my heart, I have a suit, yet am I mute and dare
not speak.


Castibula, if semely it be, speak on and speed, if not, conceal such suts,
command ought else.


Telamon. can that unseemly be, whose grant does save, or whose denial
spills my life.


Castibula. I would not with to save my life with wrongful means.


Telamon. yet give me leave to speak, and liberty to live.

Cast. I give you both if either I can give. Say on.


Tell. I will say on, though loath. For long I mingled bashful shame with love,
till love at length surpassed shame. One of the old Philosophers, Diogines by
name, an odd fellow, and full of quirkes, demanded on a time of a prodigal
youth an unreasonable some of money: The youngs man amazed at this impudent
begging, asked him what he meant to crave such a boon. Truly (quoth
Diogines) I see thee so riotous in lashing out thy money, that I thought if at
this time I had not thy reward, thy ability would not long too so decay,
that it could not though it would, yield me succor hereafter, and therefore
I mente to beg once for all: Even so good Lady, though my request may seem
unpious in uttering, extrauagant from reason, and to tax my affections with an
dishonest inclination; yet if your Ladyship afford me not this bliss, ye
shortness of my life will in few days to ceme prevent all swetenes of
medicinable electuaries. Now good Lady, if by this little you can judge the
whole, cut of the course of my sequel process, with present accomplishment of
my brief demand. The Cat would lick milk, but she will not wette her feet,
and I would feign have, but I dare not crave all.


Cast. I fear I know not what, yet ascertaine me of my doubt.


Tell. I need not at all, thy Deitye has presaged what it is, and must amend
it with thy pardon, if ought be amiss. If I should tell thee all my
torments, which alas I cannot, and thou believe them, which I fear thou will
not, thou might vaunte that thou had the truest Servant in the earth,
and I not doubt but obtain the best Mistress in the world.


Cast. Jupiter foreshield these dreaded mischiefs.


Tell. It is is not Jupiter, but Venus, that has hurt me, and must help me.


With that Castibula looking wistly upon him, in furious rage ^lange hastilye
from him. Telamon mistrustinge noe less at the first, and trustinge yet to
conclude this wooful Epitasis with a joyful Catastrophe, grasped her in the
mids with both his arms, appaulde at her displeasure, and scarce able to
speak, till comd to himself, O Lady (quoth he) what mean you to shun me, more
like the frantic foe, whom fell Erichtho has in chase with many her twifold
turninges, then one more beloved of him you have in presence, then ever.
Andromache was of Hector? The Law forbids to do a man to die before his cause
be heard. First let me plead at the bar of your beauty, and then let
your will pronounce his Judgment. Doom after death is rigor without reason. O
that you had Cassandraes gift, to deserye all secrets of the heart.

Cast. A Rat is soon judged by her rank smell, and Hiena bewraied by her
counter fayt call.


Tell. Bulls blood of it self is contagious and pestilente, but in composition
mixte with Eolewortes, is solatarye against an opilation. My bare suile of it
self bluntlye weighed, semes to auerre against all honesty, but conjoined
with the circumstances of my long smothered love, and the peril of utter
perishing but ye give me gale delivery, it may be a warning to you to salute
yt with a welcome, and not to debar his reasons of right.

I have received enterteinment at my Lord your husbands hands, far better
then by service I could demerit, for ye which I deny not me more indebted,
then if it had been less I can ever discharge. And so received Paris of
prince Menelaus, when he committed the rape of Helen, I am thancklesse to my
Lord, because I am thrall to my Lady, and for that which I owe to his manifold
good turns, my liberty is impaund and imprisond to you. Think not me
graceles for laying siege to that fortress which never any before me durst
attempt: but think me more fervent then other your vassals, which see your
beauty peerlesse, and your virtue invincible. Dan Phoebus angrye that you
excelled him in beauty, could no otherwise have pretexte to minister his
malice, but by casting his beams into your radiant eyes, to dazell the looks
of all the beholders: And this is the cause, that all that vewe thee wish as I
do, but not able to abide thy glittering glaunce, close up their eyes for
fear of blinding, and give over their desires overcome with necessity.

Now square my love with a direct line, which with no canuassado will take ye
repulse. It is natures instigation, to make choice of that, to whom we are
dearest and best beloved, then give me (O Lady) O give me my justice. And here
he pausinge, either in meditation what to say more, or expectation of her
pleasure, Castibula answered.

Thou look I should thank the for thy lawless love, and I look thou
should abate it because I am thanckles. Thisbe a Graecia^ maid, beloved of
Thermutes an Aegiptian Thief, wrote in a letter to a friend of hers, that she
had rather be hated of the civil Grecian, then he beloved of the rude
Barbarian: and without offense be it it spoken (but under no correction) I had
rather wish the my capital foeman, then after this sort to have the my friend.

It fares not with women, as with Trophonius his den, into which whosoever
entered, was without respect of persons, rapte with a fury of diuinatio^: but
whosoever shall like a woman must not be rewarded with a guerdon of ye like.
Do not thou know, that pease sown in cold ground turn to tares? That
whea^e sown in stony ground turns to darnell? That there is a kind of Rye,
which being carried into France, changes his grain? Let either the
beastlines of the cause, or the friendship of my hushande, or the honest
affection I have always borne thee, be a Supersedias to stay thy suite. Go
not about to get that with thy toil, which you can not but forgo with
shamfull foil. Thou do not only offend in a monstrous crime, but
deseruest a a grievous penalty for excusing thy crime.

Thy suite a Gods name, spiced with the gleames of of thy stailes love must
mitigate ye odiousnes of thy request. Nay Telamo^, the river Hypenis descending
down from Scithia, is a water wholesome and sauourie, the which after the
entrance of a little salt spring, waxes so salt and brackishe, that none can
drink of it. Much virtue is blemished with a small spot of vice I have read in
Vitrunius of the river Chimera, whose water in taste is very delectable, but
parting into two sundry troughes and channels, the one continues sweet, the
other conuertes into bitterness: no otherwise my husband and I as long as we
live in wedlock bands, shall have comfort of our friends, and credit in our
country, but if I disseuer this sweet society, he shall keep his honor
directed with honesty, and I kindle reproach detected of disloyalty.

Indeed Mariners commonly sail by night when other sleep, for that the winds
that come then from the earth, do calmly fill their sails: and thou think
perhaps that I have now opportunity, when my Lord is secure and unadvised of
my fetch. My Lord and spouse has the virtue of a Diamond, which is of power
to try chastity. But that shall not need Telamon, for sooner shall my soul
to weightles air ywaste, and time untwine his date, and azure heavens stand
still, ere I recule one inch from thee (O dearest Cleocritus) I dare compare
with the Lady Sulpitia, whose husband Lentulus Crustellio being banished into
Sicilie of the Roman Triumueri,, and she kept diligently of her mother that
she should not follow, yet that her faith to her husband might not be
violated, took upon her a servants apparel, and with two men and two women
stole after him from her mother.

Never shall it be said, but there is some Lady in Italy, which will be as
quirie in wedlock rites, as ye insulans of Pteleon, who in this point are
accounted most religious. If I should commit this abhominatio^, not to be
spoken of, I were more cruel the^ ye widows of Lemnos, which killed all their
husbands, and many degrees worse then ye daughters of Danaus, which al (save
Hipormnestra, that spared Lincius) the first night they lay with their new
bride-groomes, be reaued them of life. I rather would to shield mine honor,
and prevent his shame, like valiant Penthisilia, which with her pollar stiffly
stood in ^roies defense, so venture life and limme.

It is neither a show of great pleasure (though the sweetest poison pearce
the deepest, and fair words can soonest deceive) nor all the torments in the
world, no though my soul (as once was Prometheus his) were pinchte and gnawne
with Vultures strayned talantes, that can reduce me from my constancy.

And can thou think (Telamon) that Cleocritus would not with eger mood
exulterate this iniurye, whom thou know to love me more then lightlye
Aristomenes deigned to defend his domes of price, whom he in wars had won,
and rather chose to dye in their defense, then filthy men should foil their
chastytye: then would Cleocritus do greater things then that for spousall
loved wife or do thou ween he should not know it? yes, yes, send sot:
can thou or any else ent so near a morsel from him, and he not feel it?
Eratis a sheepehearde sleapinge on the mountain amiddes his flock, was slain
by a a male goat jealous of his mate, with which Eratis most abhominablye
and yet very closelye had subverted the Laws of Nature. It is hard to blind
the eyes of him that loves, who not only seethe more then all other men, but
also has the potence which noe other has, to see in his steep and in the
absence of his lover.

Restrain therefore these saluaged cogitations, and since thou fawnest
friendship on my Lord and husband (and not vnworthylye) show such fidelylye in
thy demeanor, as sometime one Alexander did to his friend: who lyinge in naked
be^ with his friends wife, and by his friends grant, laid a naked sword
betwyxte them to keep them asunder.

Thy fair protestations lie prostrate at my feet, any little prevail. For I
never liked an affected finenes in an evil face, nor good words in so bad a
case.

If then can bring under thy gaddinge appetits, and submite them to the
frendshippe you owe my husband, Cariclia was not half so glad, that her
Theagenes overcame Ormenes in the race at Pithiaes games, a^ I will rejoice
that my husbands deserts, have daunted the force of thy illegitymate desires,
and as the same Cariclia, rather purposed to receive that which is destinied to
death, then by having Alcamones deceive Theagenes, so shall these hands
first pull out this tongue, or a braundishe blade first pearce this heart, ere
I foil my name with so soul a consent.

The bird Charadrius, if one that has the king evil so her heals him
that is so diseased, but dies thereof herself: and I by this remedy rescuing
thee from sorrow, shall exalperate my bane with ten thousand griefs. If my
beauty which you say is glorious, bread these bickeringes to my great annoy
(as Dionisius and Milesius men famous in the liberal arts, because many
provoked by great report frequented their schools to learn, for envy were
banished by the Emperor, when he had no occasion to kill them) then do I wish
that either my beautye had been less and my fortune more, or else that my
fortune be agreeable to my beauty. It was death among the Delphians to steal
away a maid, O then what death deseruest thou, so disloyal a wife?

Refrain my company for the time, for it may be that often presence setts a
fether to the flight of thy affection, or marrye some gentlewoman convenient to
thy calling, for so thou shall not maculate the heroical virtue continency,
and maiste by the suauitie of her society, forget other forms that are not
for you, or if both these mislike your humor, carry the dust about thee,
wherein a male sweating has wallowed, or anoint thy self with the stale of a
mule, and this thy love will turn to hate. Follow any of these prescripts
above pres^^ed, and if thou die for love, though I could not love thee when
thou wast alive, I will honor thee being dead, Neaptolemus slain at Delphos
by Orestes Agamemnons son at the altars side, was in adoration of his virtue,
and worthy recompense of unseasonable death, every fourth year in the same
city duly honored with a devout sacrifice. But if thou chance to dye at
this present, I will not sacrifice to thy soul (which is superstition) but I
will praise thy loyalty that loved so well, and curse my hard hap that might
not requit it, and so wail thy obit with doleful tears and himnes, that if
sorrows can do thee honor, thou shall pass Neoptolemus.


Talemon. I humblye thancke your ladyship for your honorable curtosie: I am
almost as much behoulding to you, as Boristines Adrians horse was to his
master: for this horse being very ready foe hunting, that he would stay and
make ready as does a man, when he understood the chase coming, and
therewithal running very surely down the hill, and very swiftlye up the hill,
Adrian loved him so well that when he was dead, he not only caused him to be
buried with solemnizing his obseq^ies with great pomp, but commanded arich
sepulture of marble to be erected for him. Nay madam, better a (...) dog,
then a dead Lion. If in my life I obtain no remorse my pleasure will be little
after my death. O madam your counsel is not current, you give me the devil
to drink, and bids me beware poison, first kills me, and then bids me fly
death. Deny not your abilitye to the indigent and needye, Cannot she that kills
willingly save if she will? yes sure you can as quick and good; and wilful
will is eke to quick, to shed my gi^^les blood.

But I must lige deprived of you and then go seek for other booties. No sure,
hereof be sure, my life were so vn^ure, and though I live, yet so to live, were
better death endure. When I behold this corpse of yours, and your celestial
feature, the windows of mine eyes are glazed with such delight, as each new
face semes full of faults that blazeth in my vewe. Our stately dames in ^ourt,
then years do wear about their necks, to beautify themselves: but you whom I
dostrue, your pearls do wear closed in your mouth, and smiling show the same.
O Lord what foultringe of my tongue I feel, what sodeine vulnes of my wi^t when
I begin thy praises. I know the cause. Dame Pallas envious at thy fame, and
full of angry garboile, has well forecaste to dash device as often as I indite
it. I cannot greatly blame her. for if my Ladies gifts were known, Pallas
should loose her praise of price.

Were not I than more foolish to abandon thy company, then the priest
Calasiris, which being in love with Rhodopis, forsook his country gods, his
country soil; and friends; and lands, and all, to avoid the concupiscence
that might grow other presence.

A Bear blinded becomes fearrer, and I not seeing thee should wax more eger.
These thy medicines therefore, are as much to the purpose, as that good wives;
who having learned of the physician that her husband must get anappetyte, got
him straightwaye an ape and tied it to his bed, thinking that would heal him,
or hers who being taught to give her fraud bugloss, gave him blew glass that
killed him.

If thy husband loved thee not (the contrary whereof I know) this were a
revenge, and if he love thee, the fact shall be pardoned. Tertia Aemilia
perceiving her husband Scipio African to love one of her handmaides,
dissembled the matter, and would not see it, for that she would not seem to
blame her lord of incontinencie* yea after her husbands decease, she made her
free, and married her to one of her freemen: and thy Lords being of as good a
nature, why should he not use thee same manner? Sweet than, put pleading out
of place, and in his room enstall sweet peace.

Yet if I must reedifye my suit with new coi^ed soothings, and exemplify you
some by whose imitation you shall not only be pardoned, but also hent fame by
this lite deceit, call to mind the Lady Fiametta, who was so earnestlye
courted of a noble man, that she had no vent to void his suit, but by the
inioyninge of this impossibilitye. If he would exhibit her the sight of a
gardeine, odoriferous with the smell of all sorts of fresh fragraunt flowers
in the month of January, she would yield him his borne, and for the gage of
it, her faith should be the burrowe. The gentleman forechusing the event of all
hard auentures, before the loss of his Lady (like Dioclesian, which would
rather die of poison by his own hands ministered, then he would live deprived
of his Empire) took her offer: for performaunce of which, having trauerced
many a nation to search cunninge and all in vain, at last met by chance with
a sorcerer, to whom deploring with vaporous eyes his burdurus taste, and
enfeoffing him in a deed of gift in ye one moity of his lands for some
remedy, the sorcerer both promised and in short space performed this
execution. Fiametta seeing that she looked not for a gardeine in this season
with herbs, flowers, ar bours, and every thing agreaunte, neither able by
covenant to defeat the gentleman, nor willing by loyalty to defile her
husbands bed, told to her spouse the whole matter. The good man her husband
po^deringe the grateful goodwill of the gentleman, reuoluing eft his endless
triuious labors, to which the desire of his Lady did summon him, bad her with
expedition return to her friend, and pay her promise with supplye of his
pleasure.

A kingdom is as proper to an heir apparaunt by succession, as a wife to her
husband by espousall promise. Yet the Inhabitaunts in the Isle of Nerae which
bordereth on ye river Nilus, elect their prince not by regard of parentage, but
by a reverent aspect or representation of personage, and by the opinion of his
virtue and valor.

Antiochus the son of King Seleucus, fallinge in love with Stratonica his
fathers wife, and his mother in law, and very sick with concealinge it close
to him self, had by the policy of Eristrates the kings physician, his
disease bewrayed to his father, the immoderate heat of whose passion Seleucus
considering with animaduersion and the danger immiment to the defeature of his
desire, frelye forewent his proper interest, and bestowed his wife upon his
son. Then let me atteine thy love, though I sustain it with sorrow, and
depart from it afterward with my annoyaunce.

Whereas thou do comfort me in mockage, with a promised celebration of
magnificall funerals when I am dead, I swear, that in lieu of a former
beneficence, which I hope for at thy hands, I will not only publishe thy
clemency whiles I live, but when thy soul shall leave thy body as is
forefined, thy sepulcher shall be engraven with a more glorious epitaph, the^
that kings, which was writ in four pillars, placed upon the mounteine Arcadio
in the I Will of Cipres, so much renowned of M. Aurelius and of him recited. But
if my sighs, and sobs, and throws be sown in vain, O then Castibula, without
Castibula, that of Castibula houldest nothing but the outward shape, with a
conscience more cruel then Gracchus Valerius his, who for his crueltye was in
hatred with all the Romiane empire.

As of serpents teeth, which Jason flunge into the ground, there sprunge a
troop of stern soldiers with sword and shield, till time ye earthly
brothers twixt themselves to civil co^bat fell, and fought yf^re a grislye
fight: so of every denial that you denounce* shall a myllyon of cares arise
within my breast, that shall never cease to work civil wars twixt my soul
and body, till time with fearce combat the one be dispatched.

Stay nymph, and fly me not. The lambs so fly the wolves, ye Staggs the
Lions so, the doves so from the goshauks fly, and every creature from his foe.

Its somewhat from the fraughted boughs to pluck the fruit at full, and
somewhat from the tender stalk in prime to take the primrose, I am both young
and youthful yet, unspotted of beds pleasure, and would to you, O goddess
you, give up my first fruit offeringes.

The agony of my passions are like the biting of a mad do^ge, whose
rancklinge rage endurethe even unto death, O Lady show thy mercy. One snare
may easelye take two byrds, and one Goddess vsuallye hears two men's
prayers, sweet Lady show thy mercy. she that gives quickly, doubles the
gift, give me quicklye, or else I dye presentlye, good Lady for thy mercy.


Casti. dye Telamon? I pray you of what color shall your clothe be died?
trust me, if you dye any thing, I think it will be a cote of chaungeable
coulor, which I wish as hartilye as mine own welfare, and will thank you for
it as for a benefit.

I speak flatlye that thou may know my mind, that thou neither linger in
an endeles hope, nor I deceive thee with painted words. It was enacted by the
Laws and statuts of Rhodes, that none should give counsel to destr^ssed
wights, nor seem to tender their miserye with piteous words, unless they had
some remedies to assuage their calamities, and plaster the misfortunes that
deared the patients. Therefore since my former advice could not work with
you, I cannot give you any more consolation, but leave you where I found you,
to the Gods their compassion. I will not double with you in driuinge you of
with fair language, when my meaning is nothing more then to disappoynte my
appointment, and so Imitate the waterman, which lookethe one way, and roweth
another.

Marvel not Telamon, that my curtesse propice ever at your prayers, is now
found refractarye in rigor, and unlooked for of you. Lucius Clarus consul
being sent by the senate to the Oracle of Apollo, to demand what the
Romans should do, beseiged of the Frenchmen, with many tears and great
offeringes kneelinge fortye days upon his bare knees could get no answer of
the God. The cause was, as Apollo afterward told ye Roman priests, which
came of ye same message, that Lucius Clarus was an evil man, and besieched a
great demand.

Certes though it be meet that brute beasts be chastised by other brute
beasts, and the bitinge of a scorpion with the liver of a scorpion, and heinous
faults with smarting corrections: yet I will not punish you with open penalty,
neither publish your secret crimes, if you will bridle your brainsicke jade
with an hartye paenitet.

Great and abstruce is the mystery of chastity, which who so violates,
deserves no less torment in every member, then the whole body is able to
tolerate.

When a certain ship, wherein was the diesse Berecyntthia mother of the Gods,
arrived at the port of Hosty, it ran upon the sand, from whence by the space of
four days, xxx.M. men were not able to remove it By chance came thither one
of the virgins vestals named Rhea, who with her girdle tied to the shipp, drew
it to the land as easelye as she would have drawn a thread from the distaffe:
so although my beauty, as you term it, be strong to deteine you in your
doting mood, yet let the goodness of my virtuous chastity, withdraw you from
your naughtines to a love of mine honesty

You say that one snare may take two birds, and I resaye, that one garment is
not meet for many, neither shall one Castibula fit diverse men's lusts.

It were more brutishnes in thee to defloure thy friends wife, then Clodius his
incest was abomination, which discheueled his own sister: and as mad an
excuse for me to say I made the partner of my priuities, because I loved thee,
as Ca^us Blessius his was, which having conspired with Tiberius Gracchus to
subvert the common wealth, asked forgiveness of Lenates and Rutilius the
consuls, and Ladius which the^ sat in counsel with them, because he made such
acount of Tiberius Gracchus, that whatsoever he willed him do, he must needs do
it, yea though it were to burn ye Capitol. And good cause has my husband,
which now uses me as a Lady like the old Romans, then to houlde me as a
slave as the Barbariens do their wines.

An husband is to his wife like a spider in the mids of her web. For if any
part of the said copweb be but touched with the point of a nedle, forthwith
the spider feels it: And if the wives honesty be never so little cracked, she
shall inggle closelye but he will perceive it.

Trachinus a merchaunt having robbed the Phoenicians of their merchaundise,
coming ashore, commanded his men to buy whole dearies of kine, whole heardes
of oxen, and swine, and sheep, to do sacrifice, and make good cheer, and pay
for the^ whatsoever was asked at the first word: so it is an easy matter, but
most wicked, to be liberal of that which is not mine own but my husbands.

(Telamon) it befits not a gentleman to bath himself in these brothell
conceits. One of the Marranes of Italy (for so were their kings called) for
shuttinge up his gates by night, that he might sleep surely and take delight
with his concubine, was deprived of his realm. Neroes scholemaister would
often say: though I knew that Gods would pardon me, and men knew no miss of
me, yet for the villanye of the flesh, I would not sin in the flesh.

There was a law of the Romans, that nothing should be denied to women with
child of that they asked: and never any woman longed more for that she saw,
then I long for reformation in these thy most horrible detestations.

Therefore good Telamon, let reason be intercessor to freewil, to depose these
inexpiable perturbations. I cannot declare yt I feel, neither you feel that I
will declare. I am more tormented inwardly at thy sodeine assaults, then those
poor creatures were outwardly, which the cruel Alexander Fereas buried
quick, and fastened together face to face: or those among ye Scithians which
for any great fault had the Statute extended upon them, which commanded them
to be put in the bellies of Horses or Oxen sown up again, and there rot till
they were dead for those which came under the rod of the Tyrant Maxentius, of
whom Virgil entreats.

Faustine the wife of Aurelius louinge a Fencer, could not be brought to hate
him by any Apothecaries drugges, till one devised the Fencer should be closly
slain she not witting, and his blood given her warm to drink as a potion,
which indeed immediately made her loath him: so if nothing can mitigate your
affection but my utter confusion, I rather yield to dye, and give you a
confection of my blood, then so oppositelye to my foreled life, degenerate from
honesty, and derogate from mine honor.

Seneca his wife prosecuted her Husband with so dear love, yt after Seneca
was slain, she did not only offer to kill her self for his sake, if she
had not been stayed, but also after the same manner that he was slain. And let
this hasty warninge wreste she from thy suite, yt I would not only be as
desperate, if any mischaunce should befall my husband, but if it might
pleasure him, incur the like at his command therefore desiste.


Tell. As it is death to women, to be denied of that they demand: so is it
natural to them, to despise the thing is given the^ unasked.

Julius Caesar the Emperor was blamed, because (as ye cust^m was) he rise not
up courteously to the Senate, that saluted him on their knees. And I might
impute it to thee for discurtesy, that thou guerdonest not my kindness with
equiualente clemency. Yet that you may know, it is not your beauty alone
has bleared mine eyes, but your virtues rather, that have subdued my
affection, and that it may be proof how well I love you, I promise never
hereafter to solicit this suite, but moderate my self according to your mind.


This contented Castibula well, who after this used his company many a time,
and never heard word of former toies.

But wicked Telamon went an other way to work. For whereas he saw Castibula use
him more familiarly the^ all other me^, and himself in despair to atchieue
his wi h, he after some inter^ission of time, indented with 2. cut throat
v^cabonds for a 1000 crowns, to w^ite one night for Cleocritus in 1 little
grove, where he used to walk after supper in summer eueninges, and there slay
him, and that they might know when he was alone, he would give them inckling
of it. All these contriuementes were accomplished out of hand, Cleocritus
slain, the murderers fled, and never heard on, neither who, nor where they
were: great search was made, and Telamon most busye among the rest to
examine before the Justices suspected persons, and those who had been at any
odds with Cleocritus.

But woe begone Castibula misdeemed the worste, and gessed a troth, that self
Telamon was accersary to his death. But waiting for a time of revenge said
little, only closetting her self in her bedchamber, vexed her mind and body
with x. thousand cares, and in her racking torments sc^itche^ out these
words.

O day which ay shall me annoy Castibula, alas Castibula, now strain thy
howling Him^es, let grief be-wraied by grisly lays, surmounting seais^
Alcyones, and passing Pandions fowls, Not Tellus fretting at the Gods to their
despite and to our pain, vphurld more v^ly monstrous feend (whom siphon men
ar wont to call, whose bloody paws purgd Cities bounds, and skowring
scourged poor sackeles Souls) then is this No man Telamon.

O Father of Heaven, why do thou throw thy rattling boults in vain upon the
whistlinge woodds, and will not deigne to spend one Brande on this pernicious
freake, to wreake thy gawly ire?

My plighte more piteous is, then was Octauiaes erst, whose Sire was slain,
and Mother bere^te of breathinge life, of Brother eke deprived was she by
Treason not by guilt. And more unlucky is Castibula, then was unfortunate
Deianira, whose Father was at last decay, Tydeus her brother a poor exiled
Squire, Hercules her husband killed by the shirt that Nessus gave her to make
him love her (as he said) and Hill her pretty boy left a poor Orphan.

When slumber sweet in pensive parts does reign, and sleep in eyes all
tired with tears does rest, I apprehe^d Cleocritus genial face: me thinks these
arms they claspe their wonted phere, me thinks his foming foes pursue his
flighte, and then me thinks he does recoyle a back; and fenceth blasing
brondes with naked fistes. O state wherein I stand, O present pining pain. O
death, no death, but ever dying grief. With ease Electra I repeat thy case,
whose father slain, thy brother yet might venge: but nothing can revenge
this haggishe deuelishe fact.

But be sure (Cleocritus) that if Portia, Cato his daughter, and Brutus his
wife, her husband being slain among the Phillipians, when she could not get a
knife to kill her self, eat hot coals to reaue her of her life: If Turia the
wife of Quintus Lucretius put her self in deaths hazard for her husbands
sake: that thy Castibula will not be said lagge in as bold attemptes.

These matters a little ouerblowen, Telamon began to comfort^ her, and tell
great tokens of his good will, and though unworthy of such success, yet did he
crave Cleocritus his succession: Castibula was very tractable, and consented to
contract her self, thinking this a trick time to work her teene.

When the matrimonial rites were pompously consummated in the open
congregation, Castibula (as the Brides are wont) drunk to her husband, which
drink she had provided for the nouce, and brued it of deadlye poison: both of
them having drunken, in Propatulo she unfolded the primordies of his adulterous
sollicitinges, assuring them and her self, that the dart that thirld her
husband came from his device, and this was all the amerciment she could exact.
The Law of Talio is a good Law quoth she) and therefore none can attach me of
cruelty. What musings and mazinges there were in the Church, what tears and
outcryes, cursing Telamon, and playning Cleocritus and Castibula, I will not
recite.

Telamon with heavy cheer repaired home, and immediately dyed. Castibula
returned home, cast her self upon her bed, and calling for the remnaunt of her
children, Philotimus and Fuluia, bespake.

Come little Orphans, and take your last conge of your lost mother. You are a
small mease left for so great a family, and therefore my hope is, that I need
not doubt of your good demeanor after my departure. If you can agree about the
participation of your goods, and let your goodwill be interchaungeable, it
will be a bliss to your other banes, but if you disagre and serve strife,
your famelie will be ruinated, your substance dissipated, and you confounded.

Sevlurus Cheronens^s bedridden, and re^^y to appear at the Tribunal of death,
having lived the revolution of many a winter, called his Sons, (which were
80 in number, and had every one take an arrow and break it: when they had
done, he bad every one of them take a sheafe of arrows and break them, which
they could not do: Thus you see (quoth he) that the conjunction of many in
an uniform unity, cannot with ease be crushed and suffer wrecke, but every
one singled by himself is easily dissolved. The three Horatij marshalling
together in the combat, queld their cou^termarching enemies, but separated and
disioyned, were all one after an other slain by one of the Curiatii: therefore
peace which is the preservation of all cominalties, and my motherly blessing be
with you both. Let the ill luck of your parents, be a pattern to your good
life.

Ah Fuluia (for I seau^ Philotimus to his own discretion, being a man, and
Gods good direction, who I trust will man him) be courteous to all with
modesty, and coy of your honesty, but not with disdain. For many men think
you love them, if you look but fair on them, and Pride said old Briana is
the sauce of a fool.

Your father in his life time did not meanly provide for your marriage, who
espoused you here to your friend Aemilius, therefore thank God of his mercy,
obey your husband as your Lord, and only love him without countinaunce to
other. If any man shall molest you with such temptations, as latelye the
cursed Telamon meant to anticipate mine honor withal, cut him of quickly. For
a small disease delayed becomes desperate, A little spark spreading burns a
whole City, and where delay is the tree, danger is the fruit.

Thus embracing them both, and kissinge them a 1000. times, and still
blubbering on Philotimus his cheeks, with whom she could not find in her
heart to part, and taking a short farewell of all her other friends that stood
by, and commending Fuluia to Aemilius, and Philotimus to the counsel of his
Tutor, meaning to make amends to God with penitencie, for her twifolde murder
committed on her self and Telamon, prayed as follows.

O Mighty God, most great, most good, that wreakes thy wrath on them that
break thy vows, forgive my sins. O your thy precious oil into my wounded
heart, and let the drops of mercy swage the rigor of my smarte. Thy blessed
will I have despisd, thy lore forlorn, my crooked will I have disposed, thy
statutes to repeal. But now my Lord, my loadstarre bright, my former deeds
do dule my heart, and sorrow does her self submit, to take death for her
dowry: yet not that lasting death (O Lord) O God prevent that prejudice,
though merit say Amen to Hell, yet let thy mercy deigne me heaven. The
humble heart has daunted the proud mind, eke wisdom has given ignorance a
fall, and trial has taught ye folly could not find, and penitence has
cruelty her subiacent thrall. Thou that did grant ye wise king his request,
thou that in Sea thy people did preserve, thou that forgauest the wounding of
thy breast, thou that did save the Thief in state to sterue, wipe out of mind
my faults, and this new moody fact, and since with faith I fly to thee, and
hope by faith to attain desire, let prayers appease thy righteous ire, and me
enjoy thy heavenly throne.

Oh God thy name be praised, dear sire I feel thy ghostly comfort, continue
it till I be dead. O Death where is thy stinge? Hell where is thy victory? My
God will not forsake me. O Savior bless my siely babes, and cheer them with
thy grace, and sanctify their hearts, that they may glorify thy hestes. I
die, I die, sweet Lord receive my soul when I am dead.

Herewithall she yielded breath. Great mourninges were excited in every corner,
and woeful Philotimus swounding three or four times, could scarcely be
relieved with life, who in time recovered a little, compiled these herbycall
verses, to bewail his own and his sisters piteous plight now left desolate.

When root is old, or rots by decay, then woe to the branches.
when sun abates, and moon is eclipst, the^ ay to the less stars.
How can a blind man go without some guide for his ayder?
how shall a realm keep rule, unless some prince be protector?
Sigh siely heart and dye, since the head thy chief is erased,
since Gods will is to work rhy woes, serve his auowry.
Castibula buried, and Philotimus and Fuluia having compounded for the division
of their substance, Fuluia was married to Aemilius, and Philotimus hauking
after honor, and desirous to live with Aurelia, who was now a maid of honor
to the Duchess of Venice, would mauger the counsel of Senior Mondaldo, and
all his other friends, in all have post it to Venice, and there live a
Courtier. And when his former flagge of defiaunce to Venus was objected to him,
he alleged the good Emperor Traians example, who studying most painfullye
till he was sixtene years of age, then gave his Book leave to lie shut,
and practiced himself in other excercises: much more lawfully (said he) may I
be dismissed, who am now full twenty years old, or very nere.

He was no sooner arrived at Venice, but he sent for Aurelia, who accompanied
with other Gentlewomen, came to him without hunching: at the first meting their
sudden joy stopped the co^duits of their speech, but at length Aurelia saluted
her sweet heart. O friend, O sweet and my desired friend, these eyes they see,
these hands of mine thee touch, yet scarcely can this mind believe the same,
and scarcely can this bruised breast sustain the joy that is enclosed therein.
O gladsome glass wherein I see my self. Philotimus was earneste with them to
take a small pittaunce with him at Supper: Aurelia and the company would needs
comstreyne him to take the pains with them. I will not be dainty (quoth
Philotimus) such Guests as I be plenty, but easily condescend.

The Supper they passed with merry glee, and joined Mercury to Bacchus, yt is,
good communication with good cheer, on every side.

And because Philotimus was now to be initiated in the Court, mistress Valeria
one of the company and an ancient seruitour, prefined these precepts.

Let your apparel abroad be robes, though your homelye attire be rags, be
courteous with your hat, though you be currish in your heart, and if the line of
your living will not reach out, either improve your Lands, and rack your
rentes, or court some Lady and coossen her of coin, or flatter your Prince and
purloine some good office.

Philotimus answered: I wish to clime withouten crime, or not at all: I will
not wringe out wealth from others, to bring about mine own toy will. But you
use me as Clitomachus used his Spaniel, who would cast him carrion, to try his
clearnes, as you give such counsel to know my honesty.

I had forgot to tell you what graund gretinges were betwixt Parmenio and
Antigone, but as I remember these were Antigone her words. Old acquaintance
and new remembrance, welcome to ye Town with a very vengeance.

Par. O Antigone, I can tell you a tale in your ears, will do you good in
your belly.


Ant. You Sir? What occupation are you of?


Par. A Brewer, if you will lend me your fat: and thus they two varnished
their their old vain, meaning never to leave it, while their tongues were
sharp enough, and their backs broad enough, to cut with their floutes, and
bear each others frumpes.


Philotimus seemed newlye enchaunted with Aurelia, neither without good show of
reason. For what color appears by Phaeb his broken beams directly shining
upon the clouds, or mark ye color of the morning clouds before the Sun
does show, or or as the cluster and ere the grapes be ripe, with orient purple
red are striped here and there such sanguine glowed in her face. In my mind
(quoth Philotimus) Grea the chamberer of Helen, was not so happy as thy maid
Antigone, that may enjoy thy company.

And at his depature, he brake out into this exclamation. With thee is Phaeb in
love more then he was with the nimphe Leucothoe, on thee alone he fastneth
those eyes, that he owes all the world, sometime more rath he rises in the
East, sometimes he makes it late before he go to rest, and for desire to
look on thee, he does prolonge the Summer days. His light sometime does
fail among, the fancy of his faulty mind infectes his feeble sight, and
makes men's hearts afraid by daunting of his shining.

Parmenio his tongue was very fine and voluble to run over his masters whole
life this time of vacation from his mistress, wherein he showed her that she
only had the regally and pre-eminence of his heart to queme at her pleasure.

Truly I thank you always (said Philotimus to him) for your good
remembrance. I think thou kepest a notarie of my doings. Trust me quoth
Parmenio, it is small reason you should keep a dog, and bark your self.

As they were wending to their lodging, Philotimus doting on his mistress, what
say thou, said he, Parmenio, to my choice of Venus? It is not that beggarly
whorish Venus, whom Jupiter begot on Dion, but Venus whose majesty is
engrossed among the gods (as Plato deuides them in his Symposion) O Parmenio,
if thou did see with mine eyes, and could discern with my judgment,
the inestimable virtues of Aurelia: then saying would judge Aurelia to be
pearelesse, and judging would honor her as thy princess.

By my troth quoth Parmenio, if I had no better capacity to conceive then you,
I would cut of my head, and go a vowing with my shoulders. Well said Philotimus,
it is like to be a fruitful year when boys be so saucie

Philotimus was in great favor with Aurelia for the time, and as francke he
was as the Princes son of Athens, who would not enclose his granges, that
the fruits thereof might lie common to the gathering of the poor, and would
never walk without a mass of money, to give to the lame and impotent in ye
streets, and kept an ordinary table for all such as had no sustenaunce. But
two contrary planets can never abide jointly in one house, nor prosperity and
prodigality long agree.

In brief, aspiring Philotimus sparing no horseflesh, had need for his speed,
and a gulf for his port. Neither had he landed where he leueled, neither could
he launch toward his haven, when his Argent by a straung kind of
Alcumisticall art, was countercoyned to open beggerie, and Dauie debte stood
watching with a mace at the door, ready to arrest him without bale or
mainprise of any friend in the world.

One Cornelius a stale hacking Courtier, who had long fed his fancy on
Aureliaes well thewed shape, but hitherto had bare commons, for that Philotimus
had wiped all the fat from his bearde, gubd Aurelia with gold to make his fare
better, and backed Philotimus with rebukes, to joint him of his iointer.

Now did Aurelia fly and follow both at once, as every wave drives other
forth, and it which comes behind, both thrusts and is thrust it self: so
she beloved of Philotimus avoideth his demerites, and loving Cornelius pursued
his possessions.

Philotimus winding Aurelia to have munched on this carri^, and mouze no more
on his poor meat, trotted to her lodging once or twice, where she would not be
