thy vproring brawlinges, that then my being was out of thy reach: else either
had I got an odious conquest, in giving a woman the overthrow, or else had
suffered a shameful conflicte, in taking of her the Bastinado. Have thou not
heard of biblinge Phiale, Dianaes Nymph? her in all points art thou like.

But could I depart from her, who has (ah) loved me from the beginning, and
who nouer meant but continue to the end? I dare say (Laida) thou louedst me
well. Well quoth I? Yea and so well, that thou could have wished with all thy
heart, a pair of pinsons of my hyde: you are a sweet nut, the Devil crack
you.

I had no cause to doubt thy flinching: for whithersoeuer I had gone abroad, I
might have carried thee in my cap.

Thou know (Laida) that the lees and dreggs of wine draw always to the
bottom: yet if honey be put into a wine vessel, the lees as ouercomen with
the honey, rise up to the top, and the honey takes their place: even so, thou,
which in foretime have enjoyed the deepest part of my heart, though thou were
as sour as lees, as soon as the honnie Aurelia, exhibites herself, must by
necessity brook to yield thy self. Lay the Crystal stone against the Sun,
and put any thing nigh it that is combustible, and incontinently it is set on
fire: such is the potence of Aureliaes virtue, that whomsoever it approaches,
immediately it inflams him. He that sees such a game before his eyes, and will
not let thee slip, has small skill in hunting.

As for me, I am not without the stone Tabrices, a stone, whose color is
consonant to the Crystal, which Euax and Aaron, and the old Philosophers say,
gives favor, eloquence, and honor, to them that use to bear it about.

Small drink seems stronger to one that is fasting, then to one that is full:
so Laida, every sip of thee seemed to be sauced with a pound of sugar, till I
had tasted of Aureliaes banquet, and then it seemed to have neither taste nor
relish. And no wonder, though thy sweetenes be so soon sowred. For sweet
milk, and sweet blood, and other sweet things, are soon altered in
concoction, and when the stomach assayeth to digest them and can not, then
turn they immediately to be sowred: Even so, thou: But apply this parable to
thy self, I have no leisure to intend thee from Aureliaes business, and any
time is lost that is lent a thankles person.

To wind up all in a short conclusion, this thou haste heard, ye hot wines
ingendex cold diseases: and this I tell thee, that my scalding love is turned
to cooling hate.

They aee evil horsed, and worse wiued, that ride on coltes, and marry young
giglittes: both these I will avoid as nere as I may.

Neither thine, nor his own, Cornelius.


To avoid all tedious prolixitie, because many more things will occur to be
spoken on, know you, that Cornelius and Laida, ever after this lived as
denounced enemies, sildome meetinge, but he with powting, and she with
flowting, incensed many a knarrie jar

But poor Philotimus he was tried with alll tribulations, he was abused of
every refuce rascal. Cornelius and Aurelia to excuse themselves, accused him
of trecheries, of coosenage, of every villainy that may be named, which the
rather was believed, because he now was fallen to poverty, and so constrained
to live hardly. His creditors were most of them such, as had wasted ye
revenues of his lands, and now were most importunate for the discharge of
his debtes. Philotimus did often send Cornelius his daring challenge (though
his strength were far inferior) to meet him in any convenient place, which
the coward would not be brought to do.

Thus loaded with more grief, then can be borne with Geometry (whereon the
body of man does hang) and sendinge out more sighs, then can be numbered by
Ciphers, or told by Arithmeticke (wherein man's Soul consistes) the poor
forlorn Gentleman entered his chamber, where shutting the door, he closely
with himself debated his distres in this sort.

Ah Philotimus, thou wretched wighte Philotimus, that can neither live with
credit, nor die with honor, cursed be the hour wherein thou wast got, a
cloud dim that day wherein thou wast borne, damned be the parents that
brought thee to light, and whelmd be the World with fire and brimstone, that
with rebellious insurrections, and inordinate commotions, all and every one
have conspired my decay.

Am I that Philotimus, whose bare word was wont to be of more weight, then
ten thousand pounds of some man's money, and now am called in question of petty
shameless coosenage, which (O Lord) thou know how much I abhor? Am I that
Philotimus, whom all my friends have ye best flower in their garla^d, and now
am cast out for a rotten stinking weed, trod under foot of every
passenger? O Lord save my credit from shipwreck, and bring it to safe harbor,
where taken as a wrecke of ye Sea by way of escheate, in time it may be known
how unspotted it is. Thou haste all men's hearts in thy hands to dispose at
thy pleasure, forget my transgressions, mortify my affections, and renew
their good opinions, who now despair of me.

What is becomd of him, that sometime was the Gem of all ye Jewels in Athens,
then praised of all, to encourage him to virtue, now prased for of some, to
restrain him from vice? Is he not now thought a sensual reprobate, deuorced
from all piety, lulde in security, given up of God to his palpable lusts,
with the bitt in his teeth to flinge where he list, like an harbourcles
vacabond, or a brother of Caine?

My long acquainted friends, combined to me with egall faith, why fly ye touch,
why start ye from me? If I have taken stand, why cast ye not forth your wonted
lures? Or if as yet I be not well mande, why make ye no labor to reclaim me
from haggardnes? Arraigne me at the bar of Seurre judgment, exaggerate my
crimes with amplification, impannell an inqueste of Russet coat Robbins, let
Cautle Subteltie be the foreman, and Summum^ius the Judge, and Cuthbert the
cutthroat commense his action, what amercement or penalty can they assign
me, or wherein can they say I have offended, that should alienate any man
from former liking? Indeed I have wastfully spente (more caytiffe I) the
surrender of my fathers lands, and run my self into desperate debtes, and now
in steed of blew coats to wait at my table, have a couple of Sergeants to
attend me through ye streets, that I slip not the collar. But of what sparkles
did this heat glow, or from whence did these exspences take their Exordium? to
glut mine own insatiable appetites, or to feed the ve^ues of foisting mates,
who never held out fiste while I had a groate, that they pulled it in empty,
and now not only all of them have abandoned my company, but some that have
soaken many an angel out of my bag, if they reckon but two pence that I
borrowed ^t theirs, are ready to send out process to attache my body?

True is the Proverb, save a Thief from the gallows and he will be the
first shall do thee a mischief. I have clothed these ragamuffins, I have
fed these clammed michers, I have been their harbinger, when they had not a
cave to couche their cockscombes in. I thought their boulstered names had
stuff to show, ye praise did hight. I raisde a star whereto direct my course,
in whose prospect my tackle faild, my compass brake. I threshed for corn, and
all is turned to chafe.

O that I had either been Zopyrus, to have disciphered their falshoode by
Phisiognomye, or they had been such as Socrates, to have changed their
crooked natures by education. I have red that if Basil be put ^ouertlie
underneath the dish where a woman should eat, her queaste stomach straightway
lothes it. O that I had such a virtue to have stayed my friends falsehood. But
letters that are made of the powder of Rochalum, can not be red but in clear
water, and those that pack their cogging in a cloak of dissembling, cannot be
descried but by a wary forwatch. Adrianus painted grapes so artificially, that
birds pecked at them, neither could any descerne them, but with diligent
marking. The Hollin tree bears bark, and berries, the one kills birds, the
other feeds them: and such fellows bear aungells in their faces, and devils
in their devises, the one enticeth good natures to follow them, ye other
entangles them in their deceites.

Because I have dug mine own ground, till I come at the clay, and now ask
some licour at my neighbors Well, they not only deny me with a snub, but
out of my hearing r^^ile without cause, and make ladders on my back for
Slander to climb on.

Erect your Trophies (Captains) you have got a goodly conquest, and gained
knighthood this day, by bringing a sackles soul into a fools paradise, as
Clytus taking three or four ships, called himself Neptune. When a man's
hose be down, it is easy to kiss him where he sat on Saturday. Your scoffs
be out of season, far from the documents of Euangelical Gospellers, which
preach both in season and out of season, but taking an holier text then any of
you, and affoording a little more profitable notes.

Hanno with durable pains, taught birds which he pent in a cage, in time to
cry Deus est Hanno, Hanno is a God, and you with crafty forged dealing, have
(ah Craft) made the common sort extol you to the skies. Because you would
cast of the yoke that is laid on your neekes to be the means of my ruin, you
bear them in hand when you are in corners, that as the Salamander is cause of
her own death, by spirtinge out the fire which does nourish her: so I with
prodigal expenses, not procurd bp any of you, have ruinated my state, and
brought it to this stay: and therefore have I foultred, because I followed not
you.

Woe to the Geese that have the Fox for their priest, ay for the Lambs that
are nursed of the Wolf, in the ditch falls the blind that is led by the blind.
It is hard to draw water in a dry soil, or fish out skill from an empty
ponde. As to be heard where ears are none, or Lead to be graven in Marble
stone, so hard it is to hear counsel of you, which may accord with any good.

Good souls, you have nothing within, but gutts and garbage, neither heart,
nor liver, nor any good entrails. Go sleep in a Trunk with Clearchus, catch
flies all day with Domitianus, or go to the plough with ye Aracosians. You may
sooner be Doctors at Dawes cross, then Sergeantes of the Quoife, and commense
Coridons with your clownish conditions, chen be created Counselors for your
policy which is none.

Well, the time which was, will be (says Plato) where he speaks of hi^ great
year, and I my self hope for a year of Iubile. The Sun which falls in the
West with Eclipse of his light, rises in the East with his fiery garland: high
springes may cease from swelling, but never dry away. In the mean time though
they speak me as fair, as Judas did Christ the same night that he betrayed
him, can a Gentleman's courage put up this injury? Shall they spurn and I not
kick? I will use mean and maine, sack their souls, and ransack their
dwellinges, and send them vengeance with advantage, I will assail to scale
their fort, or else will smell of smoke. The banks will undermine, if the Sea
flow not over, and if my fury have no vent, my heart will cleave asunder.

Nay, since they say what they list, they shall hear what they list not.
Though I be an Ass, and borne to bear, yet they shall snaffle my tongue to
wrappe it up in silence. Thy tongue Philotimus? A goose gagles, and an hen
cackles, and will thou gain the game with tatling?

No, send them thy gauntlet, challenge the combat, wage battle, match their
malice, Innocence bears a shield to break their blows. Pursue the
fugitives, make havoc of them, hail them by the hairie scalpes up and down
the streets, chop them as small as flesh to pot, let not a parcel of their
memory remain by the remnaunt of their ashes.

O Philotimus, thou have eaten bulbeefe, and braggest highly: you touchest
near, but draws no blood. Every hooked nose is not a conqueror: few cursed
kine have long horns. Where be thy burgonie, thy beauer* thy collar, thy
corslet, thy powldringes, thy tasies, thy vollie of shot, thy planted
ordinance, thy casted mounts, with all thy garrison of well araied soldiers?
A rustie rapier is no trusty rampire. It is not a sharp sound, that proves a
full battle. A Caesar's mind, and a Codrus might, can never agree.

Tush, thou art like a Thief, that thinks every Tree a true-man. Thou may
as well be feareles, as he that holds ye wolf by the ears: new wine will
seaech to find a vent, and wit will walk where will is bent: when the wind
is not in a good coast, we must ship our ^ares and further our course: venture
and conquer. Have thou never a knacke in thy so^s head, never a shifting shoo
of an Irish Hobbie? Can thou not feign a hot mouth, when thou have a cold
maw, sound the trumpet of great friendship, and then begin the skirmishe with a
false alarm? If thou can not compass thy whole desire, be content with
that which may come to pass, flatter for advantage, and be not ashamed (quoth
Phormio) in Terence. Go burning sighs unto their frozen hearts, go break
the yse with pitties painful darts: and when they are within thy reach, give
them the cup of bitter sweet, to pledge their mortal foe.

Then may thou chaunt thy cheerful chance, but interim, thou chats about
mooneshine in the water. Thou shalt as soon make music with a puddinge and a
capcase, as a current gloze of this device. Climb the Sun by the beams, and
any thing thou say, begin to assay it.

Alas, what helps the dial the blind, the clock the deaf, or wit him that
wantes opportunity? they hate thee, and shun thee, and will not now hear of
thee. Though the Mariner have cunning to govern ye vessel, it lies not in his
skill to calm the Seas. It is enough to point to the straw where the pad
lurkes. The smell of the Radish killes the Serpent, and therefore they avoid
it: The smell of burnt Penniriall kills fleas, and therefore they cannot abide
it: And they, because they know thy craft, will beware of thy trains. Onions
and Garlic sown near Roses, though they touch them not, draw all the gross
and sour moisture into themselves: and though at this time they are not in
thy bosom, yet have they the Art optic to search out thy drifts. They will
know thy visage by thy vizarde, thy pretended subteltie by thy counterfeit
simplicity.

Well then, since this is a sauce too sharp for thy diet, and these be herbs
too strong for thy nose, leave of this Phrygian melody that incenseth to
battle, and take the Aeolian harmony, which appeaseth the tempest ot the
troubled mind. Thy taint is past anointing, thy burnt is past balming, no
remedy remains, but death must be surgeon of thy sore. Come death, and throw
thy pearcing dart into my panting breast: Death is a port, whereby we pass to
joy: life is a lake that drowneth all in pain. A chief relief to conquered
men is desperately to die. Adieu delights that lulled me asleep: farewell my
joys, and dulced bed of rest: sweet were the joys that would both like and
last: strau^g were the state exempt from all distres.

Happy was I (that was a woeful word to saine) ah happy was I, that now am
most miser. No miser Philotime, wast thou ever in thy life, and that at this
time makes the most miserable. The stricken Dear withdrawes himself to die,
and so will I. Brutus will first choose to make a Cutlers shop of his own
bellye, with his own hands, before he will fawn for his enemies favor: Lucrece
receiving a private skar, would never live with open scorn: let these be
preparatiues to thy speedy dispatch. Do thou not remember them, that hearing
Platoes Phaedon of the souls immortality, cast themselves headlong into the
Sea, to rid them of their lives, and enjoy the souls pleasure?

Auaunt Satan, thou preuailest not. Though thou set me upon a pinnacle, and
offer all the kingdoms that are in the world, thou loosest thy labor: my God
will defend me from this extremetie. Shall I pull down this bodily building,
that his Fatherly providence has erected to his own similitude? No (says
Plato) and he after Plato, in his book entitled Scipioes dream, A prisoner
committed to ward by commandment, may not shake of his shackles when he
list, nor come out of prison but by authority. Since his Godhead has bestowed
this corporal mancion on a sinful wretch, and framed me likewise after his
caracter, I will not dissolve it as though I mislikt it, but continue his
goodness with thanksgeuing.

Aristotle ouercharging his fantasy, with too subtle a search beyond his
wits reach, did himself to die, to his own conde^natio^ and hinderaunce of
us* Judas and Saul may terrify and edify, judging themselves by their own
censures, and warning us all to fear, but with faith. No violence is
permanent, and Opinion is sometime of impossibilities: the one may persuade us
that the other slakes grief, but the other deceiving with deluding
contrariety, shortly returns to his former recourse. The corruption of one, is
the generation of an another. Our misery in mortallitie, which is but
momentany, be it dispossessed without Gods appointment, newly begets, and
renewes a fresh, a never parting pains in the life to come.

Then let inward spirit better outward speech (as Alcibiades speaks of
Socrates in Platoes symposium:) there meaning to commend his master Socrates,
he compares him to Syleni, as they sat in the Ingrauers shops: for without
they were ingraued with pipes and instruments, but being ripped and opened, they
had the Images of Gods. Ulysses could forbear his maids choakinge checkes for
a certain proof of suspected probabilities: Etrascus loving thirty years,
could not atchieue his hearts desired choice, yet at ye end, found reward of his
mistress. Achilles for a time fair Brysi^s did forgo, and yet they met again,
the^ think you may do so: the Lord may restore thee to former state, the^
first seek his kingdom, and all shall be ministered. If he have given thee a
bill of diuorcement, never to join issue with wealth nor pleasure, he requires
no more service but prayer for patience, and constancy in this service, till
thy Audit day in heaven.

It may be, he has brought thee trom bench to bar, to plead for thy safety
with care and sobriety, least being exalted to sit on the bench, thou had
judged thy self with dissolute haughtines. As ye yongman Tuscus, by Petrarches
report, seeing his feature, as it were a lawnd for beawties bloudhounds there to
draw, which was perilous for his credit, and dangerous for their safety,
thinking rather to make grief of his gain, the^ that should hurt any which
could help none, all to gashed his face with voluntary knockes, to deforme
himself, and defraude them.

Far therefore be that from me, which Epiucrus thinks in Tullies books of the
nature of the Gods, that nothing is governed by the providence of God, because
says he, those yt be immortal, take no pains, but being idle and vacant,
trouble not themselves with any manner meddling. No, not a leaf falls to the
ground without Gods good leave. Therefore let every part either in Commodie
or Tragedy be willingly played, as he shall assign.

Ah doting Dido, sie pholishe Phillis, unfortunate Camma, misborne Medea, more
cruel then Atropos, to untwine your fatal threedes. What mente ye to
disinherit your lawful tenauntes for term of naturalll life (your bodies I
mean) more easelie disioynde, then knitt together, as Cato says in Tullies
Oldage? Though every one of you had silver feet, as Philostratus in Heroicis
reports of Thetydes in these words Guropes a thetis: Though you had such
grace in your eyes, as Homer reports of Juno: Though you had Jove's hair,
which he speaks on in his first Ilias: or brave Achilles his, when Pallas
coming down from Heaven at Iunoes request, pulde him backwarde by the golden
hair, which Homer calls Zanthen comen. Yet if a man should make Epigrams on
your best virtues, your praises would be as mean, as Euphorbus his the
Trojans* which Homer did give him when he was a dying, in whom he bewailed
nothing but his fair ruff of hair.

Well then, though I am loath to unload my deeringe cares to my dearest
friends, and bewray all my wantes whereof I am ashamed, yet it is virtues lore
to seek means to live, and war^e ventering shall give the onset. I will write
to Aemilius, our au^cie^t friendship is yet but memorable, and my good welfare
to him is estimable, he is my best friend, and my best belouing brother, and
therefore I know he will give me assistaunce.

Then taking pen in hand, in which for fear ye ynke did seem to freeze, for
that he blushed, and was abasht to beg, he wrtt to his brother of all his
distresses, contriuing his Arguments into these two heads: That for the
indissoluble bond of their sacred sworn friendship, and natural regard of their
near alliance, he hopte without pausing he would send him purueyaunce, and
renew ye state of his passed life with the imparture of his new relief.

His page Parmenio, which never departed from him, was the carrier of these
letters, and for want of an horse poasted on foot. M. Aemilius was aghast at
the tenure of these letters, and half and more ashamed to retain such a
guest, and so far he was from ayding his ill apaide brother, that he charged
Fuluia his Wife, and Philotimus his only sister, not to meddle with Philotimus.

Nay (quoth he) the Crow that has no care of her own birds, will not tender
the nestlinges of blackebirdes: the Be that cannot feed of the sweet of the
honysuckle, will hardly make honye of the juice of the Thistle: he that sees
not his blemishes in a Crystal glass, will not blot them out by looking in
black marble: when hemp will not spring in a fertile ground never tilled, it
will not grow and flourish in a dried heath: since he could not keep pace, when
his legs were lithe and leenie, and his horse and footecloth at his command,
how can he outrun his fellows, when his limbs are all lamed, though you lend
him a crutch to lean upon? His answer he returned in this order.

Aemilius to the luckles Philotimus.
Poor soul Philotimus, God send thee more wit then thou use in begging,
and to bag better alms, then thou gettest by bragging: but he that spendes
all, deserves the like blanke, and a proud hearted beggar, has sildome proved
better. Whilst you study on the stars with lofty looks, and scale the
skies with taratant terms, you stumble at a straw for want of good footing,
and diue in the ditche, where I fear you stick fast. Had you craved my help
with humiliate submission, it had caused your health, and medcined your moning:
But you entreat me not, as I am your better, but charge me with duty,
because I am your brother. It follows as well of our fraternity, that I
disburse money to acquit you of debt, as that Arthemioes nurses son should
succeed in the Argiues kingdom, because he sucked ye same milk that Arthemio
did, who deceased intestate without an heir apparaunt. Neither consanguinitie
of brotherhood, nor aunciety of acquaintance, shall seduce me from my
caueates, or reduce me to your cautels. For friendship has no form, when his
matter is perished (which is honesty) and brothers have freewil, not custom
to constreyne them. By the same steps you came to beggerie, return till you
come to your mineral of riches, and when you amend, I will chaung my mind.
Farewell.

Your friend that was willingly, and unwillingly is your brother, Aemilius.


These were tidinges vnbethought on of Philotimus, no sooner heard, but taken
to heart. Yet though his best joint were ^rased, he would not for choler
deface his other members: though his likliest graft did bear no fruit, he did
not condemn and contemn all his plants: though in the fairest goblet he had
found poison, he would not refuse to drink in a meaner piece: imitating Hylus
ye Persian, who defrauded of his Lordships, by his nearest friends, used the
means of alians to obtain a kingdom.

Philotimus then having recordation of an old companion of his in the Court,
who friendly had professed much, and was proficient in nothing (as occasion
gave proof) thought, that since there was one ace left that might do him
good (though he had mist of that cast, which might have won the game without
co^tradiction) to try the dice, which never were constant, and consequently
with confidence setting pen to paper, writ these few lines.

Philotimus to his friend Erogazus.
GOod friend Erogazus, health to thy person, and wealth to thy friends. O
Erogazus, I now must write thee woonders, and crave thee for my Sanctuary. My
plenty has bred me poverty: my fair and sunnie downye day, has all beburnt
my hew: my strength has given me a fall, much like Narcissus fate, whose
too much beauty was a bait, to catch him in the brook. My wealth was
weary of his welfare, and never deignde to wowe that Lady Wit, till Woe
entistte it neither to despise nor spite her hestes. I am now more vncouthly
transmuted, from my ancient state, then he who with looking on Mineruaes
naked side, did loose his shape, and was transformed to a Beast.

The blind Poliphemus did not more mournfully and wastfully cry unto his loved
fair Calatea, then I a poor blind forlorn Gentleman both of friends and
affection, do with dole and despair, call till I cough, but without an
answer, to my Lady Aurelia.

Aurelia (dear friend) has forsaken Philotimus. But why? God knows, not I,
except it be for this, to make me a pattern to my peers, of fortunes frailnes
and beauties lightness, and herself a sampler to light huswiues, of love for
living, and inconstancy in love.

My creditors were not so ready to lend me money, as they are unreasonable
for a sudden dischardge: in this resemblinge the Epycures stomachs, who
having a great Service brought to the table at once, would not eat any meat
but having every (...) brought seuerallye by it self, like inglun^ous
corm^rauntes gu^d up all: so they in my time of harvest would ^^k me no co^^e,
but in the depth of winter, will needs have a crop.

My lands are morgaged, my demanes leased, my apparel pawnd, nothing wanting
in me to the accomplishment of all misery, but that I wear no gives, with
which they think to gin me: for my chamber is a prison, and I pend up in it,
out of which I dare not look, least some evil eye look on me.

My solace in this agony is too sorrowful to recite, and my pleasures want a
name, unless Pain be their nickname. My Lute lies mute, my Cytharin sad,
my Virginalls (whose quauering notes Aurelia often has caused) being bereft of
her that was a virgin saint as they thought, are iarring out of tune. If
Thales (who with music cured the sick of the plague) or Zenocrates (who with
melody helped frantic lunatickes) should make me harmony, I should rather
think the Gods had sent me this joy for my further pains, then take delighte
of their lays with my discordant ears: so often have the Gods deluded me with
doatinge, so disturbed is my mind with disordinate carkinge.

In this extremetie, I crave thy help to appease my creditors, and ease my
pains: and though my ability be skant to counteruaile your cost, yet
inconstant fortune will once batter for better, and then a grateful mind shall
requite with double guerdon. I am not ignorant, how little thou owe me, nor so
impudent to demand ought of duty: yet must I commit an inconvenience, to
prevent a mischief, and for ancient benevolence, crave your benificence. Few
words may suffice a willing mind, and one syllable is too much for an
unwilling miser. Farewell (good Erogasus) on whom only I cast the ancker of
my care. I expecte thy speedy answer, and hope for present help.


What entertainment these letters of entreatie had, you may judge by Erogazus
his awnswere, which follows.

Erogazus to Philotimus.
THe malignant nature of thy entice malady, does notify the danger of thy
disease, and warnes us to decline his perilous contagions. The infected of the
plague, do naturally covet to contaminate others: the Drone deprived of his
wings, does seek to clippe the Bees of theirs: when the Raven feels herself
draw near to death, she kills her yongones to lead the dance: and I suppo^e
(Philotimus) thou think it a comfort in thy misery, to have a companion in
extremity.

If one trea^ bar-footed on the bird Drymus, forthwith his skin comes of, and
his legs swell, and they also that handle him which is hurt, lose their skin:
the crystal waves that beats against the muddie rock, do purge the rock,
but do pollute themselves with filth. Alphenor offering to rescue Phedimus and
Tantalus ystrick with pearcing shaft yfere, and lying groueling on ye ground,
was gride himself through bulcke, with dint of deadlye dart: your part must be
minsed by these comparisons, and your festered wounds you wish me swadle up,
must seek another surgeon, or remain without remedy.

Though the River Peneon run in the Lake Titaresis, yet is it never mi^ed with
it: though for old acquaintance sake I can take pains to see you brought to
the gallows, I mean to be no partner of your ha^ter.

As the Colt will not be brideled, so the Ass must be spurred: as no rule
could rain you, whiles you were in your huffe: so skant must tame you, when
you are in your need.

To beg, is no way to get my friendship. If you will keep friends, you must put in
practice this old delectorie. Give, take, seek, all things, few things,
nothing, else you may put your flock in ye common, but it shall hardly find a
shepherd, and seek for an alms, but find Gods seath For proof whereof,
because you shall believe me, I my self will be as provident to keep my right, as
you were nothing wise to demand without reason. Farewell good Philotimus, I
have not for you.

Yours if he might, Erogazus.


When Pilotimus, had red these letters, ah wretch (quoth he) that have no end
of thy goods, nor beginning of goodness, no cause to spend thy money on thy
self, nor honesty to lend it to thy friends, that had rather reserve thy
coin to a needeles use, then deserve well of others, with parting from a
penny: the mothes eat thy money, the worms wroot thee out, and utter decay
be both your destinies. Now when he see no truth, neither in lover, nor
brother, nor friend, divided as it were into choler and grief, he writ this
Pamphlet following.

Mihi creed, credendum nemini.
HE whose trust has wrought him treason, which treason causeles, has brought
him misery, warns all good natures to beware by his folly, and gives the^ this
pamphlet for a friendly caveat.

Vouchsafe to read it, though you ride him that made it, and if (as I wish)
it be to your benefit, (which shall be my comfort) let it not miss to be my
bale, which is my custom.

Rifle it to the bottom, though it be but a trifle, fools bables may serve to
make wisemen sport: search it to the sinews, and try out his entrails, though
his stature be small, his heart may be big: leave what you like not, and believe
what you list, you have scope to escape harmles: It is my theames bidding to be
dainty in choosing, respect or reject to your private contentmentes.

Thus under protection of your friendly benevolence, and undoubted ascertaunce
of willing attention, I will prove my purpose manifestly, by discoursing
dispersedly, through al or the most creatures extant in the world, and least I
incur that idiots fault, which intending to write ye life of Priamus, began
many degrees of with his great grau^dfathers predecessor, and so spent his pith
ere he came to his purpose, to avoid the like, and needles superfluity, I
begin as follows.

BE sure to those to whom you plight troth, but be slow to trust any with reposed
confidence: for he yt makes breach of his passed protestation, is far worse
then Pythius the deceitful Siluersmith of Syracusa: and he that trustes too
far, though upon some trial, is like the foolish Beara^d, which having tamde
his Bear, will thrust his hand into his iaws, or venture so far within his
gripes, that sometimes he is urged to cry for help

ARct seems the knot, and indissoluble ye bond of mutual goodwill twixt ye
parent and the child. Yet Tullia Tarquins daughter for couetize of the empire,
not only berefte her father of life, but in spite made her couch to be drawn
ore his body, and with her trampling Iades bruised his corpse. Seruius Tullus
was molested of his children: Zan^ppus persecuted his Father Pericles: Oedipus
was imprisoned of Eteocles and Polinices: Bassianus sought the death of his
father Seuerus.

Although Aristotle say S. Ethicke^, that Love descendes rather th^n ascendes,
that parents love children more then they requite it, ^et does not this love
always indiuidually concomitate and follow parents. Justinius the abridger of
Trogus Pompeius writes, that the wife of Demetrius for desire she had to
reign, betrayeth one of her sons, who was an obstacle to her enterprise, and
by that horrible fact dismissing all motherly affection, when another son of
hers named Grippus had recovered his fathers dominions, she, as though she had
been imbased by this conquest, attempted to embrew her hands in the blood of
him, although it happened most diametrically opposite: for offering him a cup
of deadly poison, when he came thirsty from exercise, he of duetiful courtesy
desired her to begin, and urged her so far, that he found but great proofs
of her intended purpose, wherewith the Queen being overcome, poisoned herself
with the potion which she had provided for her son. Phaenix deflowred a
damsel, whom his father Amintor kept in cube, and did affectionat, for which
cause, his sire thundering bitter curses, inuoking the fell condemned furies,
bequeathinge to his hire the hateful harms of Hell, and wishing such a son
might never be sire, or beget babes to be his nephews, whom he might cherishe
in his bosom, he fled from his fathers malediction, and left the hope of all
his wealth in Hellade, where then Amintor reigned. Meleager, when the turretts
of his native City Calidon were battered by crewes of Cu^et land, could not,
having taken a little displeasure, either be cal^^ed with the Lords fair
offers, or be entreated with the Cleargies prayers, or be induced by his
Fathers beseechinges kneeling on his knees, to save the City, till time,
paste time almost, not for their socour sake, but of his own disposition, the
toy took him in the head.

If that were law now, which was lawful in Sophocles his time, for children
to disposesse their doting parents, we should find, I doubt not, no small
multitude, that with Sophocles his sons, would go about to prove their
fathers fools, that they might enjoy their lands and goods. I cannot forget
here to insert the condemnation of these children reluctant to nature, by ye
char^e piety of the storke to their dams, who relieve them in age with food,
of whom in youth they received satiate sustentation: and the godly duty of a
poor woman, who (as Valerius writes) having her mother delivered by the
Praetor unto the Iayler to be executed in the prison as a malefactor, and a
straight mandate given, that none should visit her (saving that her daughter by
the gentleness of the Iayler, got leave to come to her, who notwithstanding was
narrowly searched that she brought no food, because he purposed to kill her
with famine) a great while aslaked her mothers hunger with the milk of her
breasts: the Iayler merueyling how she lived so long, at length perceived the
honest guile, and reporting the straung fact to the Counsel, procured her
pardon.

When the brood of the Pellycane waxes hoar and haughty, they violently smite
their dams, and grievously wound them. Nero made a funeral corse of his
natural mother, and this Tiger Nero, a straung begotten swain, was preferred
by Claudius to the Imperial throne, before his own son Britannick.

BRethren descending from the same loins, having copulation of near
consanguinitie, should not, me thinks, degenerate from kindness, and turn
native Love to defiant Hostility. It were straung in these days, to see their
natures perverted, but that it has been so among our forefathers: yet it was
no wonder in their golden days, for so it has been from ye worlds first
foundation.

Petosiris the Junior son of Calasiris, Priest at Memphis, by guyle meant to
defraude his elder brother Thyamis of the succession of his Fathers Priesthood,
for the which they sought a combat, or at least should have fought, if
Petosiris his heels had not served him better, then both his hands and heart
were able to do. The Empire of Rome being left in even moytie, to the two
brethren Bassianus and Geta, Bassinus to the intent to have the sole regiment,
fraudulently butchered his brother Geta. Abimilech slew threescore of his
brethren that he might reign alone, when only Ionathas by Gods provision,
scapte the sckowring.

Record the quarrels of Marcus and Lucius, Titus and Domitian, Tyberius and
Germanicus, Numitor and Amulius, Saturn and Titan, who upon the point of
commandment and government were not as though they had been brethren, but
maintained wars as most cruel enemies.

Ovid says in the first of his Metamorphosis, that the violence of the
boisterous winds (which brothers are by kind) are so turmoilous and so cruel,
that every one is pent pur partiewise in sundry place: and therefore to the
gray Morning, and to the realm of Nabathie, and Persis, and other lands far
underneath the morning star, did Eutus take his flight: the setting of the
Sun, and shutting in of night, belong to Zephyr, that braies his balming
breath: the blastes of blustering Boreas reign in Scithia, and in other lands
under Charlsis wayne: to Auster is appendaunt all the Sotherne coast, who
bears showers and mists continually in his mouth.

FEw Husbands marry wives, unless misled with lust, but with some foretriall
of their honest manners, and none there be after long time of marriage, but
having found them honest, trust their demeanor: yet give ear what I will
tell you. When Hidaspes King of Ethiopia had been married to Persina the term
of ten years, who in all this space had not conceived, and was known honest
from all other, at length with great joy she was pregnant by him: in the act
of which generation, Persina looking on the white picture of Andromada naked,
brought forth (contrary to ye Ethiopian color, a child very fair and of
singular beauty: the mother vewing this wonder in her child, and fearing her
husband would suspect ye wrong, yt such a colored child could not be of his
getting, to avoid ignomie, was driven to this shifte: She laid the young one
forth in a certain private place, exposing her to the unstable state of
fortune, placing her Fascia by her, to declare what she was, and store of rich
Jewels, that the finder might foster her: all this done, that the King might
not see her, Persina told the king that his daughter was dead: see here, how
little Persina credited her husbands disposition, who never had showed her any
unkindness, neither could she in herself find any cause, that might offend him.

I have hard of a certain married wife, who at certain moons would feign
herself mad, that under that pretense she might do what she list, and
accompany whomsoever best pleased her humor, her name was Paephaca. Arsace the
great kings sister of Persia, marrying Oroondates a noble Gentleman, and the
kings deputed Lieutenaunt at Memphis, of how many far inferior to her
calling, was she enamoured? First of Thyamis, after of Theagines, and of others
many mo. This Arsace was accustomed to say that, which most women prasume upon,
that in her greatest trespasses, Oroondates would be pacified with one
flattering welcome, or the least tear wrunge out of her eyes.

Who was more meek then M. Aurelius, yet who ouermatched with two deceitfuller
queans? If a woman hold an opinion, who can draw her from it? warn her of her
fault, she will not believe you: give her counsel, she will not take it: If
you look at an other, then is she jealous: if you love her, then she disdaines
you: threate^ her anon she complains: flatter her, she waxes proud: rejoice
not in her, then are your surly: if you forbear her, it makes her bold: and if
she be chastened, she turns to a Serpent: finally a woman will never forget
injury, nor give thanks for any good deed. Though Claudius were a cruel
king, Hanniball a meek king, Alexander a valiant king, Phillip a wise
king, yet was Claudius cooled with his wife Agrippina, Hannibal kindled with
his Thamira, Alexander his courage abated with Rosane, and Phillips wisdom
sotted with Olympias.

Yet Cecilius Balbus says, that one obiecting to a Gentleman named Damelius,
that he had a stinking breath, and he chiding with his wife for not telling
him of it before, truly (quoth his wife) so would I have done, but yt I
thought all men's breaths had smelt in ye sort, and therefore it is like she
never joined mouth to any man, and yet it is red, that the wives of certain
Lacedaemonians (who were taken by the Spartanes, and imprisoned) coming into
prison to them, changed apparel, and by that cloak intruded themselves into
danger, and sent out their husbands in habit of women, muffled as though they
had wept for their distressed Husbands.

Homer says, that Juno the wife of Jove, Neptune his brother, and Pallas his
daughter, had conspired to bind his hands, and throw him down the Heavens,
had not Thetis descending, caused Briareus, called Aegeon, the hundred handed
Giaunt, climb up, who fearcely there resiaunt in their sight, did so agaste
both Seaishe God, and all their route, that Jove did escape this bickering.

THere is some regard of one kinsman to an other, more then of an aliaunt, or a
transmarine stranger: yet would I wish you, to use your kinsfolks, as on
should use a fair staff made of reed, which he may carry in his hand, to
fear his enemies, but if he lean to it or use it, it snaps in sunder. If you
chance to prosper, your kinsfolks will use your countenance for their
credit, and others will fouourably and gently entreat you (as it was a custom
in Rome, none to be put to Justice, but first the ancient books should be
searched, to se if any of his predecessors had done service to Rome, whereby
the captive should merit to have pardon of life: whereupon Marius Consul
triumphing of Iugurtha King of the Numidians, and Bochus King of Mauritane, who
favored and aided Iugurtha, Iugurtha was beheaded in prison, and Bochus saved,
because the graundfather of Bochus had made learned Orations before the Senate,
and profited the commonwealth with his wise sentences) but if your welfare
impair, then your good kinsfolks renounce your kindred, least you diminish the
copy of their countenance. As Antiochus being banished his country,
comforted only with his poor daughter, fellow in his banishment, and after
having his houses broken down with an earthquake, and his daughter slain all
in one day, and hearing that an old friend of his was come to the town, to whom
he would ease himself in bewailing his estate, came to him, welcomd him, and
began his plaining moan. The other knowing him very well, but seeing he could
reap no great reputation by his acquaintance, asked him what he was,
Antiochus told, and wondered he had forgotten, thinking it had been long of
the macerating of his body, which was misshapen with sorrow: truly quoth the
other, I know one Antiochus well, a Noble man in Rome, and my especial friend,
but as for this fellow, I perceive he is a counterfeit, and therewith
commanded him to be voided his lodging. This is like Gentlemen in our days,
who will he cozins to all of any port or great report in the whole shire,
though their gransires dog scarce leapt over their grandames hatch, but if a
poor man be in the second degree, he is not in the catalogue of their
genealogy. Tlepoleme Alcides son, slew his fathers friend Lycimnion, to whom
also he was allied in love, for which fact he fled his country.

Friendship which has been, or should be the Sun of all the world, which
should give life and light to all good minds, is now endarkened, or quite
extinguished.

Aeneas loosing his friend Deicoon, quickly forgot him, and got a new one. Q.
Curtius writes, that Alexander at his return from Babylon, renewing his
accustomed feasting, and invited by Thessalus his physician to a banquet, was
poisoned by him: but ye truth is, that there was treason wrought against him,
ye chief of which conspiracy, was his friend of friends, Antipater. It is hard
to find Senecaes Arthesius, who having two friends, whereof the one was poor,
and yet for shamefastnes dissembling his need, priuelie put a bag of money
under his pillow, that his friend might rather find that he wanted, with joy
of good hap, then through his good nature being ashamd to ask, should want
that he needed through his default.

I Need not admonish you to give no credence to Loves wil^s, whom neither law
with fear, nor wisdom with discretion, ca^ restrain.

Agorastocles in Plautus not knowing how to compass his lover, had counsel
to eircuument Lycus that kept her in this manner: to send Colibiscus his
bai^iffe to him, who feigning himself to be in love with her, should give
Lycus a great sum of money to be shut up with her in a chamber, to have his
pleasure: therewhile Agorastocles should enquire at Lycus his house for his
servant, and thus he assured himself, that Lycus knowing no servant he had
but his parasite, whom he supposed he meant, would deny him: then the impastor
Agorastocles ^a^^^king hie house, and finding his servant and his money in
Lycus the house, should have him and his whole house confiscate to him, and by
this means should be Lord of his love.

How loose Love is, and how briefly expired, the sequel shall give
specialtie. When Iris the common messinger and purseua^t of Jove came to Helen,
in her loved sister in laws fair Laodices shape, and brought her word how it
was pacted that Menelaus and Paris should s^int ye strife, and were buskling to
battle, Hele^s mind was sped of her first spouse, and country town, and
alienated quite from her lover Paris: and when Venus had in a trice with a
slenting slight shifted out trick Paris in a mislie air from the camp, and
brought Helen to comfort him at his chamber, she bedlemlike began to rave. O
would to God my husbands hap had been to dau^t thy vaunting hoighes, thou
wontest to vaunt he durst not meet thee in the face, but thou would eat
him at a gobbe, which now have showed a pair of leaden heels: assay his force
no more if thou be wise, least yt it do thee ill apay.

What fumish brawles, and pettish tetishnes were betwixt Areitho king of Arna,
and his pretty bulchion and loving fuds, ye fair glassy eyed Philomedusa, for
whose love not long since, but even ye day before the bridal, he would needs
dye the loving death, and bequeath his body to the ^irie feast.

As far in love as Achilles was with Brisis, he could be content in her absence
to make sport betwixt a pair of sheets with his sweet heart Diomed, the kings
daughter of Lesbos, cleped Phorbe, and this knew the comicall beldam Misis in
Terence his Andria, that lusty crustie loytring love, as extinct vapoures
soon remoues, who gossoping with Lesbia her friend, flattered her in this
sort: It is even (Lesbia) as thou say, few true to women shall thou find.
It is a Proverb in England that the men of Tiuidal horderers on ye english
middle marches, have likers, lemmo^s, and lyerbies.

THe Gods themselves will now and than dispense with deceit, and retaile a
fallacy to such as they can and list delude.

Jupiter meaning to give Agamemnon an overthrow, sent the God of dreams to
him in his sleep in old sage Nestors shape, (whose persuasions he had ever in
price) and subornd him to give ye Trojans battle, under protection of Jupiter
his warrante: the prince obeyed ye vision, but had effect quite contrary, for
he and his garrison were both foiled.

The green eyed Goddess with her cokesing words, set Pindarus a gog to
infringe the compacte ystricke between his confederates and the Pelasgians.
Apollo to front Aeneas from death, ridded him out of the battle, and to abuse
the army, erected his portrayture in his wonted station among his soldiers as
though it had been he, about which the knights of Graece and Trojans both, yt
one sort spending their trauails to defend it, the other bending their mights
to defeat a senceles thing of life) made many a widdowe and fatherless
child: wrangling cracking Mars did swear full stoutly in aid of grekish
crews, but the self same day he periuredly deard them.

Neither are the heavenly bodies lind with such minds, as we imagine, nor their
strength so rath as surpasses all puissance of million legies, and not to be
thirlde with any thwart. For admit ye all the Gods and Goddesses descending
from the earth, could not hail Jupiter from heaven with a golden chain
fastened to him (which he insulted of himself) yet Ephala and Otus the sons of
great Oloeus, bound God Mars and imprisoned him ye space of 13. months, where
he had consumed with setters and stinking, had not Euribaea their mother in law
besought Mercurius in his behalf, who stole him a way closely in a braide.

Juno her breast with triple headed shearing shaft was hurt by Hercules. Pluto
sable God of vaste infernal Tartarum, did give the hand of self same man, a
badge of his blood in Pilie soil among the murdered carcasses, of which
wound he was healed by Paean phis^ion of the Gods.

POrtending Sothsaings of gospelling Augures, to whom ye Ethnickes were
tributorie in devotion, were superstitious collusions to supplant the
credulous, and more authorized by custom, then allowed for commodity.

When ye Graecians were in suspense, whether to march on to give onset of
battle, at that same stoure, drad flakes of lightning fire were darted down
from heaven, which falling at their right hands, were, said Nestor (and true
he said) accounted certain diuinations of prosperous luck: yet for all that,
their luck was at that time, to loose both man, moyle, and machins belonging
to war. These presagings be as true, as yt, which ye schoolmaisters of Padua
taught, that in the instant wherein you shall see a Cuckoo, not having seen
any that year before, you shall find an hair under your right foot, if you
stand still, and remove not when you see her: if this hair be black, you
shall have evil luck that year,, if white, good luck, if gray, indifferent
luck.

Unreasonable therefore are their assertions, which say, to hear an Heron cry
when thou go on Imbacie, is a sign of speeding (and yet this they ground on
a place in the tenth of Homers Iliades:) and theirs which say, to see a black
Swine before the Sun rise, is a sign of evil luck that present day (and this
has his original from a proverb of Empodocles.

Then recke not of the Augures, ne yet of ghostly prophesiers. Eunomus
prophecy was esteemed a touchstone of truth, yet could he not discuss to fly
his fate, and escape the fiste of fierce Achilles. Euridomant a prophet not
profane, but judged a right interpreter by divine infusion, could not cast his
sonns unlucky fate, Polidus and Abantes his, whom Diomede cut in pieces.

Indeed it was Calchas his cunning, or rather good chance, not so much by
sanctimonie of prophecy, but as I think, in flattery of the potentates, to
hit the truth in unfolding a secret hid: when the Graekes were in Aulis a
town of Beocie, sacrificing to Jupiter under a green beach tree, growing upon
a lively spring, an hideous Dragon crepte from the Aultars foot, with painted
scales like the scarlet grape (which creeping up to the lopiest and tallest
part of the Tree, where he found 8. young peping sparrows in the leaves, which
he rauend up, devouring the dam also, while she lamented her yongones) by and
by was enrolde in another shape, and of a monstrous Serpent became a stony
rock: the Graekes amassed at this, Calchas calculated, that as the Dragon had
devoured these 9. little ones, so in 9. years space should they be tired with
the proud perucke and pranke Trojans, and as the dragon was insensuated, so in
the tenth year, Saturnus heir would give them for their hire, the Trojan
squadrons, and their batled City should go to sack and pillage, and as he
said, his saying theed.

AStronomy is a perfect skill. But as Aristotle says in the 6. of his
Ethickes, that Prudens who is the absolutest politic man, though he be
genarally perfect, may stumble in particularities: so the best Astronomers that
ever I red, tell most palpable gross absurdities.

Ptolomeus says, that he that is borne, when the Lord of the 7. House is in
the third or ninth degree, and any evil planet behold him, will sodeinlie
die, by falling from some building, or else will die of something, that shall
fall upon him.

THe sugrest tranquillity is soonest transitory: who seeks to mount above the
moving skies, their ruin grows, where most they reach to rise: who whilom sat
in chair of high renown, adowne are headlong hurld to bottomless misery.

Sardanapalus king of Assiria, one of the richest Monarchies in the world, amid
his pompous elevated royalties, was miserably slain by one Arbactus:
conuenable to this, Cyrus king of Persia, which as well by means of other
victories that he had obtained, as also, that he had subdued Craesus king of
Lydia, was waxed of all others most rich and renowned, when he had reigned full
thirty years, was himself discomfited, and beheaded by Tamyris Queen of
Scythia.

After Brutus and Cassius the manquailers of Caesar, were, as Plutarth gives
specialtie: exiled by Octauian and M. Antonius, they for restoration conspired
against their iniurours, and pitched a field Of the faction of these seminaries
was M. Varro, a Gentleman of great honor and fame, who with the other
coniurates and adherentes, was likewise discomfited: he then seeing ye danger
which he was opposed too, incontinently disrobed himself of his own apparel,
taking another habit, who by this transmutation being reputed for a common
soldiour, was among other captives soulde for a vile price to one Barbulas a
Romayne Knight. A good while after this, one knowing him discovered him to
Barbulas, which without semblaunce to Varro that he knew any thing,
privately procured pardon of Octauian, who franckly enfraunchised him again,
and from that time forth held him in the number of his friends. After this,
Anthony and Octauian falling out to be capital enemies, Barbulas leaned to
Anthony, and with him was ouerrun in the field by Octauian, at which event,
dreading the furious wrath of Octauian, he used for his own safety, the
above remembered policy of Varro, that is to wit, he did on him the coat of a
poor soldiour: Thus M. Varro, for that he had not long seen him, as also for
that he had changed his apparel, among other captives unknown bought him
for a bo^d man, but after short time remembering him again, practiced with
Octauian, that he wan him pardon of his prince, and eftsoons possessed him of
his wonted liberty. So that Barbulas which first was good Lord and master,
was now at the check of his vnderlout vassaille: and Varro, which before was a
bounden villain, had now prerogative of his awful master: which two contrary
casual chances, gives us a certain certificate how frail and fragile the
loftiest fortune is, and how shortly her smiling lasts and endures, in so
much that as Philemon, beholding an ass eat figs from of a table, brake into
such an extreme laughter, yt he dyed with laughing: or as Philistione a poet
comicall, and Denis the tyrant of Siracusa, with surpassing joy made sudden
change of life: so we, not only when we seem most firmly seated in
immutable prosperity, are sodeinlye beraught of our unstable happiness, but
often with the surplusage of abundant pleasure, as we swine afloat in midst
of bliss, do sink and drench in depth of bale.

The Thebanes banqueting with merry, glee all careless, full of chat, had
dole of death dealt among them.

In Baldach a city in Armenia the less, which lies toward the South, there
is a prince resident called Calipho, which is among the Moors chief
governor, the king of the Tartars called Alan, hearing that the Calipho which
then reigned, was meruellous rich, invaded the city, and took it by force,
being in it one hundred thousand horsemen, besides infinite number of footmen,
and there he found a great Tower full of gold, silver and precious stones: Alan
appauled at this sight, said unto Calipho. I much marvel of thy avarice, that
did not give part of thy great treasure, to maintain valiant men against
me, knowing I was thy mortal enemy: and perceiving he could make no
answer, well quoth he, because thou love treasure so well, I will thou
shalt have thy fill of it, and caused him to be sparred fast in the same
tower, where he lived four days, and died miserably for hunger. Prester John
warring a long time with Bur, without any advantage of him, sent him 7.
young Gentlemen to his Court, showing, as though they departed from Prester
John in great displeasure, and offered themselves to serve the King Bur, who
retained them as squires and pages in his Court: after 2 years, having groate
confidence in them, the King ridinge abroad for his pleasure, took the 7.
Gentlemen with him, who being the distance of a mile from his Castle, and
perceiving they had opportunity to execute their purpose, carried him to
Piester John, who made him Shepherd two years, and afterward sent him to his
Castle with horses and men as a Shepherd.

Niobe, whose father was Tantalus, her mother a sister of the Pleiades, and she
the Lady of Phrigia land and Cadmus palla^e, bewtifull in her body, and
endewed with seven sons, and seven daughters (in so much, that she repined
Latona should have the honor of the Altar, and not she) was barrenned of her
twice seven issue, and she with sorrow changed into a s^one, where upon
the Mountain top in Phrigia Land she weepeth still in stone, and from ye
stone the drearie tears do drop.

Might I therefore be so bold as give them counsel, who are heaued up to the
type of dignity, I would wish them often to parlie and practice that, which
Cecilius Balbus in his Philosophers toys, writes of Agathocles king of
Sicilia, whose descent being base, would say of himself, Though I now be a
King, a potter was my sire,
Then call to mind thy base estate, before thou gotst thy hire.


REpose not too much affiaunce in monumentes of histories, neither settle your
confidence in a bare hearsay, unless your reading be experimented with
express proofs, and your rumor be raised from a sure ground.

Auerrois a man in most men's judgments, whose advise is worthy to be
advanced, and whose testimony is to be taken, reports, thot he beheld a
poor unfortunate patient being beheaded, walk hither and thither in the sight
of all the people: which can not be, because by heading, the veins are
sundered (the only organes and sole instruments of motion in all living
creatures.) It is also written of Dyonisius Areopagita, that his head being
stricken of, he went from the place of execution one full league or more. Noe,
it is not with them, as with the adders tail, which being stricke of will
skippe up and down, like the adder it self: and far are they unlike
Philomelaes tongue, which being cut of, wrigled up and down a long season,
as though it had lived.

In the time of Romulus, were seen in the fyrmament, as is written, two Suns
at midnoone, which cannot be (their original being natural, as it is reputed
to be.) For as the crown or garland, which is seen under the Sun, and is
called of the Meteorologians, Halon, is a waterish round cloud, upon which the
Sun beams beating, represente the same show, so a smooth and equal waterish
cloud placed by the side of the Sun, receives his beams like a glass, and
expresses the Idol of it in the same fashion: This cloud must not be too
near, for then the Sun disperseth it: nor too far of, for the the beams
will be too feeble to be reflected: but in a competent distaunce: neither can
yt be at noone day, for the heat will soon dissolve it.

A little before Galba, Otho, and Vitellius striued for the Romayne Empire,
there appeared three Suns, which were after thought to be supernatural
prognostications of consequent contentions, but such diuiners were scarce good
natural Philosophers.

For those miraculous wonders, which are in scripture to glorify Gods name,
and found in old Annaleis for the confirmation of the efficacy of Christ's
passion, I must and will dispense with my incredulous nature to believe them.

For the first, all meruailes that God wrought by the hands of Moses and other
his servants, of going through the Sea dry, of striking water out of a
Rock, of Manna sent every morning, of the Suns course stayed in the
Firmament, and many such like.

For the second, that for example, which Eutropius writes, that at the instant
of the birth of Christ, brake up a spring of oil in an Inn of Rome, which
ran by the space of one whole day. Cammester says in his history, that at the
dedicating of a temple to ye Goddess Peace, Apollo gave oracle, yt til a
virgin bare a son it should stand, which they thought would never be, and
therefore yt for ever it should persist. This Temple says he, at the delivery
of the Virgin of our redeemer, fell and severed each piece from other. Eusebius
writes to Theodorus, that a Ship sailing by ye Isle Paraxis, a great dreadful
voice admonished a pilot of ye ship, that passing by the gulf Laguna, he
should give notice that the great God Pan, (who I think, was some captain
master devil) was dead. He fearing to do this legacion or Imbacie, was forced
nevertheless by the staying of the ship (which at his arrival there arested,
and would no further) to mount up into the pomp or hinder part of the ship,
where he did his embassadge as loud as he could: which words uttered, such
dolerous lamentatious, and terrible cries were heard, as if Lapithauri and
Centauri should howl together with a diverse deformed bellowing, as huge a
noise, as when high Jove did let his thunder fly upon the adder-footed rowte,
that battered heaven with hundred hands apeece, the dint whereof the aierie
toys of high Olympus brake. Then to conclude, in generaall this I say. The aged
man is like the barren soil, a woman is a reed that wagges with every wind,
no trust is to be found in tender years, the surety of all ages is unfound.
Most women be evils (I might have said Devils) yet devils without horns, for
these they use to place in secret corners. And most men, but here will I stay
with an abrupt Aposiopasis.

Here I confess I have writ somewhat plainly, thinking, that as when curious
Euperices and his fellow, could not agree about the curing of Crysippus king
of Trinacria, it was enacted that they should not mingle or compound medicines,
but minister the^ simply as they were: so it is most requisite for redressing
these impieties, to correct the worlds Elenchs plainly and sincerely, and not
with doubling to bolster his errors. But as Pisistrato the tyrant used to say,
that Damonidas the philosopher could persuade him any thing, and therefore
would never permit him to speak in his hearing: Or as Isocrates writes to
Phillip, yt if he were in presence to pronounce his own oration, he would not
doubt of his wished success: so (courteous Gentlemen, who shall deigne to read
this treatise) if I were in place with you, to make manifestatio^ of all the
disloyal trecheries which I my self have sustained (and you may conceive by
implication) you would neither judge me a miscreant for my mistrust, nor exhort
me to a reprisell of my former credulity. But as the Graeks contending after
ye death of Ipocras and Chrysippus the philosopher, to whether of their
doctrines they should rather cleave as most canonical, at length were so
indifferent that they allowed neither of both: so you shall do best, if you
neither believe too confidently, nor faithlesly distrust, but be equal
intercoursers betwixt both extremities.

AFter the compiling of this patchie pamphlet, which in my conceit was not all
the finest web, because liberty of affection, not learning and judgment, laid
the loome, he tossinge up and down his incurable misfortunes, recalde to
memory his antifrend Cornelius, the original of them all, to whom he sent
this consequent challenge, experimenting if as yet he had got a courage.

Philotimus to the wretched dastard Cornelius.
IF I knew thy worthy style, and the dignity of thy house, I would not steal
thy title, nor conceal thy honor. But thy public deserts conjoined with
the intricate doggednes of nature, would appose the skilfullest Oedipus in
their description, and do parlie me a pardon if I be grauelled.

Thou will vaunt perhaps that thou art a gentleman by blood, and I will grant
(the worse my hap) that thou art a bloody Gentleman. And yet (varlet) if thou
were but a man, thou would never have endured so many manacinges, but once
ere now in so eger challenges, have taken courage to enter the combat. A man
said I? Yes sure, you are as honest a man, as any is in the cards if the
Kings were out: and as manly a champion, as the Comicall Thraso, who rather
thought to fear men with looking big, then meddle with the^ for fear of hurt.

Though thou have injured me, and perjured my mistrisse, yet yt thou may
know, that a worm frod on will turn rgaine, and that the chips of the tree,
which thou would hew down, may fly in thy face, I dare thee to the field,
and send thee my gauntlet for a gage of revenge: appointe the place, and bring
none but thy self, and as I am a Gentleman I promise to meet thee, my self
single alone, though I grant me as simple as any one: then shall you try,
though Aurelia have my heart in pawn which I cannot redeem, that
notwithstanding I have a gall which shall gaul the to the reins, and a body
to bear thy blows, and dear thy bones. Farewell patch and coward, and as
spedely as thou dare, prove thy self a Captain.

Philotimus thy foe, and thine accursed.


THese letters in Cornelius his absence, were delivered to one of his
servants, and by this means came to Auraliaes reading, who, had not
Cornelius vnbewares showen himself, would to stay this suite, have returned a
writ of non est inuentus, but since it happened otherwise, that all minght
cotton well, this was her rescript in Cornelius his behalf. For as I erst
said, he was a gentleman that had great store of small knowledge, one belike
conceived in ignorance, and begotten when his father was a little scared.

Cornelius to the glorious soldiour Philotimus.
O Preclare stratagems, and your erected Trophies, you flourish fair, but fight
at leisure: you make as courageous a challeng, as if you were Laelius Atticus
Dentatus, who was an hundredth and twenty times in battle, unto which he
went always with such courage of mind, and force of body, that he seemed
to presume of victory: but though thou art the prowdest that ever I heard
brag, yet art thou not the most prouedst that ever I dealt with, though thou art
one of those groyling gruntes (which Perseus speaks of in his fifth Satyr)
whose choleric garboyle pipkius full of purging drugges, can neither quench
nor quell, and one of that ruffianlike number, that will dragge the Devil out
of Hell for thirtene pence, yet your puffing and snuffing will be well enough
snaffled, your choler will be coold and your ire relent.

No feet shall be my fence, nor fear your kilkow chat: though Buten were
brag, yet Dares was his match: though Dares were frolic, yet he found his
fellows: though Teucer with his bow made havoc in hacking the Trojan knights
and coronels, yet Hector at length with his heaued codgill, paid him home with
heave and how: the yonker Antilocuus butchered Ecepholus, but for his hire
Agenor sent a Iauelin to his bared breast, and laid him loubring on ye
ground: Dyodorus his soul was sent to Plutoes Court by Pirus, Thracian
prince, he struck his leg with mighty pibble, and burst each vein and
cord. But what ensued? Thoas could not blin, but slitt Lord Pirus paunch with
teeninge sword, and many a soldier more in his reueng were dold with deadly
swoune, the purple gore whereof did flow as hilly springs are wont to spread.

As Stenhelus, friend to Diomede, was never from his back to aid him at need:
so if thou by trains or tretcherie (for I defye thy manhood) shalt do me a
mischief, I have both friends and servants too, the worst whereof will crack
your crown.

Think thou, I will so far debase my self, as to debate a quarrel with thee
by dinte of sword? In faith Sir no. The greyhound sent to Great Alexander by
the king of Albania, having an Heart, a Bore, and a Bear, brought to him, lay
still wagging his tail, not vouchsafing to look on the^, but having a Lion
brought leapt at him and strangled him, and seeing a great Eliphant, seemed to
rejoice at the bigness of him, and after two or three questings rowsing
himself, slew him.

But thy father thou will say, was a ndble man, and therefore thou my equal:
admit thy fathers honor, and aduerte what I will say, if an Ass were borne of
Pegasus, being an Ass what cause has he to compare with any horse? If you
love discord so well, beware you accord not to wear an hempton cord. For after
a collar comes an haulter.

The winsing kickenest Jade that will not abide to be shod, if you put but a
lile round f^intstone in one of his ears, and then hold ye ear hard, is very
quiet, and if you put in either ear one, you shall have him as mild as a
sheep: so if a man should but whisper in your ear, and tell you he would
meet you in such a place, you would singe, I fear me, a palinodie.

Let Pluto send thee peace of mind, and stay thy moodie manacings, and as in
years and wealth thou wantst of me, so yield thy nauale forces. Against him its
best to fight and animate thy mind whose pith thou can withstand and mate his
might: remember well my words, and tender their performaunce.

Ulysses wished to have forehayde the Lycians Prince Sarpadon, but Gods they
would reserve that fact for some more worthy champion:

You strain no courtesy to call me knave, as one that has a deep insight in
a knave, and think no scorn to call me patch: I give you to understand, that
your patches clothes be worn, and the rags stick in your teeth. Desist
Philotimus, for it will not be thy luck to resist Cornelius. When thou art
wiser in thy challenges, and fitter to fight, I will be readier to answer
the^, and not disdain the victory.

Thine as thou his Cornelius. Hazard dwelles next hardines.


It grieved Philotimus to the heart, that he could not hurt Cornelius by
neither wile nor weapon, and this he though not the least addition to the
heap of his miseries. But he quoth Philotimus, that will needs be a sheep,
cannot greatly grudge to be bitten with a Fox, and since the coward dare not
manfullye meet me in the field, let him think it no villainy though I catch
him by a train. But as the water flows, when it is at lowest eb: so one
Archaretos who had often sought for Philotimus his friendship in his
prosperity, and still was set at naught of the coy Philotimus, til time of
adversity, was now a sovereign solace to all his steering sorrows.

This Gentleman Archaretos keeping in the country, heard of Philotimus his
hard haps, and how his creditors on the one side meant perpetually to imprison
him, and his enemies on the other to dispatch him out of the way, and now this
he thouhht a very convenient time to show his loyalty to Philotimus, of
whose recovery he had good hope. Wherefore finding a means at his own
charges for Philotimus to avoid these dangers, he writ to him this epistle.

Archaretos to Senior Philotimus.
THe recordation of that amity wherewith I once affected thee, when thou not
regardedst it, and the consideration of true loves property, which is not to
relente for any unkindness, moves me in thy misery to animate thee with
comfort, and to proffer thee such aid as my ability can afford. The vine
which we see grows about the elm, will not wither and leave to grow when the
elm does die, but after the elmes decaying keeps his growth as before, and
with clasping about it embraces his elm. Certes, the fine gold conserueth
his quallities in the quick furnace.

It may be, that as the Rauen-leaueth her yongeones for three days, till she
see whether their color be congruent to hers: so thou laidest aloof for time
of trial, which might be a touchstone to try my promis: howsoever the
matter was, whether disdain took delight in dalliaunce, or procrastination
was for some purpose, let that slip: in thore days I wearied thee with my
wel-willing, and yet I am not tired to procure thy welfare.

I profess to thy person (Pilotimus) that as I have ever been enemy to none,
so have I only friend to one. When I hard of thy ruinous destinies, I could not
hold my hands from shaking, nor my lunges from sighing, nor mine eyes from
weping, so that I thought, as it is the greatest nigardize of all nigardizes,
when a man may do much and will do little, so it is the greatest infortune of
all infortunes, when a man may do little and would do much.

It is indeed impossible to make sinews of blood, of veins to make bones, of
a craggie rock a plain way, and to give absolute counsel in a case without
c^ntrolment: so that I cannot tell whether is more dolorous to me, thy unhappy
heaviness almost past propitiation, or my debilitie and impotencie, insufficient
to help thee.

Augustus dreamed on a night how he was warned by Phillip his physician, that
he should be killed in his pauilion, whereupon he avoided, and in very deed
the next day after, the soldiers of Brutus entering his tents, stabbed many
holes with their puncheons in his couch, thinking he had been there. Before the
battle against the Phillippians, a certain soldiour thought in his sleep
that Julius Caesar bad him tell Octauius, he should fight the luckier, if he
carried something about him that Julius had worn whiles he was head
governor: Octauius understanding this, got the ringe wherewith he used to
seal letters, and the same he wore, and overcame his enemies. These
presidentes endoctrine great matters. He that has been brought up with flesh,
cannot feed on bones: and you whose burgeyning and springing time has been
nousled among the wisest in Italy, will think your gold soon changed to
copper, to be shored and propped up with my Paradoxes.

Notwithstanding here thou see the two regal Emperoures directed by their
servants, and so dyrected, as they had cause to thank the Gods for their
merciful provision, and to reward their servants for their luckie
persuasions. So it is Philotimus, if thou will take a little pains at my
entreatye and for thine own ease, I would have the come the tenth of this
month to ye house of one Carnus, in a little village named Merio, distant from
the City about three leagues, there I will meet thee, and tell thee such
traynes, as by inquisition I have found to be laid for thee. We will confer
by what means thou may shun them, which is only by flying. How and when
that flight must be, I will then show thee, and furnishe thee with such money
as may relieve thy necessity. Return me an answer by this bearer what then
wilt do. In the mean time the Gods of their goodness give thee that thou
desire, and then have I all, I do desire.

Thine to use Archaretos.


Philotimus without pawsing resent him these words.

Philotimus to his most faithful friend Archaretos.
Never was travail more toothsome to thee, nor legacye sent with quicker
embassage, then the delivery of thy letters was consolation to me, which shall
be accomplished with acceleration.

If thou had seen which thou saw not, what sorrows I susteynd, then
should thou know, which thou know not what solaces surprise me.

My spirit was betwixt the Anuile and hammer, ready to be thrown into
Vulcans flames, and thou haste swaged my rages greater then the furies of
Bacchus his priests, and conducted my esperaunce to a restfull paradise.

Because I cannot thank thee as I think of thee, take (Archaretos) the whole
Alphabet of letters, and coin such thanks by their coagmentation, as
possibly thou can by the aid tf thy eloquence. Then think, that feign I
would utter those in words, and do mutter much greater in secret thought.

Doubt not (Archareios) but I will come to the place of appointment, and if my
lasie legs were ioyntles, and for that should deny to make this voyage, my
heart would repute and hold them for rebels, and substitute my hands to be
their vicegerentes.

In the mean season, I meet thee in mind, and greet thy person with ten
thousand salutations, which, because they are neither so many as thou
deseruest, nor so hartelye written as I do wish them, I leave space of paper
for the to write more, making a sign where I cannot singe. Farewell
Archaretos, whose bounty has quayled my disquiet. And as Possidonius says
of the rainbow, yt it is a glass wherein we may see the Sun, because in it are
his beams refracted, by whose reflection more manifest apparition of his
clearenes is seen, so those that have not erst known thee shall view thy
magnificence in me, and with ferlye himnes herrie thy praises.

Thy poor friend to command to his small power, Philotimus.


NOw Sir, Philotimus began to recounte Archaretos his letters, and to take
account of his passed life, how he should deserve his passing goodwill: he
pondered what might be the cause of the renewing of his goodwill, which he
thought had been obsolete and abolished. I muse quoth he, yet know not, I
meuaile, yet cannot conjecture. He was guilty of former contempt, in regard of
Archaretos, and fearful of present guile, to reward his proud haughtines: he
did well know his meeknes in manners, yet did he fear least he mocked his
miseries: he had proved his true meaning in the effect of his dealinges, yet
doubted least his persuasions were a mean to deceive him: he had experienced
his friendly affection, yet did expect that Archaretos being expert of his
poverty, would be procurde by this allurement to repay his peuishnes. Alas
(quoth he thus quoting these surmises) will he work me this despite, because
I have despised him? he may if he will, but the valor of his might is nothing
the more. The streyuing of a gnat is not swallowing of a cammell: an action got
in law by a false inquest, is no lawful commense, nor lawdable co^queste: it
is easy to strive with him that sitts in the stocks, and small manhood of a
captain to break ye skull of a dead man: laugh not when other men's houses be
on fire, least the glaring sparkles light on thine own. Pentheus for mocking
an old blind father had a cold prophecy verisied on him. I have heard, yt
the hardest block comes soonest to a knock, and that the common est flowter
as one without escapes, may be a cunning skogin in his own conceit, but shall
become a common scoff to all that know him. But I am impudente in coniecting
the worste, because I now am impotent, and have rejected his goodwil. Though
honor harborow yt us but for on hour, yet homely honesty will keep her old
nest: though my currishnes be a sign that he should delude me, yet his
constancy has sined to perpetuity of love. How can it be that he should
seek means to bereave me of breath, who studieth to strengthen and lengthen
my life? No no, his friendship is firm, and my affection shall be reciprocal: he
endeuoreth to broch a better tap to make my bear go better down, and therefore
I will not say nay, but assay to drink, apply me to his pleasure, and be
correspondent to his counsel. Upon this confidence of Archaretos his
fidelity, Philotimus at the day prefixed repaired to ye foresaid place: what
their meeting communication and the drift of their conference was, I will give
you notice by their mutual colloquy, which I have enterlaced dialogue wise.

Archaretos. Philotimus. PHilotimus, my wished friend, and welcomde guest, how
do thou?


Phil. O Archaretos my savior, ill to all, and worst to my self: all the
world is otemeale, and my poke left at home. I will not say to to thee as the
old beggar man said to his dame, God send you your health as long as I
live, but I wish that his life may be set on a long last, whose love has eased
