the wrenching of my heart, and that his prosperity may never quail, whose
comfort, as a cullisse, preserves my weal.

Dear Archaretos, forget and forgive my passed ingratitude: for then for want
of years and good discretion I could not discern my friends (as whelps
cannot see till they be nine days old) and now for shame I could eat mine
own ears (as Ajax after the slaughter perpetrated in his madness) at the
recovery of his wits killed himself.


Philotimus, quoth Archaretos, ingrate men men indeed deserve hate, yet would I
be loath to minister you cumber for a medicine of comfort, or follow your
hresident of former malice, since I mean to give you precepts of new
goodwill. Thy hot choler I perceive is turned to cold coals, and the remnaunt
of the ashes utterly dispersed: though therefore I cannot appease thy pains
with pleasure, yet will I please thee if I can with my present parlie, and
though I cannot effectually furnishe thy wantes, yet will I most faithfully
further thy wish.

Alas (good Philotimus) my fear did ever divine this hap, though my well
wishing impugned my presagings. But a man shall hardly be deceived, where his
heart does fully advertise him. For neither did Silla err in that which he
prophesied of Julius Caesar, neither Ptololomeus fail, in that he foredeemed
of Alcibiades: because the one deprived Rome of her liberty, and the other
depraved the glory of Graece.

The Romans puissance could not be infringed or brought to expuguation,
either by the Carthagenians or the Frenchmen, the Huns or the Epirotes, the
Sabines, the Samnites, or the Etrurians, but by reckles pride, and licentious
living, it was remunerated with utter ruin. I speak it to this purpose
(Philotimus) because if thou had been as wary in thy life, as thou art
wise in thy learning, or as frugall in expenses, as thou waste frolic in thy
sportes, thy state had been firm, thou had needed no friends, and all then
too Well, had not now turned to Woe.

But no man is borne without a blemishe, and the Gods themselves had their
imbecilities. Saturn was peuishe, Jupiter adulterous, Apollo wanton, and Titan
envious: Alexander was furious, Archidamus ambitious, Demetrius vicious,
Hannibal perjured, Traian a wine bibber, and Homer a vain talker. Then (my
good Philotimus) since as I hope thou art penitent for preter ouersights^, be
not dismayed in thy self, nor despair in thy fortune.

But first of all I straightly inhibit you for your better thriuing, never
once to enterteine one thought of Aurelia. Mamea was proud, Medaea cruel,
Martia contumacious, Poplia unchaste, Myrrha malicious, Domitia rash: Assiria
complained of her scandale Semyramis, Armenia of Pincia, Graece of Helen,
Germany of Vxodonea, Rome of Agrippina, Spain of Hecuba: but all these
jointly with every their several faults, may not compare with thy compeere
Aurelia, of whose wickedness thy testimony is a perfect proof.

Muse not Philotimus, that I dehorte thee from love, and persuade thee to
liberty: for I have red more in Hossienus that instructs to give counsel,
then in Ovid that learneth to be enamoured: and more shall thou profit in
following a Stoycke, then in practicing the toies of coy Dorcatius.

Philotimus answered. My dearest Arcaaretos as I had rather pledge thee in
water then any other in wine, so one syllable of thine shall more persuade
me, then the sage sentences of any other. The words of the Philosopher
Theomastes, did more prevail with some of the Graeciaus, then the huge armies
of king Phillip, and more effectual (good Archaretos) are thy gentle
exhortations, then the threatening torments of any calamities.

But alas this haste thou heard, and I have tried it, that the cruel beast
Love, does suffer herself to be taken with a thread, but will not be thrust away
with a percing lance. In this case I remit you to Hermogenes, to Tesiphontes,
and to Plutarch, who have spent much time in writing of Loves remedies, and yet
at length have been urged to say and confess, that it is a more incurable
disease in the mind, then the gout or plague is in the body. Let Ovid say
what him pleases, Nigidius what he dreameth, Samocratius what he thinks, but
in fine, they shall find it harder to get a salve for this sore, then for the
Alcumistes by their art and creticke days, to find the Philosophers stone and
the Quintiscence of nature. Nay (Archaretos) these three usurping physicians,
died persecuted and banished from their native country, not for the offenses
they committed in Rome, but for the loves they attempted in Capua.

Oh how many times did Hercules desire to be delivered from his love
Mithridata, Menelaus from Dorrha, Pirrhus from Helena, Alcibiades from Sabina,
and M. Antonius from Cleopatra, whom they would never not only forsake, but
with them or for them yielded to death? Good counsel and affection agree like
iron and clay, which by no means can be brought to stick together,

Though I were as light of foot as an Hind, as swift as Polite, Priams son,
or as speedy as the coursers of Eumelus, yet could I never leap over such a
block as is Love, or ouerrun such a mate as is Affection, or keep pace with
that precise iniunction, ye prohibites all fancies.

But yet me thinks that a denial to any of thy requests must needs be a
prescription to my honesty. And therefore since thou art no otherwise to me
then Solon was to the Athenians, Lycurgus to the Lacedaemonians, Asclepius to
the Rhodians, Numa Po^pilius to the Romans, and Phoroneus to the Aegiptians,
impera and impetra apud me, demand and command, and I will obey.

Archaretos made replication in this manner. Indeed (Philotimus) Lactantius
Firmianus says, that the common wealth of the Sicyonians endured longer then
either that of the Graekes or of ye Aegiptians, or the Lacedaemonians, or the
Romans, because in seven hundredth and forty years, they never made new
laws, nor brake their old ones, so that constancy in good matters is ever
commendable.

But he that continues his suite to her, that flies faster from him then the
hare from the horn, is much like one of the Mirundins (who at Peleus his
prayers were turned again to men) which was so enamoured of the Suns brightness,
that incessantlye he perseuerd in travail and labor, meaning to meet it at
the end of the world, there to embrace it and enjoy his pleasure.

Thou art now to leave the confines of thy country, and with a speedy
perigrination, and diligent inuestigation, to seek a mantion in some other
soil, and therefore thy heart must be on thy halfpenye, and thy mind
deuorced from the contemplation of Aurelia. And yet thou must be of a bon
couragio: for no place is a banishment to virtue says A Milo: I have no place
of abode, said Socrates, but am a citezin of the world: whithersoeuer thy
pilgrimage shall conducte thee, we are all in the compass of one heaven,
protected with the providence of one God, and once shall be united with one
consolidation.

So near neighbors are we all, that even our Antipodes whose dwelling is
opposite to us, are daily traded too for erchau^g of traffic. Alezander wept,
that there were no more earthes for him to conquer but this little one: Alas
says Scipio Affricanus, how base a thing is populer glory, which is
comprised in ye earthes limitation.

I cannot tell (Philotimus) what place is best for thee to soiourne in, because
thou art furnished for any kind of service, neither can I allot thee any
residence, because like a snayle I have ever kept my home: but search and find,
and if thou will vouchsafe to let me know of thy staying, I will not be
slack to aid thee with necessaries. Let no imaginary conceit of thy great
pains appaule or dismaye thee, for when thou art once inured with them, thou
shall find small difference betwixt thy good labors, and the daly sports of
the greatest potentates. Arsaciades his pastime king of the Bactrians, was to
knit nets: Artaxarxes his, to spin: Arthabanus his king of the Huns, to arm
for rattes: and Viantus his, king of the Lydians, to fish for frogs: vain
pleasures God wot, and far from right contentation.

Philotimus bespake. O Archaretos, no durance of danger can affright me. For
her sake (whom from henceforward I will not once remember) I ^urst undertake
Orestes his voyage, that follothe Nymphs into Hell: or Hercules his
enterprise, that for his friends cause brake the gates of Hell, and bound the
Giant Aetna, and the triple headed dog Cerberus, and entered combat with the
triple-bodied Pluto. But now farewell dear Country, adieu gallant courtiers,
farewell sweet Aurelia for evermore. I will accomplish thy pleasure
Archaretos, and seek an harbor for my self in some foreign nation, where I
shall never once hear of these, ah forefined miseries.

From 5 years to 5. the Samnits did solemnize their Lustra, from 4. to 4. the
Graekes did celebrate their Olympiades, from 7. to 7. ye Aegiptians did renew
the Temple of Iris: from ten to ten the Romans visited their God Apollo at
Delphos: but I am for ever abandoned my country, whose joys these eyes shall
never renew. The tears trickled down Archaretos his cheeks, and Philotimus
his speech was interrupted with extremity of grief. At length Archaretos
recomforted him, hasting him forward to his journey for fear of preuentions,
he appointed him well with gold and jewels, such as might well pay his
charges till time of better provision.

Alas good Philotimus, (said Archaretos) these are not thy garnished
garments, thy cloth of tinsell and gold, thy braceletts and chains, and thy
new fashioned novelties.

Well (quoth Philotimus) though I cannot go with the fashion, yet do I follow
the fashion: for in years of yore, before pride was in prime, when the purse
was rich and the apparel poor, this was the guise. Here they gave each other
the Bazelos manus, whose dolorous departure (Gentlemen) pardon me though I
describe it not, because I want an heart to attempt it, and words to utter it.
Yet I will not forget to recounte you certain verses, which Philotimus writ
immediately before his voyage, and Archaretos afterward found in the chamber
where they communed, and these they were.

Might mournful wailing end my days,
or pinching careful woe surcease:
Then hope might have his wished death,
or life enjoy his wonted ease.
But wealth is wast, and kin unkind,
all luckles haps deny my joy,
So direful grief must ever last,
and lingering life augment annoy.
In pleasant May-moone of mine age,
I mean the lusty gallant prime,
Where golden pleasure bears the sway,
and youthful sportes do pass the time:
Even then alas poor wretched wight,
my gladsome mirth was heavy moan,
My new sprung Rose did scarcely bud,
where straightway blasting all was gone.
Yet mauger frowning fortunes spite,
my swetest (l) is ever one,
Not near by birth but dear by love,
and sure more faithful never none:
His will is still as erst it was,
no froward chance can change his choice,
In lieu where of fame sound his praise,
with most triumphant joyful voice.
Philotimus, took his journey toward the borders of Graece, inquiring warily
as he went, what were the affairs of every Island, and what were the
conditions and enterteinment of the segniors of those territories, meaning, as
occasion should serve, to employ himself in some convenient service. I need
not recite to you the pensiuenes of this pilgrimage, when as he truly thought
that whatsoever was about him, was a circumference of danger, and he himself
a center of calamity. But this know, that he was afflicted with those
casualties which commonly are wont to befall to all unfortunate strangers, when
they travail in foreign nations without any protection: that is, to be spoiled
of his money, to be infected with diseases, and tossed from post to pillar,
like an esp^al or runnagate. When he was robbed of that little substance which
he had, destitute of friends and acquaintance, ashamed te beg, and detesting
to steal, he was urged to relieve his greedy hunger with the wild fruits of
the wilderness, and to take up his lodging in some secret cave or vault, or
amid some shrowding shrubs, or under some tree.

As Heliodorus Aethiopicus reports, that in Aegipt a little from the river of
Nylus, there inhabited certain heardmen, which expelled all the right owners
of the soil, and lived themselves with rauine and theuedome: so Philotimus was
now hit into a place, which a crewe of like companions had long haunted,
usurping those quarters sole to themselves. The gardeine and Roy of these
fellows, was one Toxilus, who with his lieges having one night raunged abroad
for their pray, at their returnement to cabin in a cave which siely Philotimus
had fore-possessed.

Toxilus entering the cave, and finding him lie groaning, beating his breast,
with sobs and throws, and dismayde almost amazde at this sight started aback,
and churlishly asked him what wight he was.

Philotimus answered: a wight scarce worthy naming, whose woes can none
express, and at Toxilus his importunity disclosed himself. The thief
thought this a fit occasion to increase their crewe with so meet a copes
mate, and hereupon unfolded the whole pack of his conspiratoures and their
intentes, with these persuasions to entice him.

The prince of Syracusa sacked the Temple of Proserpina at Locris, and spoiling
her treasure made a mockage at his theft. Thyamis a noble man's son defeated
of his priesthood, became a captain of theuish heardme^, such good fellows as
we: Paris was an arraunt thief both of women and of wealth: It was the law of
Arms among the Ethiopians, and whether it be there yet, or else where at this
day, I know not, that the soldiour might take all, or whatsoever it pleased
him, of that he found about his prisoner whom he caught: The Persian king did
never go abroad with open face, but with some lawne or silkin scarf, much
like this vale of mine: Aegeon spronge of Gods their race, does scour the
Seas, and souls the traffic of the trading merchauntes: Haste thou not
heard this precepte of Lycurgus, where the Lions skin will not serve, sow the
Foxes to it, that is to interpret it, where honest force will not avail, use
fraud and falsehood, and set conscience to sale. We are honest enough my
friend, as far as our wealth will serve, and are no worse then they that
borrowe money, but that we give no obligations when to pay it again, what
say thou man?

Philotimus looking wistly on him, with a deep sigh answered.

I cannot testify in words, how much I detest thy ways, nor verefy that for
good, which thou inuertest for guile: each queachye grove, each craggie cliffe
with sedge and lofty oysiers, and this same sprinting spring that's courbo
about with pebble stones, inueighs against your fact.

Hoorde in mind that story, and store well that horror, which Jupiter at his
procuration, and secret visitation of the earth, extended upon the ingluuious
rauenour Lycaon.

When Jove came down from heaven, and had entered the harbourles house of late
the Archadian king, such time as twilighte on the earth began to bring dim
darkness, he gave a sign that God was commen, whereat Lycaon made a sport, and
scoffingly said, he meant by open proof to see what wight of weight he was.

Besides he cut the throat of one that lay in hostage with him, which was an
Epyrote, whereof he part did roast, and part did slew, which when it came to
board, forthwith with dire reue^ging flame Jove overthrew the house upon the
ownees head: who seeing this, appauld with fear, slipped out of doors into the
wild and desert wooddes, where all alone endeavoring (but in vain) to make his
moan, he fell a howling, wherewithal for dery rage he ran out of his wits,
still setting blood abroch, and practicing his wonted lust of slaughter on
poor and lielie cattaile.

His garments turnde to shaggye hair, his arms to rugged paws, his skin is
hoarie gray, his shape turned to a wolves, his looks still grim with glaring
eyes, his cruel heart still shows it self in outward shape.

Thus was one house destroyed quite.

Nor he alone shall be condemned by rigorous doom, but for such like, did
Jove (as Naso says) summon all the Gods to his court of parliament, which
then he kept in princely palace amid the Heavens: A Palace gorgeous in
apparaunt sight, on stately pillars builded high of yellow burnisht gold,
beset with sparkling carcbuncles: ye roof was framde of yuory, ye silver
twisolde doo^es did cast a radiant light: And there a perfect plat of all the
world did Vulcan draw, of surges that embrace the earth with winding waves and
of the surefast centrie ground, and of ye wheeling heaven it self: there firye
foming Jove in purple robes and roial throne of Emerauldes fresh and grene did
sit, the Chrisolites and gems whereof did give a sheere and shining light.

See thou not yen milk white pathe that cross the welkin wendes? (we
commonly call it Galactaea) yen is the street that to the Court of whom I
speak, does lead. Even yonder thundering Jove remains, and yonder did he
call his Court, and yonder did he swear by Stix, leaning upon his three-tind
mace (when dreadfully he shook his bushie locks, wherewith he made both Sea
and land, and Heaven it self to quake) that all the ympes of such like
spiteful Carnes, as this Lycaon was, that francke their flesh on that they
filch, and gulpe up blood for bear, should smart of Lycaons whip, or worse
perhaps: For Lycaon you know, was coosin german to high Jove. O what a
wickedness it is to cram the maw with maw of man (as Cyclopes wont to do.)

I take you for no Christians, and therefore I use to Ethnicks the authority
of Ethnickes, which notwithstanding were not so irreligious as you, to make
honesty arbitrary, and in their choice whether they should use it or no. Libeus
was honored of the Sycilians, Ceres of the Rhods, Diana of the Ephesians,
Belus of y^ Palestines, Delphos of the Argiues, Juno of the Numydians, Venus of
the Thebanes, and Berecinthia of the Thebans: Which Gods were so devoutly
worshipped of their country nations both with daily oratories and rich
offeringes, that all men had this Plerophotia^ and full persuasion, that each
word from their God was deemed an oracle, and every offense to their God, a
corsiue to their conscience.

Those that deal with fortune, must entreat her, not force her, not get
wealth by stealth, for that will not prosper, but labor honestly for it, for so
it will thrive. The Augusts are said to begin in Caius, and the Caesar's to end
in Nero: which gives us to understand, that of virtue ensues all good things
behoofull, and by vice do decay all former prosperities.

Ah sirrah quoth Toxilus, now I remember the saying of the pleasant Philosopher
Mimns, Qui cum lasso samilico loquitur r^xa^ quarit, He that helps a beggar
out of the ditch shall be stung with his lyce. But I perceive a wrangling tongue
is the best language thou have. Yet Toxilus and his company aggrizd at these
words, neither able to reply against them, nor willing to apply them to their
amending, left him as they found him: what became of the^, I neither am very
certain, neither need you to doubt knowing their demeanor.

Philotimus thanked God, that he had delivered him from those temptations, and
prayed to his mercy, over to safeconduct him from such suggestio^s. But as
decayed Vear renues her vert grene leaves, and as the naked Serpent recouers
his scaly finnes, and every wasted thing does wear to former state: so
Philotimus began to make reiteration of his sorrows, never having respite but
for a greater spite ensuing, nor ceasing to sighe but for increasing his sobs.
When Io was transmute of Jove into an Hefars form (to bleare Jove's spouses
eyes, yt she might deem, no dame he had defloured, but only that a beast
was pasturing in the field (she rued this bestiall change of hers with
dankishe fear, and by and by assaying to complain, she lowed out, which did
her so affray, that often she started at her noise: unto her father Inachs banks
she did repair apace, where often the lithie Nymph had played her iocundary
reakes: now when she looked in the stream and saw her horned head, she was
aghast and from herself would all in have have fled, and when she thought to
lift her hands unto her head for help, she saw she had no hands at all: in
stead of costlye couch and dear bought down, she satte a nights upon the
ground, and on such grounds, whereas sometime was not so much as grass, and
oftentimes she was compelled to drink on muddie oasye pits, and for her food
on croppes of trees and bitter weeds to brouze: even such, and much alike was
the life of Philotimus. His sound reboundes into the wood, and thus he mournes.

Ye greedy gripes, forbear to tire on Titius growing-hearte: O Tantal, that
indeuorest to drink the shuning water, and Danaus daughters, that seek to
fill your tubs that have no brinkes, and Ixion, that do draw in that
incessa^t wheel, and Sisyphus with thy rolling restles stone, wail ye no
more, work ye no more, your taskes and sorrows every each one are fallen unto
my lot. O God, alas.

O Maiaes son, Cyllemus sweet, and nephewe unto Atlas, who with thy charming
rod and pipe, bringst things asleep, and fetchest souls from Hell, assuage
my woes, assist my brain with rest, and drive away those terrors wherewith my
mind is tortured. And as the drinkers of the River Salmacis, or of the lake of
Aethiop, do quite forgo their memory: or as Melampus Amithaons son,
delivered king Praetus his daughters by his charms and herbs, from being
mad: so thou vouchsafe this gracious boon, and yield no such recourse of
thoughtfull passed state, (unto my woes a nourishment) but let it now surcease
and end, and woes decay with famishment.

In Pallene there is a people, the which by diuinge thrice three times in
Triton lake, become all fethered, and take upon them the shape of birds.
Fair Phaetuse and Lampetie the daughters of the Sun and Clymen, bewraying salt
brine tears, and still be wayling brothers death, were ouergrowen with slender
bark, and turned to trees (of which said trees in guard of their goodwill and
moan, flow gummie tears that Amber men do call, which hardened with the
Sun, and falling from the boughs into the brook, do serueas things of
price to deck our Ladies wrests, and wear about their necks) so rather then
I spend my days in spitefusl dole, convert me good Mercury, to some Tree or
stone, that may do the service, or Aurelia some use.

Now brighte Hyperion was in mids of sky, and searde the field with fiery
rays, so that for very languishing heat Philotimus was constreynd to seek a
shade.

Not very far of, there was a valley thick with Pineapple and Cipresse trees,
that armed were with pricks, and in the furthest end thereof, there was a
pleasant boure vaulted with the leauy trees: by nature it was wal^ with flint
and pommy half about, and on the right side of the same a liuelye spring
with crystal stream full freshly flowed out, whereof the upper brimme was
grene with grass and matted herbs.

Here did Philotimus, that swet and swelted almost, rest himself to refreshe
his weakened limbs, and here he began afreshe to repeat his misery, which he
could never repel from his recording memory.

Into this valley had an old Shepherd (whose name was Laurus) forced with
heat, brought his flock, and he sitttinge under a pineapple tree, was
reading on an old motheaten book.

The shepherd hearing a lamentable sound, traced the voice till he had
started Philotimus, whom he found sitting under a Cipresse tree, leaning on his
left hand, sometimes beatinge his heels against the ground, sometimes
knocking his head against the Tree, sometime heauing his displaied hands to
heaven, calling for mercy and revenge, the one to pacify his mood, the other
a meede for his enemies cruelty. When his rage was assuaged, and he had fed
his fill upon this estasie, Laurus something more emboldened then before,
giving him a tap on his shoulder with his sheepehooke, saluted him after this
manner.

My Son, God speed thy study, and breed thy quiet, augment thy patience,
or lessen thy penance: and if thy grief may be told, and my rudenes
accepted, bewray it to me, I will betray thee to none: credit me a stranger
(good yongman) I will be as true to thee as the beggar to his dishe. What
haste thou trespassed against thy self, thus to tormente thee without cause or
reason? Declare (good Son) and doubt not: conference is conuenable to
subdewe direful melancholy, and difference of talk makes diversity in
thought. A slender poale can supporte a large vine, and my sleighte ability
may assiste thy debilitie. Open to me thy state, and hope to be bestad. A
shepherds scrippe is stuffed erewhile with better fare then bacon: a graish
weed does veil sometimes a gayer wit then velvet: a gray goose wear
becoms a wearied mind, but does not prove a goosish sot benumbed man: an
hoarie head does hoorde both sores and salues for every crased mind. Now of
fellowship say, and unfold thy dismal dristresses as we sit in this cooly
shade.

Philotimus seeing his friendly talk, countnaunced by a reverent aspect of
gravity, saluted him after this form.

Hail worthy shepherd what ere thou art, of greater worth then Laure I
think, if Laure himself did hear. But since you are importune to hear my
importable destinies, I will neither be captious to misconstrue your good
meaning, neither ungrateful to deny a kind demand, yt one an argument of a
peuish mind, the other a consequent of a perverse judgment: then my case
stands thus.

If pleasures be in painfulnes, if joys accord with carefulnes, if mirth may
be in misery, if banishment be liberty, then am I most pleasant, most
ioifull, most merry, most free: but ay lady mercy, I am quite the contrary.

I am a captive clapped in chains of care, lapt in the laws of lethall love,
and as the dog all only for the taste does gnawe the bone: so forth I draw
this irked life with fancies vain repaste. My corsiues comfort is but this,
that as a siedged forte with foreign force, for want of aid must yield at
last: so this my corpse thus courst with cares, for want of ease shall quickly
fade.

I have friends (or had friends) whom if good nature had taught to go upright,
when they learned to hault of wayward will, I had not been a president of
mischief, nor needed present counsel, I had been better advised, then now to
need advertisement, my winged desire had not been clogged with despair,
neither had I had this clue of care to work my warpe upon.

My fame had not failed, nor my fortune quayled, nor this bloom of my blame,
bred of the seed of their shame. My Courtiers steed had not been turned to a
Colliers cut, nor passed wealth to present want: I had not been expelled from
mine own country, nor here exposed to the wrecke of fortune.

Not to make a profound parable of a plain brobleme, nor to be nyce in
concealing that, which all the world does know, I will disclose some part of my
follies, which have been the close procurers of my fall.

I loved a Less (alas why did I so?) which made full many a swearing
promise, that I should be the platform, where she would plant her
gooodwill, and the only ground where she would graft her grace. But as I was
a sottish Ass to believe a faithles trull: so she made me an horned Ox to
harnish my love.

Why (quoth the shepherd, meaning to drive him out of his dumpes) it is a
point of a good gardener to plant in the highest ground, and therefore did
she choose thy head to graft her slips in.

Good shepherd (quoth Philotimus) be not too gamesome in so heavy a case: for
play is turned to earnest, where game makes loss, and these merry conceits
make but choleric melancholy. marmalet is no meat for mowers, nor pleasure a
pastime for distressed men: if thou list to hear my state, listen a while to
the tenure of my talk.

I was once a pleasant moate in my mistrisses eye, whiles I had a beam in mlne
own not to discern her dealinges: she the^ had a golden tongue (as Berosius
had for his eloquence) when my purse was so proud, that the lyning was silver:
I then see her lovely when my candle burnt bright, and now stink in her
nosthrils like loathly snuff, when my light is extinct. When my wealth was
first broched, I was sweet to her taste, but since it came to the dregs, she
shuns me like poison. When the honey dew of gold did drop upon her fingers,
and fortune flowed with full stream, then was I worthy to be master of her
barge, but ah now. Yet I wish her long prosperity, and my present death.

Truly (quoth the shepherd) and for thy sake (though I have as little
acquaintance with thee, as thou haste credit with her) I wish with all my
heart, she were with Charles the fourth Emperor of that name. Yet I request
not thy death before his time: for I hope thou shall eat of the goose that
shall tread on her grave. But I perceive thou art no good Grammarian, that
prefer est ye feminine gender to the masculine, and thy mistrisses love before
thine own safety. Me thinks her goodwill that will not be warmed at any
fire, should quickly aslake thy feruente affection, and since I could not
support her lack, I would learn of reason to suppress my love.

Shepherd, said Philotimus, in this she is qualifyed like Satyrus, who
embracing fire was not scortched, neither can my smoldering heat enkindle any
warmth in her.

Laurus replied.

Thou see (my son) she is a star that will not come within the compass of
thy Sphere, and therefore beat not thy brains about this bootles Astronomy.
The sweet tree Ebenus is good and pliable in his proper nature, but being cut
or hewn, turns to a stone. When her teeth does loose the knot yt her tongue
tied, thou art enlarged and at liberty, as thou were before this bond.
Naytheles, I would not have thee misconstrue my words, for it is not my purpose
to miscontent thy mind: and indeed I censure of thee, ye honor should rather
eternish thee for thy martial hardines, then that thou should do her homage
for her pranked haughtines, and I do rather persuade me, that thou deseruest a
better the^ she, then I can brook to hear thee commend such a retrograde
dissembler. These sighinges in her absence can small deal profit thee, whose
instance in her presence could not prevail. Therefore tender thy state and
forget her.

Philotimus looking upon him, neither backward nor forward, but like ye picture
of the sielie man Dulius, pitifully God knows, and very wistly, sighed out
these words.

O father shepherd, whiles thou endeuorest to staunch a vein in my liner,
thou let me blood in the very heart: thou cuttest the grass, whereof I
meant to make my hay, which though it now be a common for others to graze in,
yet I love it the better, because it might have been my frehold. She is ye
brau^ch, the root, and the tree, she is my head, my body, and my soul, the
first A. of my crosrow, and the last letter of my learning, without her I am
not, she is all in all, and all in every part. O Aurelia, dear Aurelia, What
(shepherd) do thou think my mind a roll of ware to receive the
impression of inhumaine disloialtie?

I know Bucephalus cannot be bridled without Alexhnders consent, nor the
Crocodile, tied to a Tree without Caesar's enforcement, nor Aurelia be co^yed by
any persuasions but her Cornelius his: Yet if I were able, I had rather give
thee a wedge of Gold to make a lock for thy lips, then thy tongue should be a
pickelocke to open the least part of her evil dealings, whose wished welfare is
sacred to me, and whose subtle practices shall be secret to thee.

If I had Gyges his faculty, to go unseen into my country, it were enough
for me but to view her.

I confess that by her means I am a scorn to the best, and a scum of the
worst, I expect no good by her, but suspect a propagation of my misfortunes:
yet now to forsake her after my long love, were to buy fish and give it to
the dogs, and to take thy barren counsel in these affairs, were as if a
calfe should suck a bull. O shepherd, much water passes besides the mill
that the milner sees not, and much has happened within the compass of thy
experience, whereof thou tookest little keep. I will commend the for a cunning
shepherd when I know thy skill, and will thank thee for thy courtesy, but not
take thee for a counsalour.

The good shepherd quietly answered. My good child, because I do see thee so
incapable of good counsel, in stead of a Rose to yield a sweet savor, I
was a thorn in thy nose to stir the up to sense, I spoke sharply, but meant
fatherly. Be yet of good cheer (my son) behind the door stands the wall,
and Time holds the glass of thy good fortune. But in the mean season, I do
so much the more lament thy casualties, for that thou and I have been birds of
one feather, paid tribute to one Caesar, and been fellow soldiers in the
same legar: so that he which has my misaduentures, and is enthralled with thy
present state, may vie the pair for sorrow, whatsoever the stake be.

Notwithstanding, though thou whose state was once extolled, art now
controuled of checkmate spite, and diuested from thy throne, despair not tho
as a miscreaunt, nor destruste thy possibilitye. Often have I seen as unlucky
a Burrowe harbor a Connye: Often have I known, as unskillful as great
humility, as I unadvisedly solicit my suite. So it is my good servant, that
my provident care to preserve my state, and thy sober discretion in all thy
dealings, induce me to thank thee for thy trusty policy, and to crave thee
for my phear and lawful husband. Yet I beseech thee impute not this to lust
(for such perturbations are placed in my preterpect tense, but refer it to the
virtue of thy good demeanor, which has a force attractiue, to draw all
hearts to it. What say thou man? If my breach of manners have staind my art
of wooing, then be thou a mannerly maid for me, and say nay and take it.

I answered her in these or the like words.

My good mistress, your meritorious charity has so long relieved me, and your
present courtesies offer me such kindness, that for ye one you may challeng me
to be your vassal, and with the other you have stricke me into a maze.
Wherefore I ask forgiveness on my knees, if I do not accomplish your reasonable
command, and whiles I live I will punish my untowardly mind, that is not
submissiue to your good meaning.

Yet this I will tell you, which perhaps you have heard: when the Moon which
is ye lowest planet, is in conjunction with Saturn which is the highest
planet, there can no good nativity issue from them: when Winter marries
Summer, write it up for a wonder, and when an old grift in a young plant
fructifies good fruit, ye year will pippins be preservative from ye plague:
The greenenes of my youth, and the gravity of your years, are discordes in
descante, and therefore can they never make good music. I am meeter for your
servant then your sovereign, and you fitter form y mistress then to be my
fellow. If I were pha^tasticall (good mistress) your clemency and good nature
could withdraw love from other obiectes, and allure it to your self: but I
have vowed singlenes and will be true votarie, for what cause, God and my
conscience best knows.

At this time we brake of, she telling me, that she doubted not but I would be
better advised, and I aunswering, that I wished I could satisfy her
expectation. Many colloquies had we about these matters after this, but her
earnest instaunce wrought but my resistau^ce, and my waiwardnes, her goodwill.
At length with much ado, and many entreatings, I got leave to be manumitted of
her service with her good liking.

Her reward at my departure was very bountiful, and though my ability were
too slender to make requital, yet was my grateful heart not unwilling to be
thankful..

Then in the progreffe of my travail, my meditation was, what course of life
to take. And since my lignage was obscure, and my parentage but base, I would
needs have enterprised some exploite, with the atchieuement of which, I might
have been to my posterity an original of true gentilitie. Amongst the rest,
the soldiers profession I most honored, and though I had been trained up in
learning, and never seen a lined camp, yet did I think that my reading with
a little experience, would afford me such knowledge as would suffice a
soldiour. Machaon and Podali^e the sons of Coronis, two excellent doctors in
physic, were Coronels of the Tricenses, Ithomenienses, and Occaliens, that
came to Troy with thrice ten sail. Could not I then for a need, marshall the
battaylons and phalanges of footmen, and raunge the soldiers with good
disposition? place the chariots in ye front, the choice footmen in the
rereward, and the weak ones in the midst? O brave, when bloody trumpe to
battle soundes, and lusty neighing horse bestirres him on the ground.

But when I looked back to see my self and the feebleness of my sielye
carcass, alas poor man (quoth I) thou art more like to fight with a Crane,
as Homer in the third of his Iliades says that the Pygmyes do, then attain
by thy valor to any notable conquest: surcease from this devise I wish. Nay,
Diana in her trayninge chase delights, Minerua in her chattering arms does
march, Apollo in his siluer-sounding cords disportes, yet none of these are my
content. The fragrant fields, the rural lawnes, where Zephyrus inspires the
fruitful earth, and does attire each bushe with budde and bloomie branch,
where the purple Colum^ine, and the orient cowsloppe, the daffadillie and the
pretty lilly, the dazie and the violet, do may in array, shall be my palace and
my paradise.

After all my consultations had been accomptant to my reason, this was the
foot of my determination. I met with an old ancient shepherd, with whose
tranquillity of life (being seiunged from all human troubles) I was much
delighted. Him I endeavored to serve, and after his decease, the small flock
which he kept in his life, was mine by his bequest after his decease. The
residue of my life have I led ever since in this vocation, where at my
pleasure I could study good authors, and be contemplative when I list, and my
mansion house is yonder little cottage, which thou see stand amid the trees.
My name as thou rightly gessedst at the first, is Laurus. Now, if thou can
either brook my kind of life, or accept the needines of my cheer, I will
either make thee a companion in my calling as long as I live, or give thee
whiles thou listest, such entertainment as I can.

Philotimus his humor was frankly fed with this discourse of the shepherd.
Laurus (quoth he) I gratulate thy presence, and am glad to see thee, much have
I heard of thee which now I will not repeat, and I think my self happy that
I have met with thee But as for thy life, I rather admire it then mean to
imitate it. For a candle put under a bushel, is light in vain, and a tale^t
hid, is to small purpose. If thou can give me knowledge of any Court nere at
hand, or any nobleman that will vouchsafe me his service, I shall think my
self much beholden to thee. Truly quoth the shepherd, in good time: for not
many leagues from hence, does soiourne a famous and virtuous Gentleman, the
prince of this province: whose peaceable policy and divine government, I
cannot rightly match, but with that most renowned regiment of the worthy
Queen ELIZABETH, now Queen of England, whose royal excellency,

Ah Laurus (quoth Philotimus) there hold, least in talking of Elizabeth's
majesty, thou be overwhelmed with her glory.

Her virtues must be adored with sincere contemplation, but cannot be
recensed without abridging of her praises. If thou should magnify her
highness with the eloquence of Tully, and every of these hills, wherewith thou
art environed, should Echo forth ten thousand voices, thy words would be
wasted in ye prologue of her praises, and the Echoes die for joy to hear her
name expressed. There is no vacum of her fame in rerum natura, all the world
ringes of it.

Well then (quoth Laurus) although I am not imbued with an autorchye of
faculty, to praise her as I would: yet do I not want a true lieges loyalty,
to pray for her majesty with an interminate devotion. And since the prince of
whom I speak, is in the Positive degree of her Superlatiue, thou shall not
need to doubt of his wisdom in deciphering thy good qualities, nor of his
clemency to vouchsafe the service.

To morrow next there is sollem hunting in the park here adjoining, at which
sport the prince will be present: and to morrow the Moon is in conjunction with
Jupiter, at which time I have red, yt it is prosperous to solicit any suite to
a nobleman. There by mine advise thou shall exhibit thy complaint in some pretty
piteous premeditated oration.

O (quoth Philotimus) shall I an uncouth stranger, so abruptly present my
self to a mighty prince? so I should rather seem to checkmate modesty, then
challenge meede.

Tush dastard (replied the shepherd) auaunt despair: crave in hope, and have
in ha^, often have thou heard it, and now shall thou try it, that fortune most
usually favors the venterous.

That night Philotimus lodged with Laurus, and the morning following all yelad
in grene, semblable to his griefs which were ever fresh and grene, he paced
forward to the park, where after a delicate banquet, whereto the prince was
invited in a coolye pauished arbor, after the game, Philotimus in due time
prostituting himself before his feet, and making as though he had some
straung news to utter, thereby to move his attention, delivered this speech
ensuing

If I were as shamfast (renowned prince) as modesty persuades my young grene
years, or if your honor did strike such an horror to my fainting heart, as
is wont to be common in humble suites, and partly thought commendable in such
abiected creatures as my sielie self, where now I feel the scant of skill to
bewray my wailfull case, and thereunto to move your merciful remorse, it would
also force a want of will, amyd your weighty affairs to use this careless
babbling, (whereof God knows you can but make full course account) to take wary
heed, and not to enterprise by rusticke speech, so foolish hardye bold
attempt, to awake my wits and lull my tongue asleep.

But alas (my Lord) sharp misery learnes bold policy, better solicit a
king, then always live a beggar. The cowardlye champion hembd in with listes
does often conquer, he, on whom poverty has so incroched, that scarce he can
move him out of his standing, but pinching need does catch him by the back,
must of necessity strain courtesy with modesty.

Yet I see my ambition above my condition, and a Pharisees desire pass a
Publicanes desert, and can it prevail with haulting heel to strive to hoppe
against the hill? Can the sluggish snayle with creeping pace ever reach the
Castles tower? Will the lusty Ship with top and top gallant, all fleeting away
with flanta galanta, veil bonnet to the boat so far underneath her, so tossed
through her weakness with every tempest? O happy I if this may be: and it
pleases sweet Hope to send forth an Echo and tell me It may be. The
mounting kite does sometimes seize on homely pray, Phoebus for all his
heavenly throne vouch safes to lend his comfortable light, as well to the
valleys as to the mountains. Then to my matter. And because my colors shall
not fade or soil, I will painte out my self with the oil of Truth.

I am a Gentleman borne in Italy, descended from the lignage of a noble house:
in my infancy and primetide of my youth, I was addicted to good letters, and
trained up in Florence in the study of the sciences: and after that by my
parents decease I was invested in their inheritance, I followed the Court,
and did attend upon the Dukes grace of Venice.

And Hinc illae lachrimae, from thence came these miseries. For there, not
being urged with any impositions of the Duke, but inclined to prodigallitie of
mine own wastfull nature, I made dilapidations of all my parents revenues,
and quite consumed all my possessions: then being ashamed in my needy
beggerie, to stay any longer in my country, and partly afraied of my greedy
creditors, least they should extend the rigor of their extremity, I
abandoned my native soil, seeking good fortune where I could find her: and as
Cadmus directed this journey that way toward which his staff fell: so I made
my voyage thither, whither it pleased God and destiny to guide me. Now (most
puissant Prince) if my secret instincte of divination do not deceive me. I
hope under your favorable protection, here to house my self in a safe
harbor. Surmise not I most humbly beseech your majesty, that I come as a
sentinall within your dominion, neither suspect me of any treachery, that may
be prejudice to your personage. For if by this fair pretense I intend any
other thing then to do your highness most duetifull service, and partlye to
renew my decayed state by your royal bounty, then I wish and pray, that
presently in your presence, the Gods most miraculously work my confusion. Not
much I owe to many, and little more I owe to some, but most of all, and all
it self, shall I owe to your highness, if you vouchsafe me your most regal
service. I am not harnished with a complete faculty in all mysteries, to execute
any function with facility, but (be it spoken without offense to any of your
subjects) imploye me at your pleasure abroad or at home, and if my daily
diligence be inferior to any man's, (although my might be much like hers whose
wealth was but a mite) then let me loose my due reward, and dye the death of a
villainous traitor. Thus loath any longer to trouble your highness with my
bablatiue eloquence^, and to keep my self in suspense of your wished
answer, I surcease my prating, praying God to preserve you, and expecting the
resolution of your majesty's determination.

The noble Prince seemed to take delight in his speech, which God knows was
framed with small study, and taking him by the hand made him stand up,
speaking as follows.

We see (my friend) that in the shallowest water is hid

Per. I have also red which thou addest not, that if they see not within seven
days after, they are blind for ever: and thou know how long I have borne my
crosses: yet I profess to thee Pandolpho, that I was never better comforted
with a cullisse in my sickness, then my mind was cheared with this story of
Philotimus. And therefore I beseech thee, commend me hartelie to the sweet
self of good Melanthus, and tell him, that if I might enjoy his company
together with thine, I should be restored to half my former happiness.


Pand. I will (good Periander) and now that I know thy mind, we will not long
be absent from thee. In the mean time good night my sweet friend, and as thou
can comfort thy self, and I will pray for both our prosperities.


Per. Ten thousand good nights my good Pandolpho, and so many thanks for this
present courtesy, and so many wishes for thy next company.


FINIS.
AT LONDON.

Imprinted by Roger Ward, dwelling nere Holburne Conduit at the sign of the
Talbot, and are there to be sold. 1583.
